The Red Page
Amundsen
ith the North Pole found one-and-a-
quarter times—giving Cook the benefit of the
ti action and the South Pole found twice,
bj Amundsen and, as we shall soon hear, by
Scott, the world will heave a typhoon of re-
lief, and get on with its work. Nobody else
can discover either Pole, though doubtless
' urious assorted maniacs will discover new
means of getting to the top storey and the
basement of the globe by motor-bike, aero-
plane, perambulator or ice-yacht, or by
waUdng backwards all the way, or holding
hill-climbing competitions in sprinting up
glaciers. A\ ith The South Pole, by Roald
Amundsen (two vols.; Murray, per Dymock’s
Library), and Scott’s future work, the big
book of Polar exploration may be consid-
ered closed.
Ihe South Pole is the stirring record of
a party of white men who put up no bluff
about scientific research” or “examination
ot meteorological conditions,” as is usual
in British expeditions out to bag the Pole
but went straight there and got back in
record time. It is true that Amundsen con-
cealed his intention of pocketing the South
hole till the last possible minute; it is true
that he gave out to the world that he was
aftei the North Pole, and informed Scott
on P at the same time as he informed his
crew of his change of plans. Exception can
be taken to this secrecy, but it is difficult to
see how Scott was handicapped by the pre-
sence of the Norwegian; and certainly Scott
made no change in his own plans on learning
of the start of his rival. And that rival
made no use of Scott’s and Shackleton’s pre-
vious explorations, leaving to Scott the route
lie and ; Shackleton had thoroughly explored
and striking out through unknown territory’
Indeed, Amundsen profited by Shackleton’s
mistakes, and staked his own judgment
against that of his predecessor as to the
deductions to be drawn from certain of
Shackleton’s observations.
Amundsen was a strategist, who had no
scruples about his rather “slim” action in
getting ahead of the franker, slower Scott:
Nor did I feel any great scruples with re-
gard to the other Antarctic expeditions that
were being planned at the time. I knew I
should be able to inform Captain Scott of
the. extension of my plans before he left
civilisation, and therefore a few months
sooner or later could be of no great import-
ance. Scott s plan and equipment were so
W 1 j j dilierent from my own that I re-
garded the telegram that I sent him later,
with the information that we were bound
tor the Antarctic regions, rather as a mark
ot courtesy than as a communication which
might cause him to alter his programme in
the slightest degree. The British expedition
was designed entirely for scientific research.
Ihe 1 ole was only a side-issue, whereas in
my extended plan it was the main object.
. . . .If at that juncture I had made my in-
tention public, it would only have given
occasion for a lot of newspaper discussion,
and possibly have ended in the project being
stifled at its birth. B
In Amundsen’s judgment, Shackleton
missed discovering the Pole by a hasty con-
clusion that the Barrier was afloat at the
Bay of Whales
*°, r wt L ftn Shackleton passed the Bay of
Whales in January, 1908, and saw the ice
in process of breaking up and
dritiing out, he had waited a few hours, or
the most a couple of days, the problem of
the Soiith Pole would probably have been
solved long before December, 1911. With
his keen sight and sound judgment, it would
not have taken him long to determine that
the inner part of the bav does not consist of
floating barrier, but that the Barrier there
rests upon a good, solid foundation, probably
in the form of small islands, skerries, or
shoals, and from this point he and his able
companions would have disposed of the
bouth Polar question once for all.
In this fine book Amundsen reveals him-
self as .a master of a lucid, straightforward
style, gifted with a sense of humor, and pos-
sessed of a genial, capable personality. He
got to the Pole by the expert knowledge and
practical acquaintance of Arctic conditions
inherent in Norwegians, by the expertness of
his party upon ski. by the intuitive know-
ledge. his party had of the best methods of
working and harnessing Esquimaux dogs,
and, above all, by a care in preparation of
material and methods that closelv ap-
proaches the well-known definition of genius.
Thus lig pinned his faith to dogs, as opposed
to the English explorers’ dependence on
Ponies. One important reason Was “that
dog can eat dog.” The methodical way in
which he slaughtered his dogs as he reached
certain predetermined latitudes will doubt-
less be objected to by the ladies who prefer
pet dogs to babies ; but the system of reduc-
ing his party by disposing of the dogs seems
to be better than that of sending back at
intervals supporting parties of men. And
Amundsen took his whole party to the Pole,
and insisted on every member helping to
unfurl the Norwegian flag on that desolate
plain. Peary, it will be remembered, re-
served that honor for a nigger and himself.
. The journey across the Equator in
his tiny ship, the Fram, every inch of the
deck of which was devoted to his
hundred dogs—l 6 of them had to be accom-
modated on the bridge—is almost as inter-
esting as his sledge journey to the Pole. It
was tile most dangerous section of his trip,
as, without the safe arrival of the dogs!
nothing could have been done. 1
Framheim. the winter quarters of the ex-
pedite at the Bay of Whales, developed
into a wonderful system of underground
dwellings hewn from th.e ice. The liut was
even provided with a system of ventilation.
Everywhere appear evidences of the invent-
iveness, the skill and the pluck of the mem-
bers of the party. They even rigged
up a vapor-bath in one of the un-
derground rooms. The winter was spent in
the most careful preparations for the great
adventure. The new system of marking out
depots by lines of sticks extending across,
and not along, the line of march, contrib-
uted materially to the party’s safe return.
In case the thermometers should be smashed
on the trip, Amundsen made each member
ot the winter-party guess the temperature
every morning, and found, as he expected,
that the average result of these guesses gave
him the mean temperature to within a frac-
tion of accuracy. But they broke no ther-
mometers.
Spring came, and with it the expedition,
always intent on beating Scott, made a false
start. The weather was still too cold. On
the second start the going was at first so
good that Amundsen made a rope fast to one
ot the sledges, and was pulled on his ski 340
mdes toward the Pole! Then, of course,
the difficulties began. His route was
marked by 150 snow beacons, each fur-
nished with indications of the whereabouts
ot the next —an exaggerated system of pre-
cautions, but, in this careful leader’s mind
an absolute necessity for their safe return.
>ut the chief feature of his achievement
was the value of ski to these men “born and
bred with ski on our feet.” The terrible
hardships of the ascent of one glacier, to be
followed by the necessity, for the ascent of
another before the final plateau was
reached, will engross any reader. The
account of the journey equals in narrative
quality and in thrilling interest that of
bhackleton’s return from farthest south—
and that is the highest possible praise.
Every step we now took in advance
foTfi-tr I ' a ' Jldl - v * earer ‘he goal; we could
feel fairly certain of*reaching it on the after-
thatl tbe None of us would admit

malady. YV hat should we see when we got

ad yet seen and no foot yet trodden; or—

And yet and yet....“ What on earth is
Uroa scenting?’ It was Bjaaland who made
us remark, on one of these last days, when
I was going by the side of his sledge. “And
the strange thing is that he’s scenting to the
south. It can never be—” Mylius, Bing and

At three in the afternoon a simultaneous
Halt rang out from the drivers. They
had carefully examined their sledge-meters
and they all showed the full distance—our
hy reckoning 1 cdnnot say—though

the object of my life was attained. I had

that I have never known any man to be

that moment. The regions round the North
y f s ’ the , North Pole itself—had

Sou th Pole. Can anything more
topsy-turvy be imagined?
The return journey is as graphically de-
scribed, and the party arrived at their
winter quarters on the exact day prophe-
sied by Amundsen when working out the
plans of the expedition before its start from
Norway. And Australians will be interested
m the description of the Japanese expedi-
tions methods of work at the Bav of
Whales, the awful state of their ship! the
tortures that the brown men wantonly in-
fl'cjied on anim als, and their general policy
of futile secrecy.

Gissing
The truth about George Gissing is told by
his best friend, Morley Roberts, under the
transparent disguise of The Private Life of
Henry Maitland (Kell, per Dymoek’s
Library). Gissing is dead, leaving a name
in English literature, and his friend comes
forth to picture for us the man as he was,
frankly and yet reverently. The question
whether one literary man can thus botanise
on the grave of his closest friend can be
answered only by the need for the task and
the manner in which it is done. The truth
about Gissing has never yet been told,
though it was widely known that there was
a story to tell; and Morley Roberts in this
book has done his dead friend but bare jus-
tice in setting forth his inner life. From
this narration we come to a closer apprecia-
tion of Gissing’s work, and find a finer sym-
pathy for the man. Morley Roberts has not
used Gissing’s memory for his own ends.
As his biographer aptly says :
Henry Maitland stands on his own feet
and on his living feet. He is not a British
statue done in the best mortuary manner.
Ibere is far too little sincere biography in
Hngiish. We are a mealy-mouthed race,
hypocrites by the grave and the monument.
. . . .Here is life, not a story or a constructed
diary, and the art with which it is done is a
secondary matter....lf we have lessened
him at times from the point of view of a
hireling in biographic praise, we have set
him down life-size all the same.... Here is
the man.
Parenthetically, it may be remarked that
the art with which it is done is slipshod;
but the sincerity of the writer overrides the
carelessness of his style.
&
The story of Gissing’s tragic life thus
frankly set forth is this: While a
college student with a brilliant re-
putation, Gissing became infatuated with
a young prostitute, took her off the
streets, and determined to marry her.
To provide her with money, he stole money
and articles from the lockers of his fellow
students, was discovered, and sent to serve
a short sentence in gaol. On his release, his
friends sent him to the United States. Rob-
erts’s absence in Australia for three years
temporarily severed their acquaintance, but
when Roberts returned to London, he learnt
that Gissing was back, and had married the
girl. While in America Gissing had almost
died of starvation; and on his return to
London his plight, with a drunken wife to
keep, was little better. For Roberts, too,
there followed a period of bitter poverty,
whose only alleviation was the companion-
ship of his friend. And when his wife, after
periodically going back to the streets, re-
turned penitent, Gissing forgave her.
This was the kind of life that Henry Mait-
land, perhaps a great man of letters, lived
tor years. Comfortable people talk of his
pessimism, and his greyness- of outlook, and
never understand. The man reallv was a
hedonist, he loved things beautiful.' He re-
joiced in every form of Art, in books and
in music, and in all the finer inheritance of
P.£ But tfits was the life he lived, and
the lire he seemed to be doomed to live from
the very first.
At last the wife died. The husband’s one
tear was that the telegram announcing the
death might not be true. No sentimental
heroics, no sham grief here.
All the time he was publishing novels, and
gi tting for each but foO. He wrote eight
hours almost every day of his life, and lab-
ored desperately to achieve infinite perfec-
tion in his art. He often destroyed the first
tin id of a book, to begin it all wearily
again ; for he was forced to write with the
prospects of starvation always before him
Roberts rightly comments that Gissing ought
never to have written fiction at all
If he had had £5O a year of his own he
would have retreated into a cottage and
asphyxiated himself with books.
J*
His next venture in marriage was equally
unfortunate. Distracted by the loneliness in
which his shy temperament immured him, he
rushed out one day and spoke to a girl in the
street. Not even a pretty girl, though a
respectable” one. “The first woman,” he
told Roberts, “that I came across.” He
even admitted .that he did not love her—he
did not know what love was. And this girl
became his second wife, “to bear him chil-
dren, to torture him for years, to drive him
almost mad, and once more make a
financial slave of him.” At last he left her
and fled to Italy and Greece.
. Taking no exercise, hating visitors, shut-
ting himself up day after day to his work, Gis-
sing’s health rapidly became undermined.
“He had no business to be writing novels and
spinning things largely out of his subjective
mind, when lie ought to have been dealing
with the objective world, or with books.”
On the Continent, he met a young French
lady of Spanish origin, a woman of high in-
telligence and good education, a beautiful
woman with a wonderful voice, whose hand
Meredith kissed while telling her that she
had beautiful eyes. And, in the end, he
lived with her, with the consent of ’ her
mother. He shrank from divorcing the
almost mad woman his wife had become.
And this third venture almost came to dis-
aster over —of all things—cooking. Gissing
loved fat and oily things, and plenty of
them, and French cooking seemed to him
starvation. All through this curious bio-
graphy we hear of Gissing’s preoccupation
with food. But death intervened. After his
death Roberts was concerned to rebut the
claim made that Gissing had died in the
Church.
So, for the confusion of the writers of
primers of English literature for use in
schools, there is the man, George Gissing.

The Inky Way.
W. J. Geddis, of much poundage in M L. journal-
istic circles, takes over the managing directorship of
the Tistes (Wellington). With his advent, a general
reconstruction happens. Politically, the paper will
keep its color, bu*t the public will be supplied with
the half-buried virtues of the old Seddon-Ward party
in a more forcible manner.
“B. de Burke” : The Rev. Leyton Richards, of Col-
lins-street (Melb.) Independent Church, preached
thus on a recent Sawbath :■ —-
“No greater injustice to the Church is done than
by the fact that her meetings and conferences are
often reported by men who lack sympathy with the
work done. We have a right to say that those who
report these religious gatherings should have this
religious sympathy, without which reporting becomes
a mere parody of the speech.”
And yet in the course of a long experience I’ve
known only two pious reporters. One drank himself
into hopeless disreputability, and now polishes door-
plates; the other eloped with/‘another woman.” But
I cast no stone at them. I keep it for parson
Richards. This was the same rev. who shortly after
his call (billets for parsons are always “calls” ; it is
only godless reporters and suchlike that take jobs)
sought to make a sensation by denouncing the Aus-
tralian Defence scheme, and urging Christians to
demand a conscience clause. llis sermon was a
general invitation to shirk the work of defending
the country—or else leave it to the godless reporter
and such-like. What I want to say to this newly
imported preacher of discontent is that no one should
criticise our Defence scheme if he lacks sympathy
with the work being done.
“S.T.S.”: These publishers speak a strange lan-
guage. Grant Richards advertises, for 18d., “The
Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam (Persian Yapp).” This
seems a gratuitously offensive way of putting it,
but, then, one sees the same bracketed words at-
tached to Herrick and “Everyman.” Evidently a
kind of binding. “Susan (sewed)” is another
attractive offer. She must be the original Girl who
got the Needle.
Michael Fagan, who used to be poet Henry Ken-
dall's friend and boss and patron, died a week or two
ago at Kendall, on the N.S.W. North Coast. The
Fagan brothers were in the timber trade, and brother
Michael gave his poetic friend a job as clerk at a
store, and named the place after him. Later on,
finding that poetry and accounts didn’t get on well
together, he got him another job as forest inspector.
“Side Stick”: Recent references to John Fawkner
recall that he appeared on the Inky Way in
1838, at Melbourne, as the proprietor of the manu-
script Advertiser. Next year the Port Phillip
Gazette and Port Phillip Patriot appeared, both
printed. Then came the Herald (1840), the Argus
(1846), and the Age (1854). The Herald, like the
Argus and Age, was a morning paper, and, like the
others, started out at 3d. a copy ; then it was 6d.,
then 3d. again. The Age, later, reduced its price to
2d., and, not long afterwards, to Id. The Argus,
like the Register (Adelaide), took a long time to
make up its mind that a penny was a fair thing.
They both had, and still h%ve, reason to regret the
delay.
One of the uses of Melbourne Argus nowadays ap-
pears to be to serve as a training school from which
the once-despised evening rags can collect recruits.
Ex-Argus men at present on Melbourne Herald in-
clude the editor-in-chief (Jack Davidson), news
editor (Guy Innes) —Innes’s predecessor, Jack Nish,
was also taken from the old Tory organ—sporting
editor (Frank Hart), commercial editor (Rogers)'
acting-commercial editor (Morrison) and sundry
members of the reporting staff. And Sydney Sun
has an almost equal number, from Monty Grover
(editor) downwards.
The windy Fitzpatrick, M.L.A. for Orange (N.S.W.),
once published a book of poems sawn out of the
same stuff as his speeches. Some of the modest leaps
of his Pegasus: “O Baby Boy,” “The Farmer,”
“Wanted a Leader,” and “The Last Day is Come.”
The next bunch is more suggestive of oysters and
stout: “To Jess,” “To Agnes,” “To Dorothy,” and
“To R .” Also there were “Golden Memories.”
If some other members had written that last master-
piece, it would be safe to bet that thoughts of old
times on the Tote Commission had suggested it. But
whatever else he may be, Fitzpatrick isn’t that sort.
“S.T.S.” : Note the value of a comma. In Thomas
E. Spencer’s last novel, “Bindawalla,” he wrote
(doubtless) : “Leslie lit a cigar. Ruby seldom in
after-life smelt the aroma of a cigar but she fancied
she detected in it the scent of the pine logs as they
burned at the teamster’s camp-fire.” The printer has
dropped in a comma after the second “cigar,” and
quite altered the sense.
Sumner Locke wins a £2O prize offered by the
new London magazine, The Magpie, for a shori
story, “The Biggest Kill.” The energetic little
Melbourne woman is padding the inky way in
London nowatimes.
PAPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I begin to think there’s something in the paper that
you write on
(We’re all aware the pen’s the mightiest thing),
For there are certain sorts the muse attains its
highest flights on,
While others make for masticated string.
On paper made of sweepings from the street, it
stands to reason,
All self-respecting pens refuse to sweep
In a lofty classic cadence; and a decent ink would
freeze on
The chilling pulp of anything so cheap.
The perfumed, rosy sheets of love I leave beside the
question,
The cream-laid leaves on Which one may propose,
And the horny, hand-made horrors that convey the
faint suggestion
Of cold and callous lines of legal prose.
But here’s a slip that’s worthy of a rondeau or a
sonnet,
Its surface is a rhythm, its edge a rhyme;
Its very virgin vacancy bears melody upon it,
Its “face” recalls the age of the sublime.
The smooth and sized productions of the age of the
mechanic
Inspire to airy trifles, nothing more ;
But there is inspiration of a nature quite Satanic
In this upas-fibre fabric stiff with gore.
Then here’s a thing of straw that calls the cunning
pen to scribble,
To tell the way the wind blows on the mart,
And for flimsy flam and flip-flap and for fine inflated
fribble—-
Like this—which raises footle to an art.
Give me a leaf of copy-book, I’ll jerk you moral
dicta,
Post obiter, or otherwise, to show
The sin of getting caught in aught, in flagrante
delicta—
Oh, where’s my Latin grammar?—delicfo.
You’ll wonder what I’m writing on ; what papyrustic
satyr
Is daddy to this faun (that’s whitey-brown).
Well, most of it, I’ve told you in my modest im-
primatur,
Is foolscap, making up for double-crown.
MAJORCA.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
More Light.
More light! The sun is glaring overhead,
And the hot earth below is glowing white;
nor blue, nor steel, but beaten lead,
Fining to furnaced porcelain, azure bright,
Where the horizon with the sea is wed,
And shimmering curtains veil their bridal
bed.
More light! the spirit cries, where, too
intense,
Uttermost radiance fills the earth and air:
So cry the shackled mind and stifled sense,
Smothered by lore, and blinded by the glare
And flash of science, striving for the sight
Of loved and lovely things and visions fair,
And the unearthly luminance of night.
Shadow these blasting fires ! Ah ! Light!
More light!
MAJORCA.
LINDO-WEBB.
Via Dolorosa.
Brother in sorrow and seedy clothes,
Linen unlaundered and furtive air!
Desperate, destitute, though God knows
When graft was going you did your share!
Courage! It need not be always thus;
You are not done if you wish to rise.
Sweet is Adversity’s use to us ;
A man must want if he would be wise.
What time he wrote of the passing show —
Umar, the Persian, he knew, I wot,
What hunger was ; and he had to go
In rags and tatters as like as not!
How knew he else of the way to sing
His so convincing philosophies?
What gave his stanzas the real, true ring?
A man must want if he would be wise !
How have you bartered your fair renown?
Which of the terrible trinity—
Drink, drugs, women —has got you down?
One of them was it —or all the three?
What is the fault that you curse as “Fate”
That still at the root of the mischief lies?
I’ve been right thi-ough, and I know it,
mate:
A man must want if he would be wise!
Brother in woe —has it gone too far?
Say ! Has the canker too firm a hold?
Heavens! how foolish we mortals are!
How very cheaply our lives are sold !
Dust and ashes, and Dead Sea fruit!
Sorrow and shame, and our souls the price—
Miserv utter and absolute!
A man must want if he would be wise!
N.S.W.
JOHN WALTERS.
A Rondeau.
(Of her Hatt-Pinne.)
A Hatt-Pinne fine doth Celia weare
Through inches ten of shinynge hayre;
And as she steppeth down ye streete
On many a youthe she smileth sweete
And gives “Good-daye” to many a Fayre.
And well for all, I ween, it were
If nearer to her none wolde dare,
Unless, perdie, they long to meete
Her Hatt-Pinne fine.
A lover’s lance, it stabbes ye hayre,
And likewise them who, unawayre,
Wolde come aniglie the lass to greete
Who treads our hertes beneath her feet,
And bids all pyrate lippes Beware
Her Hatt-Pinne fine !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
The Beef-fed Muse.
An American lady who has opened a students’ inn
for the Bohemians of New York, declares that “home-
made food, plain and plenty of it, is the best fare for
poets.”
Spread out no more on tapestry of blossom-
bordered silk
The angels’ fare to feed the souls of dreamers
of our ilk.
With some quaint mythological and pagan-
hued caprice
(As Jason and Medea questing for the Golden
Fleece),
Worked out in roll’d
Or beaded gold,
To make a centre-piece.
N T o heart-dust from the lilies swaying by the
river’s rim
Borne in on trays of silver by the singing
seraphim ;
No petals from the roses, blooming red by
castle sills;
No incense from the altars of the everlasting
hills,
Where breezes play,
Both night and day,
Amongst the daffodils.
No honeyed tongues of nightingales; no
wings of butterflies
Entrapped in nets of gossamer, nymph-spun
in Paradise;
No flavorings from pearly gems, dredged
from a star-strewn sea ;
No wine pressed from the rubies of the rills
of Arcady,
When in his fold
Each man shall hold
A goddess on his knee.
No lights drawn from the rainbow, and no
all-pervading myrrh
Breathed from the wattle’s blossoms when
the dreaming zephyrs stir;
No music fingered on the fronds by Mid-
night’s tuneful elves,
Who drown in cadent choruses the clock’s
rebuking twelves.
No more we’ll crave
What Fancy gave
The Muses’ larder shelves.
But give us hunks of sirloin from a lately-
murdered bull,
Aye, give us hunks —and hunks enough to
fill us very full.
Just feed your roses to the swine, nor even
keep the buds —
No matter if the scented mire is spattered
on your duds....
You’ve smeared with grease
The centre-piece.
It’s Jason for the suds!
s.a.w.
R. J. CASSIDY.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques. Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
nil Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions , ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Edi.or, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name, “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there j the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number •' is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social- or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing ivith Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or
my, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
Tj ie Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture,
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 30, 1913.
ON THE URGENCY OF POETICALLY
EDUCATING AUSTRALIA.
It is significant that the whole of the disin-
clination to instruct the people in the pros
and cons of the Referendum questions
should have been displayed by Liberalism.
There has been no serious attempt in any
of the dailies to explain just what the pro-
posed amendments mean, and what is in-
volved in their acceptance or rejection. In-
stead, those journals have poured forth a
stream of blither about confiscation and day-
light robbery and the nationalisation of
everything—the secret aim, they allege, of
a sufficiently reputable lot of politicians
who, on the face of things, are seeking no
more than to effectuate certain measures of
which the country has approved, and for
which the Deakin party was itself respon-
sible in the first instance. When the Aus-
tralian Government decided last month to
print and distribute pamphlets containing
the cases for and against the Referendum
projects, Liberalism was as angry as though
some flagrant swindle had been unearthed.
Sydney Telegraph, for example, was scan-
dalised —just as it used to be a year or so
ago when the subject of compulsory service
was mentioned. Everywhere the voices of
Conservatism were to be heard remarking,
m effect, that an outrage was proposed, and
that insanity was abroad. They are still
uttering cries of a similar description, and
the reason lies on the surface. The whole
history of the Referendum goes to show that
a nation will never vote in favor of a Con-
stitutional change which it does not fully
understand. This applies even to the radi-
cal-minded Swiss. Between 1874 and 190(5
the Referendum has been employed 18 times
in the land where the waiters are reared.
Twelve of the proposed amendments have
been carried, the others rejected. All but
two of those which have been agreed to
were turned down one or more times before
the people decided that there was no danger
in them. Of the six highly-complicated Ini-
tiative demands which have been put to tiie
electors since 1891, only one has been ac-
cepted. Several of the proposals which have
been rejected over and over again by the
nation, and which are still awaiting accept-
ance — e.g., Compulsory Insurance, a Fed-
eral match monopoly, and the Right-to-
Labor scheme of 1894 —have been approved
by both Legislative Assemblies; and they
are still approved by people who would be
infuriated if they were accused of being
Socialists. Until the common or garden
elector has got the complete hang of any
vast change in his system of government, he
will have none of it. He will not leap in
the dark. That is one of the reasons why
the fight for Home Rule has been such a
long business, and it accounts, to a large
extent, for the heart-breaking difficulties
which confront those who are striving to
save the United Kingdom from itself by
means of a Protective tariff. To boil the
position down, a nation cannot get ahead of
its average level of knowledge and intelli-
gence. It automatically achieves the legis-
lative changes to which its capacity and edu-
cation entitle it.
When the. position is regarded in its pro-
per perspective, it would be a very wonder-
ful thing if with the scanty means of infor-
mation that have been placed at their dis-
posal, the bulk of the electors of Australia
did know epough of the problems involved
in the Referendum proposals to enable them
to give a considered verdjct. A comprehen-
sion of the changes involved necessitates a
close acquaintance with the doings of the
Australian Parliament since 1905, if not
earlier. How many citizens remember to-
day, in any sort of detail, what Parliament
did so recently as yesterday? It also neces-
sitates a certain knowledge of Constitutional
law. What proportion of the electors takes
any stock whatever in Constitutional law?
Most intelligent people have a general idea
of the meaning and aim of, say, the New
Protection. They understand that the idea
was to impose excise duties on certain classes
of manufactured goods unless such goods
should have been manufactured under con-
ditions approved of by Parliament or by in-
dustrial authorities appointed by Parlia-
ment. But few of them appreciate the
deadly effect of the Trade and Commerce
Clause in the Federal Constitution, on that
almost uniyersally-approved measure of re-
form. Not one elector in 10 has troubled to
follow the closely-reasoned discourse in
which Chief Justice Griffith declared the
Excise Tariff Act to be invalid. In fact,
setting aside the average man, it seems safe
to say that not one solicitor or importer, or
architect, or squatter, or other presumably
cultivated citizen in 10 has any serious ac-
ouaintance with the questions at issue in
the Referendum campaign. Let a Referen-
dum discussion be started in any first-class
railway carriage, or club, or expensive hotel,
and it as certain as death that the man who
says he means to vote Yes will bring a stm-m
of dissent and reproach on his head. But
none of it will betray any inner knowledge.
Wild, inconclusive shouts about Socialism,
Disloyalty, anti-imperialism and dishonest
schemes to separate honest men from their
hard-earned savings will be heard. The Pro-
gressive will be called a pro-Boer—mayhap,
told that he has no right in any respectable
bar. The less muddled objectors may per-
haps introduce the State Rights question,
and contend that a thousand bizarre in-
famies may be done to Victoria or N. S.
Wales by the Australian Government if
Parliamept is given any more power than it
has. But these enraged folks are incapable
of explaining the whys and wherefores of
their weird theories. If anyone doubts the
tragic ignorance of the average prominent
citizen in regard to the signification of the
Constitutional changes that the .Australian
Government aspires to make, he might find
the following table of test questions useful
as a means of dispelling his illusions. Let
him put these queries to the citizen in ques-
tion, and if that self-opinionated person sup-
plies. off-hand, a coherent and generally
satisfactory answer to so much as a third of
them, this paper will eat its oldest and
toughest hat.
(1) What was the Trade Marks Act. 1905? On
what grounds was it declared unconstitutional? Give,
roughlv. the gist of the dissenting judgments in this
matter?
(21 What, in particular, stirred Justice HrGGINS
to complain that the Federal Arbitration Court had
been surrounded by the High Court with a “Ser-
bonian bog” of technicalities? Supply reasons, if any
exist, why this state of things should be suffered to
continue.
(3) Do you revere the British Constitution? If
not, whv not? If so, why do you regard Unification
as a calamity in the case of Australia ? Is there a
Trade and Commerce clause in the British Constitu-
tion, and, if not, how do you account for the fact
that the commercial rights of (say) Yorkshire have
never been assailed by tfie House of Commons?
(4) What bearing had Appleton's case on the power
of the Australian Parliament to deal with Trusts?
Assuming you had an income of T 3 10s. per week,
do you consider that you would get better value for it
in the U.S., where the Trusts are nractically uncon-
trolled ; or in England, where Parliament is empow-
ered to scotch Trusts the moment they get over the
odds in the matter of rapacity ?
(5) Have you read Bryce's “The American Com-
monwealth,” Dicey's “The Law of the Constitution.”
or Wise's “The Commonwealth of Australia” ? If
not, what standard works have you studied in the
search for enlightenment on the more intricate pro-
blems of government?
It is pretty obvious that the man who is
baffled badly by such questions is acting the
part of a fraud and a liar when he dogma-
tises on the subject of the Referendum cam-
paign, and the horrors of an affirmative or
any other sort of vote. He is as out of place
in any discussion on the subject as an ape
would be at the helm of a battleship. One
of Australia’s tragedies lies in the fact that,
in every walk of life, he is appallingly fre-
quent.
The Bulletin remarked close on 18
months ago, when the Fisher Government’s
Referendum proposals were turned down:
‘‘The task that faces the Nationalist party
is now mainly educative.” The pronounce-
ment still stands. And as no progress is
going to be achieved till the gloom lifts,
those who are interested in the future of the
Commonwealth require to get busy without
delay. A well-run democratic daily would
do much, but there seems to be no prospect
of anything of the sort before next Federal
elections. Lacking such an organ of - en-
lightenment, knowledgable speakers want to
pound the facts of the case into the some-
what wooden head of the people with tire j
less energy. On political platforms, during
the next few months, the dreary old topic of
the enemy’s crimes, idiocies and demerits
generally might with advantage be dropped,
and the attention of the orators confined to
some such subject as the urgent need to em-
power Parliament to frame a uniform com-
pany and banking law, and an explanation
of the reasons why nothing can be done in
that direction at present. It is immaterial
whether the problems are approached from
a Liberal or Labor standpoint; the essential
thing is that they should be dragged into the
light of day and aired in a spirit of intelli-
gence and sweet reasonableness. Acknow-
ledged experts—men of the tvpe of W. M.
Hughes. W. H. Irvine, P. McM. Glynn
and B. R. Wise —would do their country a
service if they lectured on these big and in-
volved issues. Above all, the best use should
be made by both Labor and Liberal Leagues
of the informative pamphlets which the
Fisher Government is making available to
the electors —the volumes which the Sydney
Telegraph, ever progressive, described as
“errand boy literature” and “waste paper.”
If these booklets are done with any sort of
skill —and there is no reason to think they
will not be —the elector will only have his
own apathy and incapacity to blame if, after
due investigation, he hasn’t a fair working
idea of what the Government aims at and
the Opposition objects to.
TO SAFETY—WITH DANGER IN THE
ROAD.
The Australian system of sea-defence has
made good in the eyes of those best quali-
fied to judge. Admirals of the British fleet,
statesmen with their fingers on the pulse of
East and West, have alike spoken th ir ap-
proval, and those tremendous loyalists who
some time ago waved toy flags and shouted
for the presentation of Dreadnoughts for the
sake of Empire (with and without an H)
now find that the Empire itself was on the
side of their opponents and applauds the
Australian patriotism which decided to
create a fleet unit of its own, in spite of all
the frothy mouthings of those who thought
Empire was a bundle of imported rags, or
a Governor’s cocked hat, or some equally
futile thing connected in some indefinable
way with a large stomach and a Union Jack.
Now that an Australian fleet is actually
taking fjjrm, the whole thing is seen in its
true light. The declaration that Australia’s
tate must be decided in the North Sea is
counted out, the menace of Asia is fully re-
cognised, and the defence of the Pacific is
seen as a problem of the white ra.ce which
can only be settled in the Australian way.
* * * *
The practical object of the Conference pro-
posed to be held at Vancouver is to bring the
Dominions of Canada and Maoriland into line
with Australian ideas. Everything points
to the conclusion that these countries will
be urged, and even pressed, to create their
own fleet units which in time of trouble
may be ready to co-operate for the main-
tenance of peace or the speedy success of
war. _ It is a big step forward, and imagina-
tion is not called upon to do any special
gymnastic feats in conjuring up a mental
picture of the great white man’s fleet of the
future which may absolutely hold and police
the Pacific. For all practical uses the fleets
of the United States be one with those
of Canada, Australia and Maoriland; and
with the enormous potential wealth which
can be drawn upon, and the constantly in-
creasing population of the countries con-
cerned, it does seem hopeful that the naval
strength of the white man in these seas will
grow to be such that no nation or combina-
tion of nations will dare throw down the
gage of battle or attempt anything in the
nature of invasion. Thus, armed for peace
everybody may be able to sleep quietly at
night, and the whole garden will be lovely.
* * * *
But in the working out of what really
amounts to a great insurance scheme against
the horrors of Asiatic war lies Australia's
more immediate danger. The wide un-
settled lands of the Commonwealth present
a far more attractive field for exploitation
to the Asiatic at our northern gate than the
cold western portion of Canada, the already
well-settled Pacific Slope, or the small and
mountainous islands of Maoriland. If Asia
is to find room and scope for expansion in
a country of sunshine and opportunity it
must be in the northern portion of the Aus-
tralian continent. As things are, the per-
son in Canton or Tokio knows there is no
need to hurry. He is well aware that it
must be some years before Australia has an
adequate system of defence, and with ordin-
ary luck there should be complications in
Europe which will give him his chance be-
fore the balance of war-machinery or pre-
paration is against him. But with naval
combination, and three, if not four, nations
building fleets to checkmate him in any game
he may play, the whole position of affairs
will be entirely changed. His best if not
only hope, in that case, is to move swiftly and
try to reach his goal before the white Triple
Alliance of the Pacific has become too strong
for him. Plainly, then, the Vancouver Con-
ference. while working towards ultimate
safety for all the countries concerned, will
increase the immediate danger of Australia
However rapidly the programme of ship-
building may be pushed forward, it must be
several years before the joint fleets of the
white races in these seas are equal to the
present Asiatib navies. It is during those
vears that the real Australian danger must
be looked for; and the greater the speed
with which keels are laid and plates ham-
Tngred the nearer is that critical moment
forced. Asia is not entirely without weight
in the councils of Europe ; and the trouble
out of which it may grasp its opportunity
can in large measure be engineered. When
it is remembered that with all civilised
peoples straining after peace, and most bar-
barous nations fearing war, it requires the
utmost skill on the part of European states-
men two or three times in every year to pre-
vent an international resort to arms, it must
be seen how easy a matter it is for a power-
ful outside nation to disturb the delicate
balance of affairs or increase the tension to
breaking point. With a big war on in
Europe Asia gets its chance, and unless the
future 4 b'elies all the past, Asia will work for
that chance before the infant navies that are
being built have reached the point of being
formidable barriers. This is not mere specu-
lation, but a reasoning based upon the
natural law that peoples which are held in
check by none of the restraints of European
civilisation will follow the line of least re-
sistance and get all that they can a,t the
smallest cost to themselves.
Australia’s position in the matter should
be quite clear. Eventually, if any aggres-
sive move on the part of Asia can be suffi-
ciently delayed, the great combined fleet of
the Pacific will make the Commonwealth
safe and enable it to work out its own
destiny as a white man’s country.
In the meantime sea defence cannot be
relied upon to give us adequate pro-
tection, and we must look to our military
system to pull us through. All the strategic
necessities of our citizen army—uniform
gauges, a direct railway from south to
north, the proper settlement and garrison-
ing of the Northern Territory—will become
infinitely more urgent after the Vancouver
Conference than they are to-day. All the
work of that Conference and all the hope
of a great Pacific fleet may amount to no-
thing if Australia should fail to hold its own
in the years during which the fleet is in the
making. To put the matter in the
possible way: yesterday Australia had pro-
bably 20 years before it had reason to seri-
ously fear invasion; in six months’ time
that period of probation may be reduced to
five years or five weeks. If the Vancouver
Conference does the work that is expected
of it, Australia becomes party to a challenge
thrown down to Asia for the supremacy of
the world’s biggest ocean. That challenge
may be a loud noise or a serious fact; but
having made the challenge we must be ready
to stick to it, and expect the other fellow
to hit us where and when he can without
further warning. That we cannot be wholly
prepared goes without argument; but by
good luck, and by taking earnest matters
earnestly, we may be a good deal better pre-
nared than the other fellow gives us credit
for being. In any case, it is the only chance
Wo TlflVO.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Blayney, and After.
The results of the Blayney election make
one fact surprisingly plain. Wade is still a
tremendous asset to those politically opposed
to him. Beeby, with his nebulous third
party propaganda, made a poor enough show-
ing at the first poll; but when it came to a
question of Wade or no Wade, the public
decided that any old thing was better than
to give a majority in Parliament to the party
led by the hero of Kembla. From the moment
that Beeby definitely declared that he would
give Wade no support, his stock went up
enormously. So the third party gets its
show, and may with luck become a second
party, and oust the Wade-Wood conspiracy
from its place as the recognised Opposition.
There is very little doubt that many elec-
torates in which there has hitherto been a
fairly even balance of parties would vote
much as Blayney did if a moderately attrac-
tive third-party man took the field; and a
party which had no black record behind it
(having the good fortune to have no record
at all), and which would put forward a
platform that looked a little like progress
(though it mightn’t be progress to any ex-
tent), ought to have a very fair show as
the game is now played. The nity of it is
that all three parties in N.S.W. stand for
State Frights, and all the small and tricky
devices which mav be employed to hamper
the progress of the Commonwealth. If we
must have narty oolitics. it seems a poor
business that a State like N.S.W. can’t
have something which at least resembles an
Australian party. The Laborites promised
to he that party, and their promise is still
all right; but the performance of McGowen,
Holman and the lately-departed Beeby has
been simply ghastly.

A Manly Nation at the Bast Ditch.
The “manly nation” theory looks strange
and sick when considered in the light of Tur-
key’s present situation. If the Turks have
seldom been described in the exact terms of
the theorists who despise armaments and
advocate baresark exploits in the hour of
national extremity, they have at least been
recognised as possessing the qualities of ster-
ling soldiers. Although their record for some
hundreds of years has been mainly one of
retreat and disaster, and although their em-
pire has been constantly decreasing in area,
there has been scarcely an Ottoman cam-
paign in which Turkish valor has not won
the plaudits of the world. Now the Turk
finds himself pent within his capital, deserted
by Europe, beaten in arms by the last and
least of peoples, hated for his cruelties, de-
spised for his ignorance and yet too proud to
yield. Now, here is a case for the
“manly nation” theorists. If valor, backed
by desperate zeal, could save a nation against
modern armaments. Turkey ought still to
come out on top. It has plenty of men. an
immense prestige, which, though badly
shaken, is still workable, and it is at least
10 times better armed, and prepared than,
say, Australia. The supporters of the
“manly nation” idea would have Australia
wait until it is attacked, and then bog in just
as H stands. Now. why can’t Turkey hog in
as it stands, and win?

A Study in Political Suicide.
According to S. M. Herald (23/1/T3)
There is a division in the N.S.W. Cabinet over the
question as to whether the new State Governor shall
reside at Federal Government House. The chances
are said to be in favor of the building being again
occupied by the King’s representative.
It is significant that the Herald, which
was formerly absolutely violent on this sub-
ject, had no protest to make against the
change which it forecasts. State-fright
Liberalism is already beginning to rightly
view “the Eviction” as a masterly move
in the anti-Australian campaign. The
McLinen-Henley agitation has fallen flat.
None but a numerically insignificant class
takes these deplorable old advt.-seekers seri-
ously : and of this section fully 90 per cent,
have now realised that the ousting of the
Governor-General represents a smack in the
eye to the Australian Government. The
paltry minority, composed of incredibly
stupid Tories, such as those who still advo-
cate the hired-navy system, continue to
curse the Eviction, and to subscribe money
to dispute the legality of it. But in a little
while they, too, will see, and applaud the
disreputable business as warmly as any of
the alleged anarchists who attended the
banal opening ceremony in Government
House grounds last month. Some facts
which have dawned on the Sydney Liberal
or Geebung are that by turning Federal
Government House into a State residence,
a more financially important type of Gov-
ernor may be attracted (the Rawson
variety of figure-head could not possibly
support the place) ; that Sydney will there-
by be benefited in its pocket and its pride;
and that the local Personage will no longer
have to periodically play second fiddle on
his own heath, so to speak, to a higher func-
tionary. Above all, it has been borne in on
the Liberal that the Australian Government
has been affronted and embarrassed, and no-
thing cheers a genuine Liberal more than
that. What consolation the people who
supported the McGowen Government at
last elections are to get out of the unsavory
business is, however, hard to say. Most
democrats are Australian-minded, and don’t
relish the spectacle of a Commonwealth
Labor administration being harassed. They
are, moreover, very far from desiring a
change by which the office of State Governor
is likely "to be perpetuated indefinitely. In
fact, they want the imported State Gov-
ernor arrangement abolished as soon as pos-
sible. The imported official costs the State
at least £IO,OOO per annum, and does abso-
lutely nothing for the money that might
not be done equally well by a local Chief
Justice. It is a good point, more especially
iu the case of a State which owes
£100.000,000, and begins, in consequence, to
feel the need of taking a pull on itself here
and there. In fact, it is so good a point
that the wiping-out of the office of State
Governor has for long been the first plank
in the N. S. Wales Labor Party’s platform.
To Democrats, then, the McGowen Gov-
ernment’s action in manoeuvering to dispos-
sess the Governor-General, with the object
of installing a State Governor, will certainly
appear inexplicable and exasperating. As-
suming the Herald is correct in its fore-
cast (and The Bulletin, which has been
making remarks to the same effect for the
past nine months, has no reason to think
otherwise), the position amounts to this.
The McGowen Government, by a •nammotn
sacrifice of principle, will have gained the
gratitude of militant State Frightism. But.
this section consists almost entirely of red-
hot Conservatives, who would as soon vote
for a skunk as for a Laborite On the
other hand, the Government will have won
the furious disapproval of every Nationalist
and genuine Laborite in the country. It
political battles are to be won a ong those
bizarre lines, this paper has still a lot to
learn about electioneering.

The Doctrine Which Is Written Down
by Its Own Supporters.
It must make the genuine anti-Socialist
writhe to read the views ofaUegcdlyann-
papers of the S. M. herald
stamp —that is to say, the views that are
expressed elsewhere than in the leading
articles The Socialism of these pronounce-
ments is naked and unashamed In the most
cynical fashion imaginable, the writers in
the alleys and byways give the lie direct to
those whose job it is to move heavily along
what one may call the main streets, or
market places, expounding the official policy
of Liberalism. Take, for example, the fol-
lowing, which appeared in the municipal
section of the Herald on Thursday last.—
The municipality of Manly has no.
ns we find in the seaside resorts in the Old World. As
f contrast to Manly—perhaps, also a source of inspira-
tion—take Torquay, on the English coast. ll >e coun-
cillors of Torquay have done much in the way of
municipal enterprise to draw mjjuw
corporation’s* pmposa7s "include a "modern installation
of medicated, electric and seaweed baths, the improve-
ment of the Princess Gardens, the erection of a public
library, municipal offices, town hall, and pavilion for
municipal orchestra. The pavilion is just completed,
at a cost of £21,000. The Princess Gardens are laid
out upon reclaimed land. They contain a theatre, a
cafe, a restaurant, a roof promenade, dressing-rooms,
etc. At the official opening the fine municipal
orchestra supported a galaxy of operatic artists from
the London Opera House, and an audience of 2000
assembled to hear them. Scarborough is another sea-
side resort from which the Manlv councillors could
learn some “points.” The possibilities of Manly are
illimitable; and they should be developed.
Let the reader imagine for a moment that
the mayor and aldermen of a N. S. Wales
township have been sufficiently daring to
follow' the Herald’s advice. They have
started Socialistic theatres, picture shows,
restaurants, hotels and caf£s. They have
not gone to the extreme length, perhaps, of
putting £21,000 into a music pavilion and
importing expensive singers to their Social-
istic opera house. Instead of these luxuries
they have invested what superfluous cash
they had in more or less necessary things
like brick-works, fruit-shops and milk-runs.
Now picture the attitude of the S. M.
Herald’s leading columns in regard to those
ventures —something like this :
SOCIALISM IN OUR TIME.
Six months ago the ratepayers of the thriving town-
ship of Bustte were misguided enough to return a
majority of Laborites to the local council. We ven-
tured to prophesy at the time that an era of mad
experimental Socialism would swiftly follow. Our
forecasts, we regret to say, were nearer the mark than
we ourselves anticipated in our most apprehensive
moments. Discretion, economy, the wisdom and ex-
perience of the ages from the days of Plato to our
own time have been flung aside. The Socialistic Tiger
rears its horrid head broadcast, and there are none
among the City Fathers with the sense or courage to
beat it back. Socialistic baths, playhouses, eating-
places, drinking-shops, roof gardens, caffes, etc., have
already been planned, with ruthless disregard both to
the rights of Private Enterprise, which has been suffi-
ciently courageous to blaze the track (however inade-
quately) in all these lines of business, and to the first
principles of political economy. The more thoughtful
residents are inexpressibly shocked. Many of those who
flouted Liberalism, against our express and repeated
warnings, on the grounds that it was “a fair thing” to
“give Labor a show,” are now extremely perturbed.
“No more Labor for me,” one of them was heard to
remark bitterly, a few days ago. Meanwhile the
damage is done. Capital is leaving the township in
thousands.
And so forth, and so on. That is the sort
of assault that would be visited by the
S. M. Herald on the municipality
which was sufficiently progressive to
take the advice which the S. M.
Herald itself tendered to Manly last week.
It is a curious position. A Labor paper
which, while nominally believing in munici-
pal and State enterprise, barracked tire?
lessly for the abolition of post offices, hospi-
tals and other manifestations of Socialism,
would be no more illogical, and no less un-
worthy of serious attention.
The War-horse Supply.
The Japanese mailboat Kumano Maru,
which left Sydney last week for Yokohama,
had a number of horse-boxes on the after-
well deck. They contained 15 Australian
thoroughbreds intended for the Jap forces,
and the army captain who bought them was
good enough to tell the newspaper young man,
before leaving, that he thought the animals
“would make fine chargers for the Japanese
officers.” No doubt they will. But their
departure also makes it harder for the Aus-
tralian Light Horseman to pick up a decent
mount at a reasonable figure. The Indian
and “Eastern” trade has, for years, been
draining away thousands of Australia’s
best horses; but the patriotic onlooker
has at least had the satisfaction of
knowing during all that time that
most of them were going to carry British
warriors. Also, the S’African war re-
duced Australia, for a considerable time,
to a state of equine insolvency. The
Kumano Maru shipment is going to
the other side of the Pacific without
let or hindrance of any sort. The Aus-
tralian might take off his hat and breathe a
short prayer that neither the valuable 15
nor their descendants may ever set hoof
again on Australian soil. If they do, the
odds are they won’t come barebacked. Mean-
time, Robertson, veterinary expert to the
Australian National Government, has just
yielded a report which points out the grave
state of the local military horse supply. Com-
mandant Hoad declared that £317,400 ought
to be spent at once on breeding establish-
ments. Robertson declares that all this,
and more, is needed. The Australian army
requires in one way and another 40,000 good
horses. Kitchener, Hoad and all authori-
ties concurred that it was no use relying on
hired artillery horses. They must be trained to
it, or the guns cannot be served, as is proved
in' all manoeuvres. Further, if we really
hold ourselves prepared for w T ar, there must
be a margin for great wastage, and a copi-
ous supply behind, ready to come up like a
bubbling spring. As for that wastage, it is
worth recalling that General I rench
knocked out 10.000 horses during the march
to Pretoria —which gives some idea of what
the reserve ought to be.
\ glaring example of the manner in which
the wishes of the democracy are thwarted
bv Partv Government is to be found in the
present condition of affairs in regard to the
tariff. Returns just issued show that last
vear imports into the Commonwealth totalled
£7B 098 313, an increase of £ll : 13U,8zo on
those of the preceding year, which in its
turn beat 1910 by nearly seven millions. On
the other hand, our exports last year de-
clined to the extent of £647,528. At the last
election the people spoke as clearly as pos-
sible bv returning to Parliament 52 Pro-
tectionists pledged to Tariff Reform. Now
another election is almost upon us. and the
tariff is in no more danger of being re-
formed than Lais, of Little Lon or Boozer
Rttt or anv other old-established human
derelict. Customs Minister Frank Tudor,
at heart a good Protectionist, is in the grip
nf the party machine, and has refused in his
Ministerial capacity to carry into effect .his
own 'election From the Fusion,
which has just elected one of the most per-
sistent Freetraders as its leader, even less
is to be expected. Jokook has declared that
fart of his policy is that the present tariff
shall be maintained. This statement was
hailed with joy bv the I reetory Argus,
which stated that “no genuine demand, for
increased duties exists. Of course, it is
eminently reasonable that a Foreign-trader
like Jokook should wish to maintain a I ro-
tectionist” tariff which admits £i<X,(>()o.ooo
worth of imports in the course of a year.
But what of the people who sent those .y.
men to Parliament to reform the tariff and
make it truly Protectionist? In deference
to party demands both sides have “sunk the
fiscal question,” and if something drastic is
not done they will just about sink the Com-
monwealth. The most obvious and forthright
way to prevent this is to sink Party Govern-
ment.
The N.S.W. Industriae Gazette treated
itself to a retrospect in its last issue; and
the Labor Government which runs it might;
do a lot worse than read the contribution,
just to be reminded of a few of the results
of borrow and boom. The present outbreak
of borrowing insanity on the Ma State’s part
has never before been equalled. The nearest
approach was in the late ’Bo’s and early
’9o’s. Then Cohen buttoned up, and the In-
dustrial Department found itself busy ladling
out relief soup. In the winter of 1892 the
Bureau provided shelter and blankets for
500 or 700 destitute workers in Sydney, and
30,960 rations were distributed. In the first
month of the State Labor Bureau’s exist-
ence 4965 persons registered as being in need
of employment; within three months the roll
had grown to 8945, and in six months to
13,447. By February, 1893, 18,600 were
begging for work ; 7500 of these were married
men. The year 1893-4 was even worse. There
were then 6000 workers in the building trade
alone out of collar. Over 4000 men were
sent to old goldfields to fossick on rations,
and semi-communal settlements were estab-
lished at Bega, Wilberforce and Pitt_ Town.
In the year ended February, 1895, over
13,500 out-of-work men had been registered
at the Bureau, and 16,000 had been assisted
and sent to jobs. From that date to Febru-
ary, 1896, 14,600 men nlaced their names on
the register, and 20.570 had been aided.
Between February, 1895, and February,
1896, no less than £201,591 was expended in
free rations, relief work, etc. And it is safe
to say that tens of thousands of people who
never got State assistance needed it desper-
ately, tens of thousands of others felt the
pinch of want, and still other tens of thou-
sands who had believed themselves in com-
fortable circumstances found themselves poor.
All this was the direct and natural conse-
quence of a borrowing boom—of the creation
of artificial conditions precisely similar to
those which have been produced to-day by
the deplorable Labor Ministry which has
poured scorn on Labor principles and ioined
hands with the boodlers of the community.
—
At the last meeting of the Brisbane General
Hospital committee, it was decided to ask
the Home Secretary to revise or delete the
rule obtaining in most country hospitals that
“venereal cases shall not be admitted.” A
case in support of the change was reported
in the Cairns press some few months ago.
A man suffering from “a certain disease”
(those northern papers are very delicate)
applied for admission to the Cairns Hospital.
He was refused. He promptly went down
the main street, broke a street lamp, was
just as promptly arrested, and when he ap-
peared before P.M. Grant frankly stated
that he broke the lamp in order to get gaol
treatment. The P.M. was sympathetic, and
gave him two months; in which period he
thought “defendant might be cured.” Medical
science not only admits, but impressively
teaches, that of all diseases common to man-
kind there is none so far-reaching in its
effects as syphilis. Yet. while one case of
bubonic will cause a terrified scare, the
authorities turn the other adrift to do its
worst. It is as inexplicable as it is abomin-
able.
♦
The Picture Combine, blossomed the
other day, promises badly. Not only will it
raise the price of the people’s amusement,
but it has already smashed up the beginnings
of a hopeful Australian industry. No more
Australian-made films are to be shown in
the theatres of the Combine after the stock
on hand has been worked off. The local
picture-making staffs have been dispersed.
One firm not so long ago opened a fine
theatre and studio at Sydney, and laid down
an excellent plant for the production of
films. That theatre is now closed, and the
staff scattered. Other companies had then-
operators, their actors, and their weekly
gazette of local events. The Australian
play-writer was also pushing in and getting
an occasional job, and the job threatened to
become more frequent. The Combine, ends
all this It practically controls the picture
theatres of Australia. Its various members
have thrown their exclusive agencies into the
pool, and in order to get reductions on the
quantity have agreed to use only imported
films. Thus, though the local film-maker can
of course go on producing, he will be unable
to place his product. Two remedies suggest
themselves —legislation by the State to com-
pel picture showmen to present an Austra-
lian-made film for every imported one ; and
Federal legislation imposing a repressive
duty on imported films other than what are
known as “scenics,’ and such as from their
very nature cannot be produced in Australia
suc h, for example, as the Durbar pictures.
If such productions are excepted, the Gov-
ernment will lay-out the otherwise inevitable
whimper that the authorities are interfering
with the education of the people. With
these films excepted, there is no earthly
reason why the Government should not go
ahead ; there are a hundred reasons why it
should.
The training ship Tingira is stationed in
that part of Sydney Harbor called Rose Bay.
There abide permanently on board a large
crowd of embryo mariners and their instruc-
tors The water is shallow and more sluggish
in this part of Port Jackson than in almost
anv other. For that reason the neighbor-
hood smells none too sanitary at low t de
alreadv. After a year or so of the lingira
it threatens to be unbearable. There are
plenty of more suitable mooring grounds;
and to hang on to the present site is equiva-
lent to imploring Providence to send along
disease.
From another strangled town:
You drop out at a tiny railway station, after
passing through miles of spleudidcountrv, part of
the famous Liverpool Plains (N.S.W.). The fertility
of the land is, in one respect, the curse of the town ,
for it took the eye of the early land-grabber, while
less attractive country escaped. Thus it comes about
that the N S. Wales country townships which should
have gone 'ahead most have gone ahead least. .
There is a little wooden bridge over the Narnoi at
this Ounnedah —a bridge which brings back memories
of the one that used to swing across the old willow-
nattern date. It is perched high above the general
level and the approaches rise steeply on each side—
The Bridge Across the Crick.
(ReinQ a Sort of Sermon on the Beauties of Party Gov-
° ernment and the Utter Needlessness of the
Referendum.)
[Fob The Bulletin.]
Joseph Jones and Peter Dawking
Strove in an election fight;
\nd you’d think, to hear them talking,
Each upheld the people’s right.
Each declared he stood for Progress and
against his country’s foes
When he sought their votes at Wombat,
where the Muddy River flows.
Peter Dawking was a student,
Of a kindly turn of mind.
A reformer, keen but prudent,
Aiming but to serve mankind —
Just a simple, thoughtful scholar who e’er
kept this aim in view :
“Tricks and shams are sinful folly ; we must
see the Big Things through !”
Joseph Jones had never tasted
Fame, but he was sly and fat;
And, ere he set out, he pasted
This reminder in his hat:
“Noble aims are platform blither, and the
art of politics
Is the game of nobbling voters with soft
words and cunning tricks.”
Up at Wombat, where the Muddy
Trickles down amongst the ferns,
Folk care little for the study
Of great national concerns ;
But they pride themselves on being plain and
practical and slick.
And the burning local question is “the
bridge across the crick.”
Bland, unfaithful politicians
Long had said this bridge should be.
Some soared on to high positions,
Some sank to obscurity ;
Still the bridge had been denied it by its un-
relenting foes —
By the foes of patient Wombat, where the
Muddy River flows.
Peter Dawking, scorning party,
As an Independent ran;
Joseph Jones, loud, blatant, hearty,
Was a solid party man.
But electors up at Wombat vowed to him
alone they’d stick
Who would give his sacred promise for the
“bridge across the crick.”
Up at Wombat Peter Dawking
Held a meeting in the hall.
And he’d spent an hour in talking
On Reform’s insistent call
When a local grey-beard, rising, smote him
with this verbal brick:
“Are or are yeh not in favor of the bridge
across the crick?”
Peter just ignored the question,
Simple and unselfish man;
Understand a mere suggestion
Men like Peter never can,
Or that free, enlightened voters look on
Great Reforms as rot,
While a Burning Local Question fires each
local patriot.
Joseph Jones, serene and smiling,
Took all Wombat to his heart.
“Ah,” he said, his “blood was b’iling”—
He declared it “made him smart”
To reflect how thev’d been swindled ; and he
cried in thrilling tones:
“Gentlemen, your bridge is certain if you
cast your votes for Jones !”
Joseph Jones and Peter Dawking
Strove in an election fight,
And, when they had finished talking,
On the great election night
They stood level in the voting, and the hopes
of friends and foes
Hung upon the box from Wombat, where the
Muddy River flows.
Then the Wombat votes were counted :
Jones, tw r o hundred : Dawking. three.
Joseph, proud and smiling, mounted
On a public balcony,
And his friends awoke the echoes with
triumphant shouts of glee :
For that vote saved Jones’s Party by a one
majority!
Jones’s Party—note the seouel —
Rules that country of the Free,
And that fight, so nearly equal.
Swaved the whole land’s destiny.
And the‘Big Things of the Nation are de-
layed till hope grows sick. _
Offered up as sacrifices to “the bridge across
the crick.”
Brothers, in this Age of Reason,
Seers, economists and such,
Preaching in and out of season,
Seldom seem to matter much.
And, when next you see the Joneses snar-
ing votes with shameful tricks,
Marvel not that’ Big Things languish in the
game of politics.
Dawking ‘now is sadly fearing
For the crowd’s intelligence.
Joseph, skilled in engineering.
Full of porno and sly pretence.
Still holds out the pleasing promise of that
bridge whene’er he goes
Up to Wombat, patient Wombat, where the
Muddy River flows.

Full information and details at the Lyric, Colonial
Empress, and Crystal Palace Theatres, Sydney!
so steeply that laden teams have often to double-
bank to get oyer. When the river is low they use
the old crossing below the bridge in preference. That
seems to suit the unprogressive place best.
From Curlewls, about 11 miles nearer Sydney, the
traffic is much heavier than it is from Gunnedah.
But, then, all round Curlewis the land is closely
settled, while Gunnedah is shut in by large stations —
Pullaming andtGunnible on the east and north, and
half a dozen others to the west and south. “Any
amount of room to expand,” says the local buttress
of the principle of leaving things as they are; and
he gets a map and shows you how you can worm
your way in between the stations, and get here and
there a bit of soil. But the curse is none the less
real. The pick of the country is locked up; it is
under sheep, which give very little employment,
except at shearing-time (and most of the shearing
now is done by contractors who travel with their
teams) ; so the young men must go away to find
work, and the money that would otherwise be saved
Up and put into local farms goes far afield.
There are many empty shops in the one main
street, and few customers even for those that keep
their doors open. Gradually, too, the number of “emp-
ties” is growing instead of getting less, for new tenants
do not come to take the place of those who P'o. The
large modern station doesn’t want the local shop.
Tts own store is filled, and it does all its business
direct with Sydney. It keeps an account at the bank
in the township, loads its wool and such stock as it
s'dls at the local railway station, and receives its sup-
plies there. Jt gets its letters and telegrams, and
has a telephone line to the local post office, but that
is the extent ;of its connection with the township.
If it wants men it sends to the city or picks up
“travellers.” So the grievous state of the strangled
township is becoming more grievous than ever, and
the death gurgles grow fainter and fainter in its
throat.
Sydney’s great Easter jamboree of the
horse, cow, gum-leaves, farm produce, whisk-
ers, machinery, saddleship, etc., known as the
Royal Show, ; s to be made a bigger attrac-
tion this coming March than ever. The
prize money has been increased to £6OOO,
which means more competition, and that, in
turn, means a better exhibition. With favor-
able weather a general crash of records in
all departments is probable.
The N.S.W. Labor League has been hold-
ing its annual conference in Sydney this
week ; and the alleged Labor Government of
N. S. Wales has had a little of the roasting
that was coming to it. To prevent a repeti-
tion of past betrayals, the State pledge was
altered to bind Labor candidates to support
also Federal and municipal platforms. While
the conference was about it, it might also
have invited the party to get on speaking
terms with the State platform.
“Maorilander,” his protest:—
To me it is a sad thing that amongst the worst
and most foolish traducers of the Australian is the
Maorilander, or, rather, some of him. Visiting the
Australian cities for the first time, he is astonished to
find that things are not done anyway like they are
in Mangaweka or Invercargill. This makes him say
so much that the old reproof, “You ought to see the
moon we’ve got in Auckland,” has to be rubbed in.
The latest sinner is T. L. Mills, chairman of the Aus-
tralasian Country Press Association. In that capacity
he recently blew into Australia for the first time to
attend a conference. He was in the Commonwealth
fully 20 minutes, and when he returned burst into
nrint in a string of papers. This is how he summed
things up: (1) The Sydneysider’s horizon does not
extend further than his harbor, and the Melbournian’s
no further than St. Kilda ; (2) the man in the coun-
try longs for the day when he can get a job right in
the heart of things, where every prospect pleases and
only work isivilo; (3) the Australian citizen is sel-
fishness personified ; (4) the imported Socialist of the
glib tongue. . . .has got the ear of the masses, and is
the clog on the wheel of progress; (5) the result ?s
that in neither the Federal nor the N. S. Wales Par-
liament was there any appearance of respect or discip-
line;' (6) the onlooker from Maoriland felt thankful
every time he visited those chambers that he belonged
to another country and another people ; (7) there is
no strong dominating leader., and most of the Minis-
ters have that tired feeling; (8) the Laborites of
N.S.W. largely idolise Holman, and for long enough
have been urging that he. .. .should become Premier,
but he has not the tact of McGowen; and (9) a
whole lot of other stuff that usually is served with
onions. Included in it was a suggestion that the
Australian was a “clock-watcher” or loafer. Now. I
am a good Maorilander. and I have lived in Australia
for a considerable number of years (and years are
more than minutes), and, with unusual opportunities
of judging. I say that, man for man, the Australian
is one of the best workers (hand or brain) in the
world. Only the hedge-hog observer could class him
as selfish : and as for the political observations of
Mills, their absurdity requires no comment. I hate
to mention this matter, but somebody has to tell
the Maorilander (or some of him) that though ignor-
ance ma.y be bliss, there is no need to blow folly to
the skies.
From The Bulletin's Stipendiary
Corporal ■
Enver Bey, the “Young Turk,” who upset Kiamil
at the point of the revolver in Constantinople last
week, belongs to the order of men who do things, and
there are some charming possibilities about him. In
Tripoli, with a handful of Turkish troops and a crowd
of undisciplined Arabs, he reduced the Dago invaders
to such a of nerves that they would not leave
the shelter of their ships till he had been wounded
in his attack on Benghazi. This time he is up against
a sterner problem. The Bulgar is a better man than
the Dago ; yet if Enver is to make good he must
smash up that crowd of Bulgars outside Chataldja in
short order. Otherwise the Powers will probably
squelch him. If he can induce the Bulgars to at-
tack he will probably win easily enough. But the
chances are they won’t; and he must score a vic-
tory to justify himself. The Allies are bluffing, for
everyone of them is exhausted financially if not
physically. Enver is quite willing to let Albania
go. He will also cede a big lump of Macedonia, Novi
Bazar and other trifles. But Thrace and Adrianople
and a strip of Macedonia to take in Salonica he will
not part with without a fight. So he has rushed an-
other 30,000 troops across the Bosphorus, and with
300,000 men is preparing to take the offensive. On
the fate of his first battle much depends. If he wins
the Allies will drop bluffing and make peace. If he
loses, there will be a scramble for Constantinople.
And that may mean any sort of Hell’s brew.
