It is announced that there are only a few
more yards to go before Sydney Criterion
reaches the last of its “Milestones.” Those
few belated individuals who haven’t seen
this sermon in stones are adjured to be in
time for the last lap.
* * *
At Her Majesty’s, “The Sunshine Girl” is
the complete success that musical comedy
Girls always are wherever the flag of O’d
England, Bruce Smith and the British Em-
pire League is flow#. A dull song or two
have been lopped off her skirt, so to speak,
and a few yards of superfluous gags have
been hewn from her blouse, and the effect is
to make her even more distracting than she
was oh her first appearance 10 days ago.
“Faust” is in its last agonies at Sydney
Theatre Royal; in fact, the death-rattle may
be expected at any moment now. Playgoers
are not tired of seeing the central figure of
the show thrust into Gehenna nightly and at
periodical matinees. The trouble is that
other attractions are clamoring for the use
of his death bed.
* * *
At the Palace (Sydney), on Saturday
night, Allen Doone and Co. revived that
Doone-like production, “In Old Donegal.” The
show calls for no special mention, excepting
the astounding popularity of Doone’s singing,
or, maybe, it’s the songs that touch the
(mostly) Austral-Irish audiences on the
raw sentiment. It must be the songs. As
far as this paper is concerned, it reckons
that if Doone could act as well as he can
sing, he would be the Worst Actor in the
Vicinity. The season will close to-morrow
week.
The Church and the Devil, or. as it is
billed at the Sydney Adelphi. “The Monk
and the Woman,” are still enjoying a pros-
nerous alliance at the Marlow tabernacle.
The next event there is “The Beggar Girl’s
Wedding,” hut as yet its date is so vague
that the duke, or the major-domo, or who-
ever the other party is, may be trapped by a
red-headed widow before the worst can be
accomplished.
TWO TIVOLI WARBLERS.
Claire Romaine ,
Male Impersonator.
Chas. Zoli, Dago
comedian.
The Newcomers’ Stakes at the Tivoli this
week provides an exciting finish, and the
Lancashire Opera Co. gets the decision by a
head from the Maxini Bros, and their dog.
The Staley and Birkbeck musicians, who
finish third, bring along still another varia-
tion of the over-worked zylophone, plus some
quick scene-changing. Bobby, the Maxinis’
dog, is about the best-trained canine that has
happened along since Barnold’s Dan, and is
an accomplished acrobat as well as a humor-
ist. Of the rest of the field the Patty
Franks, Alexander Prince (his last per-
formances), and My Fancy carry most of
the public’s money. Claire Romaine’s ut-
terly impossible ihales continue to please
some and bore others: and Mozetto, Chas.
Zoli and Florence Baines are going strong.
On Saturday Rosie Lloyd (comedienne)
and Will Poluski (comedian) will do their
best to justify their labels.
St: * si: *
The Era Comedy Four—negro rag-time
specialists—are about the best goods the
National Amphitheatre has to offer this
week ; anyhow, they are among the freshest.
Wilson and Pritchard, acrobats of the more
or less humorous order, are likewise new to
this glittering haunt. Louie Duggan, Lyla
Thompson, Hugh Lloyd and Leonard Nelson
are also busily employed earning their screws
and avoiding the Bird, as good artists should.
Quite suddenly came the announcement of
the last nights of “Antony and Cleopatra,” at
Melb. Royal. The tempting Egyptian whom
hungry Tony, in his turn, did find a morsel
cold upon dead Caesar’s trencher, ere yet his
red hot gaze had warmed that regal piece to
passion, will nurse the pretty worm of Nilus
at her breast for the last time on Friday.
Next night she that was Cleopatra will so
bend her spirit to a blusterous bridegroom
that when he bids her name the sun the
moon she’ll go one better and call it blessed.
In short, “The Taming of the Shrew” fol-
lows “Antony and Cleopatra” on Saturday.
The revival is advertised for one week only,
and “Midsummer Night’s Dream” will pro-
bably be ready to succeed the domestic
comedy.
i!s ij*. ;Js
In its suggestions of panto, extravaganza,
the madness of “No Mother to Guide Her”
seems a seasonable enough Marlowmatter
at Melbourne Princess’s. The central figure
is Miss Ethel Buckley, in the character of
Little Red Walking Frock, or Sweet Nan of
Old Fury—a rough diamond of purest ray
serene. Her legs and feet are bare, her cos-
tume is most becoming, her manner of re-
r ceiving the first kiss of love from a converted
Bob betokens innocence and virtue, and her
habit of threatening Villainy with a firearm
at the end of each act commends the lady
to public esteem. In the beginning, the
festive Nan looks likely to become the Trilby
of criminal life. Hilliard Vox controls her
with his Svengalic eye and De Bierey swank.
Like one in a dream, the poor, bare-legged
maiden aids and abets the baleful Vox; but
as soon as he begins to mix murdering with
burgling, the hypnotic spell passes from her,
and she denounces him to the police, who
take no notice. Nobody on the stage takes
much notice of astounding incidents in “No
Mother to Guide Her.” Nor is there any
attempt to explain the title of the piece.
Nan, indeed, has a mother —a long-lost
mother found in Miss Guildford, a sinful,
scornful woman of fashion, who does nothing
but try to drown, choke and poison Miss
Elwyn Harvey, except when she is busy
making love to the Rev. Robert Inman. The
other heroine (Miss Harvey) is certainly
motherless, but she doesn’t Want guiding.
She has money to burn; she seeks the bubble
reputation even at the parson’s mouth, and
after the man with the gimlet eye lias hyp-
notised her into accusing herself of parricide,
she needs no mother’s hand to lead her out
of quod. Being rescued by Red Walking
Frock, she remains at large with maid-ser-
vants to wait on her, until fat old Burglar
Bill (J. P. O’Neill) proves her in-no-cence
with his dying confession. He dies of deli-
rium tremens, so his confession, sworn before
Bob and a donah, amounts to convincing evi-
dence. Some of the dialogue of the last act
indicates that Bill should have been scorched
to death, rather than jim-jammed. The plot
and dialogue are otherwise under suspicion
of having Marlowed on the premises. In any
case, the entertainment is a shocking absurd-
ity. Yet it might have been worse. It is at
least free from bedroom scenes, baronets and
glittering haunts of vice. J. S. Mann’s scen-
ery is good, better and excellent; also the
acting is far more than the drama deserves.
At the King’s, the company as well as the
bill is to be changed on Saturday. “My
Jack” closes the present season. A new
stage treatment of Marcus Clarke’s “Natural
Life” will restore Roy Redgrave to the scene
of his many bygone heroisms; and whilst
Good Mr. Dawes is working out his salva-
tion, Bad Mr. Gabbett will go chasing his
human dinner with an axe. This paper has
always loved Gabbett. He is its pet cannibal.

The holidays are ending, but “Puss-in-
Boots” continues to make good eight times a
week at Her Majesty’s. Strange to say, no
members of the co. have been seriously in-
disposed thusfar.
At the Opera House the mob of Arabs
(late of Sydney) burst into the proceedings
last Saturday, and did the things which their
ancestors and the ancestresses did when the
world was young. Like the laws of the Medes
and Persians the turn of fin Arab troupe
altereth not. The principal strong man in-
variably serves as the foundation of a great
human structure, and a co-operative gallery
is formed as of old, and the performance
concludes with somersaults and handsprings
all round. These Arabs come quite new to
the present Melbourne generation, and the
effect of their joyous strength and agility is
exciting. Dr. Mario and lady, in their Frogs’
Paradise act, are other welcome newcomers ;
Kitchen and Foy, on the horizontal bar and
bounding net, put some unfamiliar humor
into their clever gymnastics ; and De Biere
is the more sought after because he is about
to take his departure. Also, Nella Webb is
going away.
Melbourne Majestic has poured forth the
old tale of “The Babes in the Wood” in a
very bright series of Pa f be-colored views.
The picture was an excellent thing in its
way. Even the naughty uncle could not have
looked worse. “The Power of a Hymn” was
a contribution of a religious nature. Accord-
ing to the yarn a cove named Tom takes to
evil courses, but when he hears a hymn that
mother taught him in days of yore, he sud-
denly quits his wicked behavior and adopts
the plug-hat of a blameless life. “Bidoni
and the Lions” was a fairly humorous com-
edy, and there was also a picture of the
famous Don Q., the extremely rude old Dago
who left cadavers lying about.

Spencer has been screening an animal-
chewing sensation, “The Panther’s Prey,” at
Melbourne Olympia. The quadruped was
introduced into a narrative of sin and adven-
ture, and its attempt to dine on a lady of
engaging contours was as realistically effec-
tive as the circumstances would permit. For
several moments the heroine wrestled on the
floor with the hungry visitor, and she was a
most dishevelled and breathless female when
the hero blew in with a pistol and made the
beast a remains. “A Trip to Montserrat”
gave some views of that precipitous pimple;
and “Saving the Game” showed snapshots of
Yankee football, which seems a mixture of
hide-and-seek and running amok. Among the
comic efforts, “Love on Tough Luck Ranch”
presented some phases of the tender passion
with all the delicacy of dinosaurs at play.
The programme submitted by the Dandies
at St. Kilda (Vic.) last Saturday was duly
examined and found generally melodious.
Fred Monument presented the best bouquet
of ditties, his baritone being equal to the
demands of such a rollicking air as “The
Jolly Sailor.” Misses Tessa Byrne and Flor-
ence Henderson were as tuneful as formerly,
and Ben Calvert asked his Beloved to Look
Down in a voice as rich as a creamy fruit
salad. Miss Dorothy Manning, Renn Mil-
lar and Herbert Walton were also among
those who spoke with considerable success.
Adelaide shows have had the advantage
of the Foundation Day holiday, a new thing
to South Aus., but taken to kindlv. Lalie
Brooke (with costumesj, Paul Gorden (with
a slack wire) and George Dean (with his
voice), all at the King’s, are the chief items
of interest. Redgrave, Brampton, Her-
bert Leigh, and Miss Vera Remee at the
Royal, and Miss Mabel Hardinge at the
Tivoli, are all acting better than their plays
deserve. Wondergraph shows “John Ster-
ling” (a film of the Crook, the Cheild and
the Grafter, very American) ; West’s, “The
Mills of the Gods” (melodrama) ; Empire.
“The Stolen Favorite” (horse). And if
Branscombe’s “Dandies” would go wrong, or
break down, or be anything but steadily and
monotonously and deservedly popular, there
would be more to say about them.
“Petramos” (from London), concerning
the Grand Prix de Lucifer: —
When you tell Berlin that Paris is inclined to be
gay, Berlin utters the scornful ha-ha and says “You
must come out with me to-night.” Reviewing the
card, I think Berlin will get to Hell first. Paris
has been well trained, arid is likely to be a stayer.
It gets so much support from Englishmen and
Americans that the Folies Bergere runs an almost
all-English bill, and the ladies who provide relaxa-
tion in the garden and promenade provide it in very
good English. Also, a man may go to the devil
there on American cocktails, and find the journey
expensive. Most of Paris’s naughtiness is trained
on wine, whereas Berlin goes gay on lager beer.
Hamburg becomes a devil of a fellow on a cup of
coffee. These two German candidates for the Lucifer
Stakes are naturally giddy, and the visiting Presby-
terian must either learn the language or note the
dimple on the cafe girl’s knee with mute astonish-
ment. London parades its naughtiness and Picca-
dilly and Oxford-street, at 11.30 p.m., may mislead
backers into giving the Fog some support. Paris
presents a phenomenal bunch of giddiness in its
main streets also; but Berlin and Hamburg hide
their chances of success where no casual punter is
likely to see them. From stable secrets revealed,
however, I place them thus: Berlin, 1; Hamburg, 2;
Paris, 3 ; London a bad fourth.

AT POVERTY POINT.
Edward Branscombe’s attempt to create a literary-
drama circle in Tasmania by shipping McMahon’s
Repertory Co. ,for a holiday season in Hobart and
Launceston, seems to have been a bit premature.
Hobart was even less prepared for the shock than the
Younger City. The title of the opening piece—
“ Chains” —drew a large audience that mostly
wanted its money back on discovering that the
Chains were merely domestic. Hobart had expected
a lurid melodrama introducing a convict gang and
the clank of leg irons. On the second night five
policemen came to watch the plot of “Man and
Superman,” and report on its morals. Indecency
was suspected and blasphemy was feared, Super-
man” sounding rather like an alias for the Almightj.
The first act was too much for a number of the
naicest local ladies. After listening awhile to the
family discussion re Violet’s supposed impropriety,
those outraged ladies left the hall. They didn t wait
to learn that Violet had been a “respectable married
woman” all the time.
Van Biene’s death on the stage of the Brighton
(Eng.) Hippodrome was about the most opportunely
dramatic exit oh theatrical record. He didn t actually
die at the exact moment when he was supposed to die
in the play, but he dropped back in his chair insen-
sible at aljnost the exact moment, and was dead soon
afterwards’. His heart failed whilst he was bending
over his ’cello to nlay the last bars of ‘‘Home, Sweet
Home.” Practically he succumbed to the hard work
of expressively rendering a silly old tune on a chest-
contracting instrument.
“O Iv ” ■ Given an efficient musical training in his
youth’, the late Van Biene would probably have been
the greatest ’cellist of his time. As it was, he man-
aged to get himself taken seriously by the musical
world, and only last year he gave a series of recitals
in London. The few things that Van Biene had been
playing for many years in “The Broken Melody he
played remarkably well. He had the soul of the
artist and the true Cockney irreverence for the cant
of art. What time he was staging The Broken
Melody” at Melbourne Princess’s, a cellist named lol-
desy was appearing with Miss Elizabeth Parkins
(Parkina) at the Town Hall. Foldesy was a capital
“techniqueist,” with a somewhat hard, matter-of-fact
tone, and Van Biene on being asked what he thought,
of the young Hungarian, said: “Ah! if I had his left
band and he had some of my grease, my fried-fish
feeling,’ we would both be fine performers The
Jewish fried-fish feeling in Van Biene s touch on the
instrument made him second to none as an inter-
preter of sentimental melodies. There was much
pathos and passion, too, in his voice and broken Eng-
lish accent. Hard to say at this distance of time
whv his melodrama fell so flat in Australia eight
years ago. It was much less foolish than many ot
the dramas we get nowadays, and his company in-
cluded at least one forcible actress —Miss Mane Ilig-
nold. But Van B. and his unique entertainment were
a failure.
The Repertory Theatre Club (Melb.) will be ex-
pected to rally to the support of Intellectual Drama
on Friday evening this week, at the Austral Salon.
Gregan McMahons circular convening the annual
meeting states that “to ensure a continuance of the
work of the Theatre and its Club, it is essential that
the club membership shall be not less than 400.”
Therefore existing members, in addition to re-
enrolling themselves, must drag recruits into the
Intellectual Light Brigade. There should be no
great trouble in making up the minimum number.
The Charge of the Gallant Four Hundred is only
£1 Is. a year.
“R. 8.”: N.S.W. Government’s proposed Conserva-
torium of Music should rake the world for the best
musician securable as its principal; and before it
has nailed him to the job, it should discover whether
he is merely a good musician or a musician hoppled
by literary crankiness. The training of musical
students is in many cities obscured by the inclusion
of papers on Schopenhauer and Metsche, with lec-
tures on the rationalism of atheism and the foolish-
ness of the marriage ceremony—things which have
nothing to do with the industry of semiquavers. Art
is lyrical, anyhow, and this dragging in of studies
of philosophers, and critics of conduct, and more or
less lunatic exponents of social anarchy is mere pea-
cockery and waste of the valuable time of youth.
“K. 0.”: The least satisfactory super in Asche’s
impressive “Antony and Cleopatra” show is one of
the two buck niggers whom he brought with his
“Kismet” outfit. All the silent, colored figures in
Cleopatra’s palace wear an air of Egyptian calm save
this bona-fide blackfellow, who looks as though he
wanted to be restless, and is apt to grin at the girls
in his vicinity. The girls give him no obvious en-
couragement to play the Irving Sayles, but he grins
all the same. . Meanwhile his mate on the other side
of the doorway looks as miserable as a bandicoot.

some picture show that has toured Victoria. In the
country districts it draws Home Rule advocates from
miles around, and every ardent Mickey brings a pill-
box with a view to purloining a few grains of the
Irish soil (duly certificated) that is spread between
the pay-boxes and the doors. They have to buy a
ticket, begorra, before they can stand on the ould
sod. V

“Stargazer”: J. C. Williamson, just back from
Europe, admits grudgingly that the one-act play is
enjoying a vogue in London; but, says he, “I am
convinced the people of Australia do not want a
curtain-raiser. They want rather a play long enough
to supply ,a whole evening’s entertainment.” Well,
why not give it to them? Many of the plays put
on here are not a fair evening’s entertainment; but,
instead of giving an additional little play, the
generous managements allow their patrons intervals
so long that many of us forget what has happened
in Act 1 before Act 2 comes along.
“Constant Writer”: Re G. R. Sims’s idea that
London theatres will have to compete with the music-
halls by giving two shows a night (B. 23/l/’l3),
Joe Waldron, chief of The Waldrons, who have made
a couple of tours in burlesque sketches on Australian
variety circuits, told me when he was last in Sydney,
some months ago, that he believed he was the only
man who had regularly put on pantomime twice a
night. This annual outbreak was well received in
Lancashire (his chief hunting-ground), especially as
Joseph has a luxuriant touch of Lancs, in his voice
and some taste in matters of figure and such-like.
But the change would be very hard on the actor.
That point is illustrated by the joy (that word’s iov,
not jag) with which vaudeville people from the U.S.
greet the fact that in Australia they’ve only to appear
eight times a week. At some American variety houses
it’s not a case of two shows a night, but of as many
shows as can be packed into a day and night, with
Sundays even more strenuous than the rest of the
week. It’s the land of perpetual grease-paint. The
change to sufficient leisure for occasional surf-bathing
or what not (especially what not) has JSiade many an
American vaudevillain compare this land to the place
Little Eva went to.
When chorus girls go forth to fight, as seen
in. the panto.
“Rosna” (in London) : A. E. Martin, sometime the
cheerful “Petramos” Of many Australian press contri-
butions, has developed into the most notable animal-
hunter in Europe. Spurred on by the success of his
friend, Beaumont Smith of “Tiny Town,” he has been
hustling all over the continent, applying the education
test to animals, and has got together a show that con-
tains nearly every beast on four legs, except a trained
rocking-horse and a performing nightmare. There is
a sharpshooting monkey, a dog that can tell the inner-
most thoughts of a politician, a donkey with a college
education, and a troupe of melodrama dogs playing
“The Bloodstained Hound.” Also, there is a handful
of giants, including a little girl of 16, who didn t
give up playing with dolls till she was 7ft. high, and
a ,dusky young gentleman of 29 years and 221 b. The
show is to open in Adelaide at the end of February.
Saharet, the famous dancer, always described as
Australian, recently got a divorce from an American
husband Saharet really is Australian, though she left
Melbourne at 16, long before her toes had learnt to
twinkle.
“T. Tintack”: “C.0.M.” (Cove Obviously Mud-
dled) has evidently got the Combine microbe badly.
Why should a big film Combine or Trust desire to
squeeze out of business the suburban picture shows,
which are its consistent customers for films? Why
it should not do so would seem so obvious that
even a frantic devotee of small production and petty
back-street trading methods ought to tumble to it
right away. “C.0.M.” and his like are invited to
consider the position in U.S.A., where the small
picture shows exist by :the thousand, notwithstanding
that a big Trust amalgamation controls both pro-
duction and the supply of their programmes.
“B.”: “Bookware” (B. 9/1/T3) writes that
“Dryden” is the only dramatist who has challenged
Shakspeare and lived”; and “All for Love” is not
such a drop from “Antony and Cleopatra” as one
would expect. Figs! What does “Bookware” call
living? When was “All for Love,” or any other
tragedy, or any unspeakable comedy, of Drvden’s
last enacted on a stage? Dryden challenged Shale -
speare’s “Antony and Cleopatra” about as much as
Colley Cibber challenged the great Elizabethan’s
“Richard III.” with his interpolated mongrel ver-
sion. Dryden seems to have been somewhat fond of
“challenging” Shakspeare. He and Sir
Davenant (Shakspeare’s putative wrongly-begotten
son) “mended” “The Tempest,” giving Caliban a sis-
ter-monster, Sycorax; and Miranda, who, in Shak-
speare’s play, had never seen a man, is made
acquainted in Dryden and Davenant’s “improvement”
with a man who had never seen a woman. Dryden
“challenged” Shakspeare also by altering the great
dramatist’s “Troilus and Cressida,” making the last
scene in the third act thereby (in the opinion of
some) a vastly greater masterpiece. But Dryden, in
his coarse experiments, went further. He made an
“opera” of Milton’s “Paradise Lost,” and thereby
degraded the noblest epic to which the muse of Eng-
land can lay claim. Truth to tell, Dryden was not
a poet or a dramatist. He was a man of letters of
the school of Pope, Swift, Johnson, etc., and the
greatest of them all. His dramas are gropings after
expression, and many are the rhymed couplets. His
comedies are frankly indecent. His verse is the verse
of the school of Boileau ; and his poems (so-called)
are metrical exercises. Dryden did, however, teach
F.nglishmen how to write good, strong, nervous Eng-
lish prose. He set a style, and it was a good style.
But challenge Shakspeare!
“Kavlee”: I find that both Lauder and Wilkie Bard,
London’s pet humorists, are better actors than come-
dians in the sense that the music-halls use the word.
Lauder is a remarkable Scotch character-actor, and
might have stepped right out of a Barrie yarn. Bard
is a quiet, impressive “character” man, who gives a
picture of a night watchman that is quite probable.
The audiences don’t burst their weslcit buttons with
mirth while they’re on the stage, but make a great
noise when they threaten to leave it. Lauder and
Wilkie Bard are at the top of the tree, and it speaks
something for the Cockneys who are keeping them
there.

The negro mummer who told the press, the
other day, that Australia would be all the
better with 100,000 Afro-Americans should
go to schodl to the intellectuals of his own
race. So admittedly unsuited are the blacks
to a Caucasian environment that their own
leaders of thought are moving the heaven
and earth of their financial power to get the
race out of America and re-established in
Africa.
In a brief biography of Premier Massey,
of Maoriland, the Daily Mail Year Book
for 1913 puts it this way:—
He is not distinguished for tactics, nor even for
tact; but shrewd common-sense, a happy gift of
speech, and an unblemished honor make him a
worthy successor to the Fitzgeralds, Rollestons and
Atkinsons, who guided the colony’s affairs in past
days.
And this is what that great Democrat,
George Grey, had to say about the Atkinson
crowd: —
The continuous Ministry, which for years held
office, robbed the people of the Country of their lands,
rendered women and children landless, perverted a
great estate given to Maoriland by a riiunificent
nation, squandered the revenues of the country, and
incurred great debts to give value to the property of
their friends taken from the people.
The Daily Mail Year Book no doubt
meant to be kind; still, in view of Hon
Grey’s description, it ought to hurriedly
withdraw before the bees start to swarm.
Thus E. B. Tredwen, chairman of the
Australasian section of the London Chamber
of Commerce, giving evidence about the Ab-
sentee Tax before the Dominion’s Royal
Commission: —
I think it will be recognised as fair that income
from undertakings that receive the benefit of the pro-
tection afforded by the laws of the country where
they are situated, should make some contribution to
the Government, and that individuals receiving the
benefit of the laws for the protection of their persons
should also make a contribution ; but I am of opinion
that it is both unfair and injurious for the contribu-
tion to be as high as if the person were receiving
both benefits mentioned above simultaneously in each
country.
But the absentee who “receives the coun-
try’s protection for his undertakings, isn t
around to help the country to protect his
undertakings when they most nee “ pro-
tecting—viz. T in time of war. Then the non-
absentee has to carry the whole swag of
fight and horrors. Even considered as
payment in lieu of military service, the
Absentee Tax is thus entitled to a fair run.
“BULLS AND BEARS.”
Lines written for The Bulletin by a “bull'’ of J,
D. Williams shares at 21s. forward.]
Farewell! farewell! to this cold world !
Let salt tears dim the eye.
Unto the shambles am I hurled;
The time has come to die.
I went into the picture show.
I saw the cowboy play,
The schoolmarm from Noo Yark who killed
An Indian every day,
The film of domesticity,
The bride who lost her ring,
And Foolshead drifting in the sea,
And airships on the wing.
But, best of all, the film that showed
The burglar’s money sense,
The highway man upon the road,
The trick of confidence,
The splendor of the footpad’s theft,
The murder weird and dree,
And men of moral sense bereft,
And glorious piracy.
And then I from the flickergraph
Sped to the Stock Exchange,
And bought me many Williams shares
At prices weird and strange.
Of “forward” scrip I soon was full,
Ere westward sank the sun,
An unsophisticated “bull,”
1 bought at one pound one 1
And now the slump is here, I know.
That they were most unfair
Who said I was unfit to take
Intestines to a bear.
For now I see that all those films
But illustrated me;
I bought some shares from burglars, some
From uncles in Fiji.
I was the sport of highwaymen.
The spoil of them that range
The Pitt-strcet bush, and buccaneer
Upon the Stock Exchange.
I was the Foolshead of the show;
The saphead and the squid ;
Blackbeard the pirate took my watch ,
I nurtured Captain Kidd.
But, ere I die, one thing I’ve proved—
As one who greatly dares —
That T’m a “bull” who’s fit to take
Intestines to the “bears.”
Farewell, farewell, to this cold earth,
Ere sinks the westering sun ;
Stranger for ever I to mirth —
I bought at one pound one.
N.S.W.
MARTIN LUTHER.
THE DEATHLESS “STAR.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She’s featured in the magazines;
Likewise the illustrated press;
She is the “star” that bags the beans ;
She is the longest-boomed success ;
She’s proved her great artistic scope
Since she appeared with “Someone’s Soap.”
You find her photographed afar;
On hoarding she has pride of place;
She brightens up the railway car,
Three-colored in her fluffs and lace —
She gets the spot-light good and fair
In Jobkin’s “Bug Juice for the Hair.”
Though “stars” may come and “leads” may go,
The actress of the poster ad.
Will last as long as joy and woe
(Perhaps in years a myriad)—
She is eternal on the bills,
The heroine of “Saved by Pills.”
N.S.W.
BILLY B.
ARTHUR LLOYD WINDSOR.
Arthur Lloyd Windsor, for 28 years edi-
tor of Melbourne Age, had the grim reputa-
tion of being a woman hater. His tiny circle
of intimates know that he was nothing of the
kind. He was a man who hated nobody ; but
he wanted nobody. So long as he had his
work and his books he was satisfied. David
Syme, his chief and closest friend, used to
say that no man needed an encyclopaedia when
Windsor was about; as a matter of fact, he
was more broadly and more accurately in-
formed than any book. He had a memory
that was almost superhuman; his literary
appetite was insatiable, and he browsed over
wide pastures. People were to him interest-
ing only in the aggregate; but he had a fine
old-fashioned courtesy for good things and
beautiful; and he reverenced good women and
children. In the last years of his long
life he was out of action owing to shattered
nerves, and his inability to work fretted his
fine spirit and drove him deeper into himself.
He became moody, unsociable and more self-
contained than ever. During that last sad
period, a few little children were his only
friends. Every day he used to wander down
to a seat in Boyal Park and sit there for-
hours. At first he had two child friends
then a whole bevy of youngsters began to look:
on him as their particular chum. He got
happiness out of this; but if- outsiders wan-
dered near, he became uneasv and self-con-
scious, and the charmed circle was broken.
Acquaintances who incautiously approached
were sharply driven off.
He lived all his life unmarried, a niece
keeping house for him. Work was his one
and only love. Syme wanted to pay him
more. “I don’t leant any more,” said Wind-
sor. “I have enough money, and I’ve the
work!” So he continued to burn himself up.
If his paper could have offered him an in-
creased capacity for toil he would have
jumped at the offer.
Dr. Charles Pearson was associated with
him as leader writer; but it was the tireless
editor who wrote Saturday’s leading article.
Striking, vivid, arresting masterpieces they
were, acknowledged everywhere among the
literary men of Australia as a giant’s work.
In the early ’Bo’s, when partv politics meant
party war, he was as a big gun thundering.
During this white-hot period, a large squat-
ter of the Riverina paid Windsor a
towering compliment. The Argus was this
man’s meat and drink, while the Age was
poison to him. But he admitted that he read
his Age for the “tonic of the Saturday lead-
ing article.” “I have the paper sent under
cover,” he said. “I read that article and I
destroy the journal. No one else oh my sta-
tion is allowed to see it. I abhor the -thing !
I will trust no other man to read it I”
Windsor, the quick-brained, was not a mis-
anthrope ; he didn’t lose heart because re-
forms did not arrive in a day, but philosophi-
cally put everything down to the slow work-
ing of evolution, and strove on. He had
studied Man from the misty beginning, and
he and David Syme often waded out into
controversial deeps and fought toe to- toe
where beings of lesser mental stature .would
have been merely “found drowned.” The
two were bosom friends, and the paper’s weal
was the chief concern of both. In those days
big questions needed no conference of part-
ners ; Syme and Windsor just shut them-
selves in together and thrashed it out. From
those momentous two-man conferences every-
thing of importance emanated, and the deci-
sions thus arrived at “went” everyw-here.
Windsor, at one time, allowed his beloved
Origin of Species theory to leak into his
articles ; but the populace would not stand
for it, so Windsor turned it off. He had
qualified for a doctor, and had his M.D. de-
gree. He was a ruddy-haired, broad-shoul-
dered man, not tall, but muscularly heavy
and with particularly fine, sensitive hands..
He would possibly have made a fine physi-
cian, though his habit of telling the whole-
truth very lucidly might have shocked sham ;
patients off his books.
When his health broke, he went wander--
ing abroad; but, back again in Melbourne.,
he haunted the office like a distressed ghost,,
and worked intermittently until he had got
the ultimate column out of himself. Pos-
sibly in all his life in Melbourne his friends:
numbered no more than six or seven all!
told; but that was only because he did not
seek friends. The few who knew him well
found him most human and genial. One
man in particular looks back on a suc-
cession of midnight suppers. _ When the
paper had been put to bed, Windsor would
collect his friends and they would wander
across the road to old Guusler’s Caf6; then
Windsor, off duty and with the satisfying
feeling that he had done his share, would
uncork his sparkling intellect. The mem-
ories of those talks make up a treasury of
golden common sense for the lucky com-
panion of the table.

HIS ONLY S ISTER: " You do say such absurd
things , Henry! When that Barker woman
asked you if you had any sisters you said:
‘ Oh , yes, a long string.’ Whatever did
you mean? ”
When the Good Australian rewards go
round there will be a few mostly-overlooked
women due for a share. So far. about the
only limelight they have had is thrown by
Statist Knibbs’s latest Census Bulletin. In
the section dealing with wives who are
mothers of Australian citizens, 49 are quoted
as having had families numbering 21 and
over; there are 27 with 20 children ; 49 with
19 children; 130 with 18; 271 with 17; 674
with 16; 1380 with 15; 2743 with 14; 5358
with 13; 9329 with 12; 13,478 with 11 ; and
20.229 with 10. Of those women who gave
birth to 21 or more, 14 were born in Eng-
land, 11 in N. S. Wales, six in Victoria,
four in Tasmania, four in Ireland, two in
Scotland, and two in Germany. English-
born wives headed the list witli families of
20. There were six of them. Vic-
toria was next with five. Of those with
19 and 18 children to their credit, England
again takes pride of birthplace, with 20 and
31 respectively. N. S. Wales natives top the
list in families of 17, 16, 15, 14, 13, 12, 11
and 10. The sad feature of the table is the
great number of women who have borne only
one child. There were 105,019 of them—the
highest number of all. Next comes 104,253
women with accredited births of two chil-
dren ; and, of a total of 733,773 wives,
89,064 had rocked no cradle. However, there
are a heap of these to whom the Statute of
Limitations doesn’t yet apply.
The Melbourne Board of Works controls
all _ the sources of water supply, some of
which are in the Fernshaw and Healesville
district, about 30 miles east of the city. In
1901 Queen Mary visited the locality, and
was more or less jubilant to find that a part
was named Marysville. Now she has sent
out a number of acorns to be planted for
oaks at Fernshaw, not far from the sacred
soot. At the last meeting of the Board of
Works a request came from the local Tour-
ist and Progress Association for £6O to be
spent on a kiosk where the royal oaks are
to be planted. A gruff member protested
that the kiosk and oaks would attract
swarms of snobs, who would probably foul
the water supply. Whereat a loyal member
groaned, writhed and exclaimed, almost
wringing his hands : “Oh, dear ! What would
her Majesty say to this?” Against such an
appeal as that, the suggestion that the water
supply of the city might be fouled had, of
course, no weight, and the £6O was voted
by an overwhelming majority of toads.

The Semaphore anchorage off Port Ade-
laide has “laid” an uneasy ghost at last—
the wreck of the Norma. At least one other
ship has piled its bones on hers, and an
uneasiness about fouling them is held re-
sponsible for one or two collisions, including
that which recently carried away the screw-
pile light, and two valuable lives. The
Marine Board of S.A. has somewhat tardily
dragged and dynamited, till now it can guar-
antee that only fragments remain, with 30ft.
of water above them.
“Briner” : An idea sprang into my mind
this week through seeing Belgian naval
cadets in Melbourne on their way round
the globe for practice. The
Belgian Navy is really a
one-oar’s affair compared
with what the Australian
Navy already is, and still
less compared with what it
will be within a year. So it
will soon be up to us to send
a training ship round the
world, partly to show Tokio,
Hamburg, San Francisco
and London what our naval
cadets are like. It will be
a little stroke for us, just
like the big one made by the
IJ.S. in sending battleships
our way. The Japanese
were very careful to pack a
cadet ship off to Australia
as soon as they were able to
manage it. And as soon as
they could trust an All-
Japanese ship to go round
the world, they sent the
Shilcishama to London,
where it made an enormous
sensation. Even the Rus-
sians, during the tension of
1878, sent the Vestnik, Afri-
ka and Plastoun to Aus-
tralia. . There is more
reason, in' one way, why we
should do the same thing.
We must get young Aus-
tralia to look more to the
sea.. and the prospect of a
cruise to foreign countries
will be an inducement extra
to those mentioned in the advts.
The British India S. N. Co., which follows
the P. and O. Co. as the biggest British firm
trading to Asiatic ports, has purchased the
Currie fleet of steamers. These are among
the largest on the Australian register, and
are husky competitors of the B. I. Co., especi-
ally in the jute trade. The absorption is a
distinct move forward by the 8.1. people
towards the domination of Indian cargo
trade. Archibald Currie, the founder of the
line that bears his name, was chairman of
the Victorian Marine Board for many years.

The house-chaser leads a strenuous life
these times. A metropolitan landlord Was
grabbed by an anxious stranger the other
day. "1 want that cottage of yours in Blank
street.” gasped the grabber. "But it’s let to
Dash,” replied the capitalist. "I know,” said
the applicant; “but lie’s just murdered his
wife in the back-room, and been arrested for
murder!” It transpired later that the home-
seeker was only 30 minutes behind the tra-
gedy, and had beaten two others by but 10
and 15 minutes respectively.

. ” : The King Edward memorial statue
is to be put at the Macquarie-street entrance
to Hyde lark, Sydney, so that we may have
a little family party there. Ardent loyalists
may be interested to know that the spot on
which the Queen Victoria statue stands was
occupied in 1788 by a tree with stout limbs,
and from one of those limbs was dangled the
hrst victim of capital punishment in N.S.W
ihe late Dr. Ilouison and other archive-
searchers fix that as the spot where the
youth Barrett was hanged by the Governor’s
order for having stolen a loaf of bread. While
the pedestal of this Queen Victoria statue
was being placed in position, there lay in
Darlinghurst Gaol a prisoner named Johns,
whose death sentence for attempted murder
was ratified by Loftus, another Governor.
1 lie mother and sister of the convicted man
came to Sydney to plead for a reprieve, but
were unsuccessful. They excited a deal of
popular sympathy, however, and at an open-
aii meeting on the evening before the execu-
tion, the mother of Johns was raised from
the ground by several strong arms and placed
standing on the pedestal. Erom that posi-
tion she made her last vain appeal- Thus
the figure of Queen Victoria was not the first
temale form to crown the pedestal.

LETTER FROM A SELF-MADE SINNER TO HIS
SON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gloom and ennui are the bitterest foes of
Mankind, and against them he constantly strives ;
Fain would I teach you how best to dispose of
The days and the minutes which make up our lives.
It isn t good form to be too pessimistic
(Form should be perfect, though morals be lax) ;
Slay each dull moment by methods artistic,
Never kill time with a club or an axe.
Heed not the voice of the dullard who proses,
Preaching the doctrine that pleasure’s a crime ;
If you must break the commandments of Moses,
See that you only break one at a time.
Strut while you may on the stage of existence,
Carefully posing and playing your part,
Be an apostle of dogged persistence
Rather than slave to the canons of art.
Art is as long as a lane with no turning.
Life, which is short as a ballet girl’s skirt,
Chiefly consists of unsatisfied yearning,
Yearning which harrows the soul to its hurt.
Life is a garden which Death’s hand encloses;
In it fair blossoms in clusters are hung;
Stretch forth both hands, then, and gather its roses
While they are blushing, and while you are young.
Youth can expand like the blossoms and flourish,
Strong in the strength of its powers still divine;
Age craves for viands which strengthen and nourish,
Youth can exist upon kisses and wine.
Empty the cup while the rich wine is creaming,
Time will despoil you of all but the lees ;
Age is a season of fatuous dreaming,
Fate locks the treasures, but youth holds the keys.
When on Eve’s daughters you lavish caresses,
Never let one of them make you her thrall,
Binding you fast to her side with her tresses —
Safety’s in numbers ; make love to them all.
Hearts are but toys, to be broken in pieces,
Girls were invented to flirt with, and so
Never depend on one woman’s caprices,
Always have two or three strings to your bow.
Sip every pleasure, but keep your head level.
Let not one passion your senses enmesh ;
Have an entente cordiale with the Devil,
Keep on good terms with the world and the flesh.
N.S.W.
IRONBARK.
A DAZZLING CAREER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fiis promotion was very rapid. He had only been
in the police force six years when he was raised to
the rank of senior constable. Eight vears later he
was made sergeant. —News item.
Great Scot! To what gay, giddy heights
A constable may climb !
To what ineffable delights
He may attain —in time!
When once a John begins to rise—
Begins to soar and soar—-
He 11 look with absolute surprise
On what he was before.
When once a member of the force
Starts on the upward track,
If he’s a man of keen resource
You’ll never hold him back.
If he’s the right and proper type
Of constable, in ten
Or fifteen years he’ll get a stripe—
You cannot hold him then.
His luck is in: some burglar chap
Will shoot him in the ribs.
And then behold the lucky trap !
Behold liis honored Nibs!
A senior constable js he
With two stripes on his arm !
But eight more years and he may be
A sergeant stout .and calm !
Another dozen years or so
And (if he still survives)
Another step he’ll climb ; and lo!
The hunting-crop' arrives !
A sub-inspector sterji and chaste
The young hedge ,cop affrights,
But many die before they taste
Promotion’s swift delights.
TOM BEASLEY.

“Bloggs” on meaningless gifts:—
One of the most admirable features of the Chelms-
ford regime was the refusal, last week, of the lady
member of the firm to accept any sort of farewell
presentation. If this sets a fashion, an anomaly will
have been removed from our social life. The presen-
tation habit is one of the most idiotically paradoxical
developments on record. Bank-manager Brown, say,
is being transferred from Dustyton to Mudvil. He
is not hard up. He needs no public endorsement of
the facts that he has behaved himself in Dustyton,
and made the average number of friends. Yet to
such a pitch has the presentation custom or insanity
arrived, that if his friends (and others) were not
blackmailed to give him a silver tea-service or a gold
watch, or a dressing-case, it would be generally
considered that he had been a failure. 1 spent six
months in a backblocks township once. During that
period the following achieved presentations:—
(1) A pub-keeper, on leaving to take charge of a
Sydney pub.
(2) A tobacconist, who, having gone insolvent as
a result of love of beer and hatred of toil, decided to
start as a bookmaker at pony race-meetings.
(3) A farmer, who had a big win at Randwick, and
about the same time sold his place at a high figure,
and thereupon decided to chuck work for life.
(4) A priest steering for another vineyard.
(5) The Golf Club professional who, being gravely
suspected of stealing members’ small change, etc.,
had been privately requested to move on.
An inevitable feature of all these ceremonies is that
the less the recipient needs the more he gets. Thus, a
Governor’s wife, with a private income of her own,
would probably be tendered a string of pearls, while
a popular bankrupt would be lucky if he got away
with a pawnable walking-stick.
While the owners of factories and work-
shops have been obliged by law to provide
their employees with a healthy amount of
breathing space, the clerical work-person has
had no such protection. The man who runs
an office can still dump his clerks, male and
female, into any old cellar, supplied with
nothing but artificial light. Little pokey
rooms, with insufficient space in which to
swing a cat, can therefore be seen any day in
any Australian city crammed with clerks.
The result is that nervous breakdown is com-
mon among this class of worker, especially
those of the female variety, and the trouble
is increasing so much that it has become a
national danger. Nervous breakdown on the
part of office workers supplied Sydney Hospi-
tal alone last year with 197 indoor cases and
600 outdoor patients; and it is safe to say
for every case that went to a public hospital,
at least a dozen were treated privately. The
obvious remedy is to subject offices to exactly
the same legislation as. factories and work-
rooms. The clerks, especially the female
portion of them, are notoriously unorganised,
and are not members of a strong fighting
union which can butt into an election and
play a big part. So they have been left. And
unless they organise, it looks as though they
will continue to be left.

Df the second batch of 33 Australian
youths just admitted to the Military College
at Duntroon, eight are from N.S.W., three
each from Tassy and South Aus.; one from
Bananaland ; and no less than 18 from Vic-
toria. Westralia, which supplies none at all,
is, in most things, good Australian —there is
none better—but wages are high and work
plentiful in that State; and a “military
career evidently makes no appeal yet to
the local youngster.
According to the last census, the oldest
married woman in Australia was something
between 95 and 99. Her husband was JT
or less. Sixteen wives whose ages are be-
tween 90 and 94 have husbands under 84.
At the .other end of the proceedings, two
wives of 16 have husbands over 50, and one
wife between 25 and 29 has a husband over
90. Husbands and wives who live to ex-
treme old age do not figure among those who
have a phenomenal number of children; blit
one husband and two wives whose ages are
less than 44 have 21 children or over. And
four girls of 17 have made a fair start to put
up the same kind of record by already ac-
cumulating three children each.
Melbourne Labor Council protests against
Defence Minister Pearce allowing the cadets
of alleged superior schools, like the Mel-
bourne Church of England, Scotch and Wes-
ley colleges, to have their own drills reckoned
as officially good. There is a nice point here,
and one which must be settled definitely if
the Australian citizen army is to be the real
thing. Of course Mrs. Toorak objects to her
Percy drilling with common boys, and even
Mrs. Smith feels strongly, on the subject of
Johnny associating, even in the ranks, with
Billy Jones ; but it will be a poor business
if the prejudices of these good ladies are al-
lowed to create a system of caste which will
differentiate corps from corps and regiment
from regiment. Mrs. Toorak would natur-
ally like Percy to be an officer, but poor
Percy hasn’t any brains to speak of and can t
be ; and, indeed, it is reasonably probable that
Billy Jones may yet issue orders to both
Johnny and Percy. This being so, Mrs.
Toorak’s only refuge for her nice boy who is
not to mix with common Australian citizens
would be a regiment composed entirelv ot
nice boys, which would drill, train and fight
all on its superior own. Existing legislation
intended nothing of this kind; but Mrs.
Toorak manages somehow to secure a big
pull socially on stout Labor democrats when
they get into office, and that social pull may
vet make our defence system a weaker thi g
than it is. It is up to Pearce to mention
the fact firmly, but in a gentle voice, that
Percy and all his kind must step into the
rank's and serve their country exactly as
Johnny and Billy do. There is no hardship
in this The Australian citizen army will
have a levelling influence but under ge spur
of wholesome patriotism it wdlbeaevelhng
nn Percy’s superiority will not suiter, n
there is any of the article in his composition,
but humbug will receive little consideration,
and Mrs Toorak may yet be astonished to
tod £ a manly lad Percy realU is since
he took his place in Billy squad. #

There is a young man at Geelong who
ought to take a Tatt.’s ticket or a wife while
his luck is'in. Driving a couple of horses
and a waggonette along a country road on a
dark night, he suddenly met one of Fitz-
patrick’s trains doing a 38-mile-an-hour
sprint. It hit the turn-out, killed both horses,
smashed the waggonette to splinters, and
gave the surprised young man a joy-ride for
60 yards on the cowcatcher of the engine.
And all he got besides was one small scratch.
“A.J.C.,” who has just been through the
mill: —
The Child Study Association waited on Premier
McGowen, of N.S.W., the other day, and pointed
out to him that it was increasingly difficult to get
good milk for infants in Sydney. The usual vague
promises were, made in reply. But isn’t it time
this alleged Socialistic Government of N. S. Wales
did something to prove that it really has some
claim to the title? Private Enterprise may be
able to supply good milk for infants, as the Tele-
graph asserts, but it certainly doesn t do so in the
mass. The pasteurised milk of commerce is unfit
for infantile consumption, and the iced milk of the
carts is as bad. What is required is fresh dairy milk
from healthy cows, produced under special condi-
tions. It is” all very well for a reporter to scour
the city and write that he found at least one
dairyman who could supply such milk. The average
man has absolutely no hope of getting in touch
witn sucn dairies, if they exist. Thev do not adver-
tise, and there is no bureau of information which
will tell anxious parents where such milk can be
obtained. Not only ought there to be an adequate
s , 1 .ply of such miik, but the sources from which it
can be obtained should be as easy to find as the
post office. The consequent saving of child life
would be immense; and it is more logical for the
State to spend thousands of pounds in starting such
dairy farms and distributing agencies than to splash
it on importing more or less tainted armies ot
immigrant’s. Of course the best food for an infant
is that supplied by the mother ; but there are, unfor-
tunately, thousands of mothers who are unable to
meet the need. Under existing circumstances the
babes of those mothers are seriously handicapped,
and if they live, are almost inevitablv doomed to be
inferior physically and mentally to those who have
had the inestimable boon of natural food, lo sup
pfy the next best thing, that State or municipal
dairy is necessary, and must come. #
“Sub Rosa”: The Education Department
of South Aus. is in a ferment. When the
present director assumed office “promotion
by merit” came into force. It s a first-rate
rule, only unfortunately it opens wide the
door for favoritism, or suspected favoritism,
which is almost as upsetting. The teachers
are divided into two classes, those who know
the Director and those who don t. Inose
in the second class rightly or wrongly think
they have small chance of getting out ot the
ruck. When an appointment is made the
usual question put by this class is, Comes
from Moonta, I suppose? rhe Director
came from Moonta. No doubt from the
worry of it all. the present Director is a
wreck, and for his own sake as well as the
Department’s, it is to be hoped the Govern-
ment will put the direction into the hands
of a board of three. That would remove the
present bogey (or whatever it is) of favori-
tism from the premises. If it isn t done,
something is going to break, and that always
means a mess to be cleaned up.
The Old Soldier voices a theory:—
There is an outcry about imported timber bein'*
used at Cockatoo Dock for the fittings of Australian
warships. But why should wooden fittings be used
at all ? We all know that when ships go into action
the wooden linings and bulkheads of the cabins,
together with every other inflammable article in the
ship, from the captain’s furniture to the photo of the
smallest middy’s best girl, will immediately be thrown
overboard. Broadly speaking, wherever wood is
now used metal would do as well, if not better,
since it would be both lighter and stronger—a
serious consideration in warship construction. The
dockyard tradition that cabin-fittings must be made
of wood is a senseless inheritance from the days
of wooden ships and smoothbore guns, whose pro:ec-
tiles were far less destructive than those of to-day.
Down with wood, imported and otherwise, and up
with aluminium and steel!

warehouseman, of George-street, invited us to call

voted it excellent.
Domestic economy, The Bulletin sup-
poses. Still this paper hardly expected its
religious contemporary to be so frank about

The most biting criticism of the new
dejected-kangaroo stamp has come from
within the service. A country postmaster in
N.S.W. was sorting his lettters, and came on
a couple bearing the new stamp. “Ha!” he
whinnied. “Sticking coupons on the letters,
are they !” and he gleefully surcharged each
one twopence.

The Oldest Inhabitant parts with another
friend: —
A house which has stood for 90 years in Elizabeth -
street, at the south-west corner of Hyde Park, is
being levelled to make room for a Mark Foy building.
From 1816 until 1825 Judge Wylde—he was a Judge
of the Supreme Court as constituted before the Char-
ter of Justice —lived in it Probably he built it. This
Wylde had a grant of 11 acres at the extreme end
of Macleav-street, ground originally reserved by Gov-
ernor Phillip for the use of the aborigines. He sold
to J. H. Potts, who was accountant to the Bank of
New South Wales. Hence Potts Point. Wylde was
afterwards Chief Justice at the Cape of Good Hope,
where he died in 1859. At one time Mortimer W.
Lewis, Colonial Architect, resided in the Elizabeth-
street house, and for a great number of years it was
the home of James Wright, a brewer, whose vats were
on the corner (S.E.) of Bathurst and George streets.
If the Australian really has a sense of
humor, some of him must have grinned ironi-
cally recently when EJ. N. Sloman, the new
headmaster whom the Sydney Grammar
School has thought it necessary 'to import
in the good old fashion, was picking a fare-
well chop in London. Among the valedic-
tory bone-pickers were 22 ex-Grammar
School students earning a crust in England,
presumably because their own country
(which includes their own school) hadn’t a
crumb for them. If any humorist of them
all unobtrusively dropped a sardonic chuckle
into the tomato sauce, the situation certainly
provided an excuse.

The Flight
that
Failed
IT was a mild young man that met
Miss Gadit (40 in the shade).
And as a gallant youth he set
About to please the ancient maid.
They twittered for an hour or so.
And then he took her for a row.
They met again by happy chance.
His arm was round her waist this time.
It happened at a little dance.
And so the cuddle was no crime;
But she just grabbed the lad when he
Said, “ Sarah, will you fly with me ? ”
She smiled and softly murmured,.“Nay,
There is no need to fly, my dear.-.
lam an orphan. None can stay
My wishes. We can marry here.”
“ Great Scot! ” he gasped, “you’ve not quite caught
My meaning. I’m an aeronaut.”

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Fritz” : Some of our native insects have
acquired the craze for imports in a marked degree.
For instance, the vine moth used to feed exclusively
on the gum mistletoe, but since the introduction of
the grape vine and Virginian creeper it has left the
mistletoe alone, and now feeds on the other two
plants. One result of the change is that the mistletoe
is spreading rapidly through the Victorian bush —so
rapidly, indeed, that it has been proclaimed a weed
under the Thistle Act.
“Gee-Gee”: Brief for counsel to advise
in re Timothy Finnerty. This was a simple case of
drunkenness. Timothy was found (according to the
evidence for the prosecution) lying across the foot-
path in the main street at Parkes (N.S.W.), “dead
tight.” The season being New Year, the constable
charitably placed Timothy on his feet several times ;
but Timothy was topheavy, and it was apparent that
the only place for Timothy was in His Majesty’s care.
Now, Timothy was in due course called upon to “an-
swer the charge.” He denied absolutely and indig-
nantly the drunkenness, and justified his indignation
by explaining that he was a laborer workin’ out on
Mclntosh the solicitor's farm, and was in the habit
of going to sleep about two o’clock and sleeping all
the afternoon. “Ye see, yer Honor,” he stated, “I
just sat down on the footpath and went to sleep.” The
magistrate, without comment, fined accused £2. Will
counsel for the Abo-liars please advise—
(l) Whether Timothy Finnerty has sufficient
grounds on which to appeal against such conviction ;
and particularly—-
(2) Whether Finnerty would be justified in sub-
mitting his case to the immigration authorities for
publication as an inducement for laborers to come to
this country ; and —
(3) What amount Finnerty should charge the im-
migration authorities for such right of publication ?

“Fritz” : If any rooster is anxious to tell
me something about snake hybrids let him stand
forth and crow. I’m led to make this announce-
ment by the fact that a new edition of serpent has
appeared along the Murray between Swan Hill and
Mildura (Vic.). It has ruddy blotches on the back,
and is from 2ft. to 3ft. long. I’ve classified it as
a black and carpet snake hybrid, pending further
evidence.
“Jam Tin” : Can any accredited Austra-
lian produce a genuine instance of moon-blindness?
1 have heard of several cases from blokes who were
told by coves, but I've never been able to trace the
coves. In one of his books Frank T. Bullen mentions
the affliction, but, despite Bullen’s reputation, I
doubt the narrative. The reason I ask is because
I’m sleeping in the open, and I don’t want to have
my lights doused.
“Leo Lear”: Talking about fences (don't
know that anybody was talking about them, but it’s
all the same), those in which logs were the basic
principle have mostly disappeared under the flame
of bush fires and the flat of rabbit inspectors. The
most picturesque was the chock-and-log, which may
still be seen criss-crossing over the landscape in
parts of the Upper Murray. Another favorite barri-
cade with spars was the dog-leg fence, now extinct
except in deserted corners. But for everyday pur-
poses no stile was more common, where timber was
plentiful, than logs heaped together and piled on
one another. This had the double advantage of
clearing the land and providing a barrier at the
same time. There are places in the Wannon (Vic.)
where fences were made of sods. Into these the
rabbits entered with a shout of joy visible miles out
to sea, until they were blasted out with equal pro-
portions of powder and language. But of all the
constructions that the early comers built, the stone
walls of the volcanic belts are the most lasting, solid
and infernal rabbit residences extant. Built with a
base of 4ft., and sft. 6in. high, at a cost of from £1
to £1 ss. a chain, it now runs the owners into five bob
a chain to pull the thing down, and then £6O or so a
mile to replace with wire netting. Where good tim-
ber was plentiful, the old timers with the tidy
habit favored the post and three railer, another
costly edifice which is not now used. This obstruc-
tion has been known to last 60 years.
* » * *
“S.R.”: The immigrant can settle down
to it—sometimes. Two youthful and inexperienced
Londoners, stranded in Sydney through a blind faith
in immigration agents, last June wandered towards
Israel with their payable property, and then trained
it to Wagga. From Wagga they tramped without
blankets to Yanco, 70-odd miles, doing the pad in
three days. They had one pay to collect, when the
big July rain caused a shortening of hands, and
after silencing the hash-house, their joint capital ran
to a blanket apiece and two spins in the two-up ring.
Then they “joined the troupe,” and “broke down
the River” to get a shed. They had no idea of
what “the troupe,” “breaking down the river,” or
“getting a shed” signified, but they preferred the
whole of them, with their accumulated possibilities,
to hanging round the works, sponging on boardin
house keepers. They tramped, without getting work,
through Kooba, Tubbo, Kerarbury, Benerembah, Brin-
gagee and Wyvern, and back to Narandera, Lockhart
and Corowa. When they did eventually snap a joi»on
a Murray station, they heard complaints regarding
wages, tucker, accommodation, the jackeroo, and
sundry minor details, and they suggested a deputa-
tion as the most likely method of obtaining redress.
Two others, besides themselves, out of a total of
13, lined up, and they were all promptly sack'
They are beside me now, camped waiting for the
fruit, and one of them has so far adapted himself
to his surroundings that he has promised to “hum”
a stamp to post this at the first town we strike.
“Gouger”: The camel’s internal machin-
ery is a marvellous defier of hard times, but it is
not proof against some of the* tucker on the Aus-
tralian tracks. In one camp at Normanhv, ’way out
behind Cooktown (Q.), I saw 36
camels that had died in one night
through eating the new green
leaves of the ironwood tree. Like-
wise, the guttapercha plant is
sudden death to the four-legged
ship of the desert as well as
horses ; and the peach plant is in
the same catalogue. Still, it is
not as deadly as the far-famed
Darling pea. Animals have been
known to recover from the mad-
ness that follows a pea feast, but
horses, anyhow, are seldom any
use afterwards. I reckon the goat
to be the toughest inhabitant of
the continent, but there is a plant
like an ordinary lily up on York
Peninsula that disturbs his works.
A leaf of this vegetable, if crushed
in the hand, produces a juice
which causes instant and violent
burning. Saw a William goat do
some high and lofty tumbling
after chewing a leaf, and, with
terrifying howls, bolt for the near-
est waterhole. Don’t know the
name of the plant, or I’d post
it so that you could add it to the list of undesir-
ables.

“Kim”: Peter called upon the local ser-
geant one day with a complaint that his fence had
been burned down by his neighbor, Hore Maori. The
sergeant accosted Hore thus: “What’s this Peter
come tell me, you burn his fence down, eh ?” “Yes,
I burn his fence down. Next time I burn his blirrv
whare down!” “By jingo, Hore, you go to gaol.”
“No blirrv fear. I tell you why I burn his fence.
Sometimes ago I go ’way. While I ’way Peter he
steal my missus. I no care ’bout the missus—she no
good; but when he get the missus she wearin’ my
daughter’s jacket, and I pay two pound for that
jacket in Oamaru. I go to Peter; I say, ‘Keep te
missus, but give me te jacket.’ Peter say, ‘No.’ So
I burn his blirry fence down. Inogo to gaol.”

“Gouger”: A slimy pest in the Queens-
land scrubs during the rainy months is the leech.
About an inch and a half long, and black in color,
the blood-sucker hangs on to a man’s leg with a
tenacity that would do credit to a bull-hound. They
have literally to be torn from their prey. Lathering
legs and feet with a thick layer of soap is about the
only remedy for the scrub leech, unless you keep
out of the scrub. I’ll do that if anybody will get
me a job in town. Anything’ll do except being an
editor. I couldn’t stand loafing all the time.

“Inchiquin” : Re long journeys by horses.
Owned a flea-bitten half-bred Arab that did some good
trips in his day, and lived to the age of 23. He was
owned previously by two Queensland tank-sinkers.
Every second Tuesday, about 10 p.m., one of them
would start off for the mail, 60 miles away. After
two hours’ spell he would tackle the return journey,
and be between the blankets by midnight of Wednes-
day. I afterwards raced the horse once at Kangaroo
Island, and he could have won easily, but threw his
rider after getting the lead.
“Tiaki” : In Maoriland such trees as the
tawa and the honeysuckle are noted splitters, and, in
many cases will “fly up the back” (i.e., split up the
back “scarf” without breaking across the stump),
and woe betide the man who gets a lift from the fly-
ing timber. He ceases to take any interest in bush
contracts. I knew one poor fellow who was letting
down a drive of trees among which were a number of
tawas. He was standing well in the rear, to be out
of danger, when a slab of white tawa flew out from
among the smashing, grinding mass of timber and
pinned him through the body. It was the odd chance
in a million, and it happened. Another victim lobbed
a long red pine in the fork of a standing hinau. Its
impetus balanced it for a
moment, then the heavy butt
brought it shooting back, and
it neatly cut him in two.
Honeysuckle nearly always
leans up hill, and is influenced
by the wind. It is smooth-
barked, and its branches are
spare; so, if the hill is steep
and it “goes up,” it generally
glides back again like the
Devil’s battering ram. One
poor bushman got nailed to a
stump by one of these back-
sliders, and, although he was
fatally injured, he smoked a
cigarette and made out his
will (in pencil) in the pre-
sence of his mates. “I know
what’ll happen when you cut
off a length,” he said grimly.
“As soon as the air gets to
me I’m gone.” He was right.
I knew one bushie who put
his head in the back scarf of
a tawa to hear if it was crack-
ing. It was, and he idled the
next six months in a hospital.
The most awful death I’ve
met was that of a Maori. He
had sawn a huge kauri tree,
and then wedged it from the
back scarf, but some of the
big timber is so straight that
enough wedges cannot be
“banked” in behind to over-
balance it. Henare had
placed his pipe under the butt
on the stump and gone down
to the camp, which was shift-
ing for a tangi. He went
back to the tree to get his
pipe. Three days later his
absence at the tangi was com-
mented on. Returning to the
bush, his mates found him
dead, with one hand jambed
under the stump. The tree
had “ridden on its stump”
(i.e., tilted sideways a little) just as he was about
to withdraw his pipe. In his efforts to escape he had
gnawed the arm to the bone.
“W’lang”: Re the country policeman (B.
26/12/’l2). There was one in a mining township
in Vic. who had never been known to make an arrest.
One day a man wandered along with snakes in his
hair. Jonnop took pity on him, locked him up,
bought a bottle of brandy for the good of his nerves,
and, when asking the Jay to deal with him, leniently
said he was a decent fellow, but got too much
drink. Discharged with a caution. The Jonnop gave
his late guest some good advice and half a crown.
The late guest was very grateful; and, as he had no
swag and nowhere to sleep, he asked permission to
occupy the same room till next day. Request granted.
The guest gave no trouble: he left early in the
morning, and took the blankets with him. It isn’t
safe to slip in the mud in view of that Jonnop
now.
A FRAUD.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
The guile of humble mendicants
Who beg of you a sprat for bread
Is terrible. So portly gents.
In self-defence have often said.
They give no more, because they think
The penny’s always spent in drink.
’Tis true the beggars lie like fun.
And I am moved indignantly
To tell a simple tale of one
Who worked a shocking fraud on me.
He wrecked .vhat little faith I’d left
In men forlorn, bereaved, bereft.
He came to me, a pallid wretch,
With trembling limb and woeful eye.
He wept a minute at a stretch,
And “Charity” was all his cry.
“For Gorsake, mister, stay and hear,
And spare a bloke a bob for beer!”
For want of beer he’d nearly died.
I gave him coppers, and he ran.
I followed him, and stood and spied
In indignation while the man
To Brown, the baker, slyly sped,
And spent my hard-earned browns on bread !

“Flinderino” : Mrs. I‘ete? Spiller, of Run-
ning Creek, was always fond of children, and as a
child she used to borrow other people’s youngsters
to nurse. AVhen she married Peter the people on the
creek told each other what a happy mother she would
make, and how her mug of joy would slop over when
the stork paid its initial visit- to the Spiller humpy.
However, time passed by and no stork or native
companion showed up. The crestfallen Airs. Spiller
confidentially informed Airs. Jones that Peter re-
fused to consent to a visit from the long-legged bird.
Peter could not entertain the idea until the house
had been paid off, and there were four more years to
go. When the four years were up and the house be-
came their own, Mrs. Spiller brightened up wonder-
fully and became her old self once more. But, alas!
the joys of anticipation were short-lived. AA-ith sad-
ness i:.i her voice and a lump in her throat, she told
Mrs. .’ones that Peter said babies could not yet, be
tlir -ht of, as the house wanted painting first.
“ ! 'iouger”: The blacks of the N.Q. coast
are ■ pert catchers of the dugong, so much prized for
its oil, and fish for them from dug-out canoes. Alostly
the f: king is done at night, for it is by the dis-
turbance in the phosphorescence on the sea that
Bingh: loca, his prey. The canoe silently glides
from shore under the noiseless but skilful handling
of the paddle. In the prow stands a dark shadow-
like figure, armed with a long pole, in the end of
which is a short three-pronged harpoon. To the har-
poon is attached a strongly-plaited cord, which, in
turn, is fixed securely to the canoe. The harpoon and
pole are only loosely spliced together, and tear apart
the moment anything is struck. AVhen right over the
unsuspecting fish, the man in the bow, putting all
his available avoirdupois on his pole, leaps upon
brother dugong. Next instant a dark arm has gripped
the stern of the canoe and the outfit is- being torn
through the water by the pain-maddened fish. But
this part of the performance is merely a matter of
time, and is superseded by the boat crew towing the
erstwhile tow-er. Meanwhile the fires have been
lighted ashore, and when the fishermen arrive with
the bacon (which often weighs scwt. or 6cwt.) the
proceedings are nearly as joyous as on a recent Satur-
day when a hilarious tribe of Sydney aborigines
pushed ’Enley, M.L.A., through his hat” on the old
camping ground of the Governor-General.
THE CERTIFICATE TEST, 1920.
“Stolir Ruddell” : Binghi is the boss, per-
son of a two-up ring, not as a reckless gambler, but
as an expert spinner. To anyone who has never
tried, it looks the simplest of operations to make
two pennies twist furiously in the air; yet fe\
of the thousands of habitual “swi-up” players I
have seen can spin decently without violently
swinging the arm. Binghi takes to it as he takes
to gohanna, using only his wrist. And he loves it.
I have seen him in shearing sheds and on public
works eschew hazards, where there was big money,
to spin for pennies, or for the mere fascination of
“sending ’em up” if no pennies or players were
available. AA hen he does take to it as a profession
he’s a weirdly undetectable “nob flier,” and runs
risks which would end in serious disablement for
AVhite Brother.

“Thomas Butler”: It is quite a mistake
to say that the barramundi frequents only the Daw-
son River. I have caught him in the Palmer River
below Mavtown (N.Q.), and know that he is a resi-
dent of the Mitchell, Walsh, Tate, Lynn, Kennedy
and (I believe) the Laura and Endeavor Rivers.

the fish makes a most delicious dish. I suppose

“The Rook” : The wild tobacco plant can
be one' of the most infernal of our .noxious. weeds
when it likes. Lake Victoria Station, on the Mur-
ray, 50 miles t’other side of AVentworth, has had
thousands of acres rendered useless by this pest.
AVhen I first went there, in 1889, there were only two
small bushes of it (growing at the old Lake Vic-
toria pub, long since demolished), but inside five
years, thousands of acres were covered. No grass
grows amongst it worth mentioning, and nothing
will eat it; and its whiskers are so thick that it
takes a rabbit all his time to get through them.
Don’t know where it originally came from ; some
say sheep brought it from South Aus., but whoever
is responsible ought to have it growing on his
grandmother’s grave.
“Edclyson”: Mailing was one of an
assorted party that went camping near Donna Buang
(Vic.) this Christmas. Mailing is a patient soul
who will spend long, sad, silent hours at the end of
a Me, waiting for fish to come out and bite him,
while others are toying in the shade or climbing
the rugged heights. When so employed on the second
day, Mailing caught a fine fat eel. It was his first
success, and it would not have happened had he not
been fishing for black fish. Mailing took his prize
to. the deserted camp, coiled it on a dish, and put it
in the perforated zinc, safe. swung under a sapling.
Fired with his victory, the angler then voyaged up-
stream, found a nice hole, and sat for five dead hours
in anticipation of further adventures. He returned
to camp, weary of everything but fishing. The camp
was still deserted. It was in a state of great dis-
order. Things were thrown about. The meat safe
was down and out, and looked as if it had been
walked on by elephants and laden camels; but the
most damaged and deteriorated item in sight was
Mailing’s eel. It lay in segments, and every section
was battered into the subsoil. The sticks and stones
with which the assault had been committed lay thick
about. The trap and horses were gone; everybody
was gone. There came no reply to. such inquiries as
Mailing addressed to the desert and the range. In
the absence of eel stew, Mailing did his best with
cold duck, tinned pudding, and asparagus fresh from
the can. Then he smoked and waited and wondered.
On towards midnight ten-twelfths of the camping
party returned in the drag. Mailing gathered a few
important facts from the general flow *of explanation.
Poor Roper had been bitten by a snake—terriblv
bitten. It almost appeared that the snake had got
Roper down, and was worrying him, when Tve got
in his first belt with a large piece of a tree! The
blow damaged Roper somewhat, but was important
inasmuch as it quite disconcerted the snake. Then
■Wells hit the snake five times with a pan, and Bol-
ster contributed serious injuries with an axe. The
rest of the picnic threw, rocks and timber at the by-
this-time wholly discomfited reptile, and then com-
mon attention was turned to Roper, who was writh-
ing in mental and physical anguish. Roper had two
punctures in his thumb. The party recognised that
hasty measures were essential to the saving of a
bright , young life. AVith great- readiness of mind
Bolster offered to cut off the offending thumb there
and then. But Tye and AA’ells counselled less drastic
measures. They pulled a cord ligature about Roper’s
wrist- that nearly amputated his hand, and then ran
the victim of the serpent’s guile nine miles to the
township. Roper ran at the back of the drag. They
gave him all the camp whisky to put him to sleep,
and banged all the camp tin-ware in his ears all the
way to keep him awake. At a wayside farm house
they bought more whisky. Roper was now in the
hands of the township doctor, and the camp was
pleased to inform Mailing that if he did not die of
alcoholic poisoning he would probably live to a
green old age, thanks to the prompt actions of his
friends. “AVhere was the snake?” asked Mailing
“It was in the meat safe,” said Tye. “Here are
some bits of it.” He dug fragments of eel from the
moist earth. Mailing did not enlighten them.* He
did not think it would be judicious. He merely
found out for his own satisfaction that Roper
had jabbed himself with the fork with which he had
intended to prod certain meats—an inadvertence due
to the excitement of discovering a venomous serpent
squirming m the safe.
“A.E.8.W.”: About 20 years ago, when
camped near the old barge which then did duty as
a ferry landing stage at Mosman’s, in Sydney Har-
Lor, a boat party of us witnessed a family disturb-
ance, in comparison with which the commotion
caused by a twin-screw steamer’s propellers ; s a
dead calm. It appeared as though there had been
some dispute over property or a girl, and as the
corroboree got warmer, pieces of what looked like
shell-incrusted garden hose and motor tyres with
clinker adhering to them flew in all directions.
There were no signs of gore; yet when the blue
nose came to the surface of the water it looked as
though he had been through an explosion at a pre-
historic factory. Next day we discovered on the
rocks the virtually legless body of an octopus. It
was about the size and shape of an average man’s
body, and the mouth strongly resembled an abnormal
human navel. (It is nose and mouth combined.)
The eyes, which were located where a man’s breasts
would be, were about as large as an ordinary cup,
and had the AVowser expression. We guessed then
that the shark had been declared the winner on a
knock-out.
