“E.S.S.” takes a snapshot of the way
some of the big estates were built up:—
Jimmy Daylight was a selector, dummying a C.P.
for the boss of the station —the big man’s way of
grabbing a freehold that he couldn’t get bv fair
means. Naturally Jimmy hardly lived on his" selec-
tion at all, merely camping a night in the hut
occasionally. He was kept well posted as to the
movements of the inspector, and it was only when
the official was in the district that Jimmy \vas at
home.
On one occasion the inspector arrived in the neigh-
borhood unexpectedly, and Jimmy had only a few
hours’ notice to get into residence. The place had
been untenanted so long that grass was growing in
the hut, and was knee-rleep all round it. The “cocka-
too” yard wore a deserted appearance. On arrival
Jimmy did some rapid circus tricks around it on
horseback, shut up a mob of cattle to tramp it
down, and penned a lot of strange calves. Then he
built a big fire in the hut to make an ash-heap, and
while this was burning he tidied up inside, cut the
grass away from around the outer walls and drew a
load of wood he didn’t want. He chipped a path
from the door to the yard and another to the water-
hole, and he was trotting a draught horse up and
down to give them a worn appearance when the in-
spector arrived.
Jimmy had hardly breath enough left to say “Good
day”; he had never worked so hard in his life be-
fore. Being caught unawares, he pretended to be
hreaking-in, and said “AA’hoa, boy!’’ soothingly to
the horse whenever it moved, which it did only when
he pinched it or pricked, it.
“Pretty old to he breaking-in, isn’t he?” said the
inspector.
“He was broke in before,’ Jimmy admitted, “but
he’s been running in the bush with the brumbies and
got unmanageable.”
“Been doing a bit of fanning here?” looking at
the cut-up grass along the paths which Jimmy hadn’t
had time to clear away.
“The grass is a nuisance in wet weather, hangin’
over the paths,” Jimmy explained.
“Ye-es,” said the inspector, doubtfullv. “Rather
long since we had any rain, thodgh.”
Jimmy searched the skv for a friendly cloud. “I
was expectin’ some,” he said. “My corns was
troublin’ me terrible this mornin’.”
I’ho visitor inspected Jimmy’s premises with a
suspicious eye, and asked how often he slept there,
and what he worked at. But James knew his
business—which just then was to be a fluent liar.
So, as usual,, all that happened was a caution to be
at home a bit more frequently in future.

‘B. de Burke”.: Mrs. Robarts has been
assuring the Ass. for the Advancement of Science
that while the sight of Black Brother is very keen,
his sense of smell is undeveloped, “as he is able to
sit, apparently with perfect comfort, amid the most
objectionable odors.” A long experience of the nig.
has taught me that his sense of smell is perfect. lie
can stand any kind of smell, from the odor of sanc-
tity upwards, being apparently utterly indifferent to
all varieties. Alore, he can distinguish every separate
smell, if he smells a dozen at once. A black tracker
gets his living by smell as much as by sight; and if
you took one to Footscray he could tell vou after a
week’s smelling-round the source and identity of
every stink. This applies at any rate to Victoria
(northern), N. S. AVales and Queensland blacks.
Also, if you and they are moving towards a corpse,
they can pick up and classify a smell some hundreds
of yards before a white man can detect it. Their in-
difference to stinks I attribute to the fact that they
carry so many about with them. A tracker who can
stand the smell of himself after he has hustled for an
hour on a hot day can put up with any sort of in-
suit to his nostrils.
THE SACRIFICE TO COW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let others sing of war’s alarms;
The heroes of the hour endow
AA ith medals, cheers and limelight charms —
I rise to glorify the Cow.
Some nations pledge their souls to God;
Some kneel before the Sun and bow ;
Some scourge themselves with whip and
We sacrifice our young to Cow.
Not with the knife we end their days ;
Not one swift pain to pale the brow.
No ; ours a creed that, ere it slays,
Gives years-long homage to the Cow.
Long ere the dawn has fringed the sky
Aon boy and girl’s had sleep enow.
Up ! Though the lids cling o’er the eye,
Haste to the Temple of the Cow !
Dark, and the night still finds them there,
Dulled with the service. Still we vow
Children and children, and to spare—-
It pays to sacrifice to Cow!
N.S.W.

W. J. Miles, the extraordinarily pertina-
cious man who has been fighting the Sydney
Meat Preserving Co. through court after
court, and was lately felled by a majority
decision of the High Court, isn’t finished
yet. Mitchell, K.C., of Melbourne, who has
gone to England on the Coal Vend appeal
case, is to ask the Privy Council for leave
to appeal in the Miles matter also.
In the obituaries of Arthur Lloyd Wind-
sor there has been no mention of the ex-AGE
editor s deep-rooted aversion to being asso-
ciated with uneducated, coarse-grained folk
even in public vehicles. The Victorian
champion of Democracy and the Working
Man was exclusive as well as reclusive. His
political sympathies conflicted with his in-
dividual tastes, and he made no concession
to them in his m&nner of life. Wh&t time
he was battling for Labor he lived in Col-
i ins-street, that he might be able to walk to
the office. Tram and train travelling was
almost abominable to him on account of the
people with whom a daily traveller rubs
shoulders. After his retirement, in 1900,
he lived at Park-street. Brunswick, and in
the big open space of lioyal Park he found
the solitude that was congenial to him.

A line from a Water-Crank:—
I put none of Alfred Deakin’s achievements ahead
of Ins services to Australia in the matter of irri
Station. Hugh McColl, of “canawl” fame, converted
the eloquent Deakin when he was a young man
As far back as 1884 Alfred presided over a Commis-
sion on Mater Supply. This created a new interest
in irrigation. He visited semi-arid and arid
America, and set to work to apply to identical condi-
tions identical propositions. He temporarily failed
primarily for lack of “head and water,” the master-
reservoirs in the catchment areas not being built.
A n jmpecuniosity prevailed, notably in brain power!
at the time (late 80’s). And, in the second place,
the distributing channels ran through large estates,
the owners of which were too purse-proud and fat
and too lazy to use the water, and too mean to
paw the water. rates.. Since then, however, closer
settlement and irrigation have been made twins, and
many big estates have thrown in their marbles. All
the water-conservation and d’stributing schemes in
Victoria were really born in 1884, and their present
success and promise of greater success can be traced
to the pioneering of Alfred Deakin.

Melbourne’s first photographer was G. B.
Goodman, who, in August, 1845, had a studio
for daguerreotype in Little Flinders-street.
He guaranteed that the sitting would not
exceed five seconds, and the whole picture
would be finished and delivered in four
minutes. The price of each picture (framed)
was one guinea. After being in business
four months, and taking £B7O in cash, Mr.
Goodman unexpectedly, one summer morning,
packed up his “studio” and left for Adelaide.
N. E. Lewis, whose resignation from the
Tasmanian House of Assembly (with that
of Speaker Davies) precipitated the general
election, was born in Hobart in ’5B. Seven
years previously, Mrs. Humphry Ward, the
novelist, was born in the same old town. In
youth Lewis was sent to the High School
at Hobart. One James Anthony Froude, an
Oxford man in clerical orders, had just pre-
viously been engaged to come out from Eng-
land to be headmaster of the school, but the
principals discovered that Froude had writ-
ten “The Nemesis of Faith,” and his ap-
pointment was cancelled. Lewis went from
the High School to Baliol College, under
the great Jowett. In 1885 he returned to
Tasmania, and in the next year was elected
member for Richmond in the Assembly. The
Lewis family settled in Tasmania in Water-
loo year (1815). N.E. is of the third gener-
ation. His wife is daughter of Charles
Youl, of Symmons Plains, Tas. —of the
family which gave Victoria Dr. Richard
Youl, for about half a century City Coroner
of Melbourne.
“Backblocker’s” reminiscence:—
Mrs. Henry Forsyth, who was drowned on a fish-
ing excursion in M.L., last week, was unique as a
sportswoman. She hunted regularly in Leicester-
shire ; and, on horses which she had bought cheap and
practically made herself, she won a reputation for
nerve and skill that no other Australian has enjoyed
in that hard-riding and hyper-critical part of the
world. She drove as well as she rode—four-in-hand,
tandem, unicorn, any combination of horses that
could be imagined. There were few things she didn’t
know about fly-fishing, though she only took to this
highly technical sport late in life. Incidentally she
wrote a fairly successful novel and was at work on
another when she died. But she was seen at her best
in her own home, near Dudley (N.S.W.). Sir Harry
Rawson, after his first visit there, described it as the
nearest thing to Paradise he had seen in Australia
Situated near the edge of a cliff, one aspect took in
the Pacific Ocean 700 feet below, another a lake and
a_ series of mountains stretching away in the blue
distance. The place had originally been a miner’s
hut. She kept the adobe floor, the flame tree which
grew through the roof of the old edifice, and the
enormous fireplace. Round these she constructed a
house which, while defying all the known laws of
architecture, was, artistically, absolutely satisfying
—for one thing, doubtless, because it harmonised so
perfectly with its environment. The living rooms
radiated off the drawing-room (the only one in the
Commonwealth, probably, with an adult tree in the
midst of it), and the old-fashioned garden pushed it-
self unexpectedly in between them at every turn.
There was a steeplechase course on the grounds and
anyone who felt like a ride before breakfast could
rely on getting a lead over it from his hostess. Alto-
gether she was a remarkable character, the tvne of
daring and absolutely efficient Australian woman
whom one reads about often, but rarely meets.
Dr. Anthony Colling Brownless, who died
at Darlinghurst on January 21, was the
eldest son, by a second marriage, of the late
Amthony Colling Brownless, for years the
picturesque Chancellor of Melbourne Univer-
sity. The son, like the father, married twice;
the second wife was a daughter of Mr. White,
a__ well-known turfite, and his wife, Myra
Kemble, an old Australian stage favorite.
The Tasmanian Speck has been meanly
robbed of the only scrap of distinction that
has come its way for some time. It was to
have been governed by plain Mister Ellison-
Macartney; but, at the last moment, the
new cocked-hat was captured, thrown, and
branded with the well-worn K.C.M.G. iron.
Now, he will graze with the vast chaotic
herd that is pastured in the back paddocks
of Whitaker’s Peerage.
Melbourne is again bereft of the ghostly
comfort of its Anglican Archbishop. Lowther
Clarke and his family went steaming away
to dear old England a week or so back. It
is good to be an archbishop. It seems also
good to be called Lowther: that family looms
large in England wherever privileges and
perks are to be picked up. Even grievous
lack of both eloquence and tact doesn’t seem
to be a disqualification when a Lowther is
out after an archbish’s billet.
“Scribe” conducts a post-mortem :
A. L. Windsor, successively Argus and Age editor,
was born at sea, and so was Schuler, his successor
on the Age. Windsor’s father owned and lived upon
large sugar estates in Barbadoes. At eight years old
Arthur was sent to school in Devonshire When t>e
began to consider life he studied a while for the
Church, but the Old Testament proved too steep. So
he took a classical mastership at Clifton College, and
wrote. Macaulay having praised his work, he published
a book of essays called “Ethica,” which may be found
in the Melbourne Public Library. Then the Argus
advertised in London for an editor. Windsor did not
apply, but a friend obtained the billet for him. An
inexplicable condition was that he never should enter
the Melbourne Club. At the Argus he followed
Watts. His other predecessors were King, Wilson,
Higinbotham, Jarrett and Patmore; his successors
Haddon, Willoughby, Watterson (with Cunningham),
and Cunningham suit's. Age editors have been E.
Syme, G. P. Smith, Harrison, Windsor and Schuler.
Windsor stayed two and a half years with the Argus,
and then retired to the humble pie of the Castlemaine
Mail for three years. Back in Melbourne he wrote
for the Age, and was editor from 1872 to 1900 —28
years of a peculiar kind of brilliant success. He fullv
believed in the principle that journalists are all
of the same religion and politics. What? They
never tell. Windsor always passed for a sort of Tory,
but nobody could nail him to anything. He was a
paradox like Wilkes, who assured King George that
he never was a Wilkesite. His contempt for the cag-
mag of news was sublime. His forte, in which he
was beyond rivalry, was the hitt"r satiric political
or social article. Emphatically he was a writing
editor. Syme was in fact the editor all along, Wind-
sor the grand vizier. Like G. P. Smith and Harri-
son, he had an unerring scent for libel; Syme said
he could go home comfortably and leave the paper in
hands of such men. Yet I am convinced that
Windsor could not have held on longer at the Age
than at the Argus if Syme had not been behind him
tor the practical business part of the job. He was no
more good than James Smith for the current, fact-y
political, up-to-date leading article ; that sort had to
be supplied by such as Pearson, Deakin and Hoare,
Windsor &S rams ’ or by Syme speaking through

The fighting man on the Government side
during the hurried Tasmanian general elec-
tion has been “Teddy” Mulcahy, Minister
of Lands. Teddy was born in Limerick,
which breeds hard fighters, and prophetic-
ally enough he came into this world on the
day the Crimean War began. When he was
four years old he went to Tasmania with
his parents and one sister. In 1891 he first
offered himself for West Hobart, and was
returned at the head of the poll. After the
election he became Minister of Railways
and Mines. Since then he has held various
other offices ; and at odd times he has also
been shipwright, men’s clothier, blacksmith
and boss of a theatre. Also, he sings a good
tenor song and plays the fiddle, and he has
conducted the choir in the old Hobart R.C.
Cathedral.
“P.X.”: Once more I see it
printed of that peerless advertiser,
Sydney Kidman, the “cattle king,”
that he began without a bean as
a rouseabout or something, and
worked _up to the proud eminence
of owning the most country and
cows in Australia. Time the
pretty yarn was dropped. Kid-
man never was poor. In fact, he
was so far from poor that at our
pretty expensive boarding-school,
while we other fellows fingered
shillings, he was the “big pot” who
flashed pound notes.

son ot Colonel Johnston, who commanded
the Rum Corns at the time of the Bligh
rebellion The eldest son of the Colonel,
George Johnston, was in charge of the Gov-
ernment cattle at Brownlow Hill, and was
ufled there. Governor Macquarie ordered
Greenaway, the Government architect, to
piepare a vault on the Annandale estate for
the reception of the remains. Many of the
tamiiy were interred there; but when the
estate was cut up and sold, the contents were
removed to a vault at Waverley.
HIS ATTITUDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“My attitude has always been as it is now.”
—Joe Cook.
One weary time I wagged a pick
In deep, dark mines, and smoke and ate
By miry Tom, and Bill, and Mick
In reeking drives, and with my mate
I punched the coal and gouged the slate.
The hopes behind my dome-like brow
Were then the hopes I cherish now.
They put me into Parliament.
From boots and pants I scraped the clay ;
I donned the garments of a gent.,
And fought the workers’ fight all day,
The wicked hopes of Fat to slay.
My attitude, you will allow,
M as just the same as it is now.
I seek with votes and fiery speech
The great rapacity to feed
Of Property; I strive to teach
The people that their friend indeed
Is old insatiable Greed ;
And what it’s always been, I vow,
My moral attitude is now.
I’m he they lifted from the pit
To help improve the toiler’s case,
And eheek-by-jowl, with Fat I sit,
Endeavoring with brazen face
To keep the worker in his place.
A rigid weathercock, I trow,
Joe always was, and Joe is now!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

This bank is getting a move on. The fact probably
is that it didn’t cut deep enough into its capital after
the crisis, 20 years ago, and still had a lot of over-
valued assets on its hands. In the ordinary
course it would then have to hang on to profits in
order to write those assets down. During the last few
years values have recovered in a very emphatic way ;
and this has doubtless had two results—it has re-
lieved the co. of the need to hang on to profits, and
it has enabled the co. to get rid of old assets without
loss, and put the money into business with more life
in it. Visible profits have practically doubled in
seven years, and the circumstances just mentioned no
doubt explain why. Which is another way of saying
that there is no reason to question the legitimacy of
the present profits because they were only half as big
seven years ago.
The least satisfactory feature of the Bank of Vic-
toria’s accounts for some time past has been the com-
paratively poor holding of liquid assets. Take this
comparison first:—
Liabilities.
Dec.,’ll. Dec.,’l2.
Notes in circulation.. £21,754 £12,430 Dec. £9,3“>4

Premises, etc. .. 377,545 367,378 Dec. 10,167
The bank thus owes the public £7,031,379. Of that
amount only 28 p.c. is liquid. There may be special
circumstances in the case; but, ordinarily, this is
not enough, especially at a time when deposits are
on the downward track and the legitimate call for
advances is certainly not likely to be less. There is a
temptation to lend freely when things seem good ; but
it is poor policy to lend so much that very profit-
able business offered by old customers must be turned
away later on when things don’t seem so good.
But while this bank may be working on too narrow
a margin of cash and liquid assets, its general posi-
tion appears to be strong. It owes the public, as
mentioned just now, £7,031,379. Its own capital
and reserves total £1,805,853. Thus, for every £1
of its own it has no more than £3 17s 6d. of the
public s money—a very low figure. And the lower
the figure the better, of course, the security of the de-
positors and the smaller the shareholders’ risks. On
the other hand, the less public money a bank has to
play with, the smaller naturally would one expect
its earnings to be. In the circumstances, the Victoria
does very well to show these figures relating to
profits earned in proportion to capital and reserves:

The business, one would judge from that, is good •
though, admittedly, the test is not always reliable.
Just before the crisis, for instance, this bank showed
earnings so good that it was paying a 12 p.c. div.
The misfortune then was that the earnings were onlv
on paper.

aiways likely to be prejudiced by the fact that the
bulk of its shares are under par—which those of the
Commercial of Australia are always likely to be until

It is satisfactory to see that though money has
been coming in pretty freely the bank hasn't been
making advances with a lordly hand. Total liabili-
ties to the public are £1,913,272. Coin and liquid
assets total £503,357. The latter is equal to 26 p.c.
of the former. It is still a very poor holding of
liquid assets, though the figures are an improvement
on those of recent terms. Still they show this —-
that on its present resources the bank can’t safely
lend more than it is lending now. And that is not
a good position for any bank to get into. A bank
is certainly under no obligation to lend any client
more money; but the one that can’t or won’t help
one good customer over a tight pinch, is liable to
lose several customers. It isn’t the bank which
helps in good, prosperous times, when money is
cheap, that deserves thanks. It is rather the one
which says no and hangs on to a bit in such times,
so that it may more freely lend a hand in bad ones.
This doesn’t apply particularly to the City of Syd-
ney except, as said just now, that this naper would
like to see it with more than 26 p.c. of its lia-
bilities represented by cash, considering that we are
probably just a little way over the crest of a boom,
and many people will probably be wanting and de-
serving more help presently.

June, 1912.. 14,237 10 „,, 9.653 4,554 47,186
f Assets written up. Reserves trans-
Dec., 1912..-! ferred to capital, £82,687
V 28,611 10 p.c. = 11,989 16,622 63,808
That is the best we can do with it. It was not in-
tended that anyone should be able to do very much.
For years past, knowing that municipal enterprise
was threatening, the co., like most other N.S.W. gas
cos., has been stuffing away profit. That is to say,
it was using undisclosed profits to purchase assets
which never went in the published accounts; or it
used them to write down assets below their fair value.
Then the Gas Bill dropped in like a bomb. It pro-
posed to disregard reserves altogether in fixing the
standard dividend. The Act, as passed, gives the
cos. six months to capitalise their reserves. To take
advantage of that provision the old make-believe of
the cos. had to be exposed ; one after the other they
began to shake the hidden trumps out of their sleeves.
The process was called “revaluing the assets.” By how
much this co. wrote its assets up it is impossible to
say exactly. For a good while previously it had
180.000 shares issued and fully paid to £l. Early
in 1911 it issued 30,000 more. The final ss. was due
on September 1, 1912, That made 210,000 issued
and fully paid. In July it was decided to issue an-
other 105.000 shares—in case of accidents. Only Is.
was payable on these—in August. When the re-
valuation of assets was made, and the big
surplus was shown, the co. decided to pav
out as a bonus 7s. 6d. per share on the £1 paid
shares, and 9d. per share on those paid to Is. Calls
were then made on the 105,000 shares, and the bonus
was applied to the payment of calls. Practically,
therefore, the shareholders got for every two old £1
shares one new bonus share paid to 15s. 9d. Another
Is. 3d. has been called, making the shares 17s. paid.
The total amount of the bonus was £82,687. But
apparently something more than that was written on
to the book value of the assets. It will be noticed that
the profit for the past half-year is shown in the table
as £28,611. It is, however, probable that part of
this consists of written-up values. All that can be
said with certainty is that, after providing for the
bonus ( £82,687), the accounts show £28,611 more
than they did six months ago.
Now something else is to be done. The formal re-
serve fund stands at £63,000 —just enough to pro-
vide one fully-paid share for every five hitherto
issued. The directors propose to ask the shareholders
to agree to this being done; and The Bulletin
doesn't think the suggestion will lead to any serious
rioting. This will make the capital £378,000.
(The unpaid 3s. on the 105,000 shares was due on
January 6.) By these various juggles (legitimate
enough, in the circumstances) a 6 p.c. dividend will
he worth as much to the shareholders as a 10 pc
div. would be on the actually paid capital. Mean-
time, to keep the consumers in a sweet temper, and
also to entitle the co. to pay the old rate of dividend,
the price of gas has been reduced from 3s. 9d. to
3s. 6d. per 1000 ft., as from January 1.
The Gas Act, 1912, now in force, provides for a
standard rate of dividend of 10 p.c. p.a., and a
standard charge for gas of 3s. 6d. per 1000 ft. Cos.
are specifically authorised to capitalise their reserves,
but they must do it within six months. This capital
is entitled to 10 p.c. also. Any subsequently-raised
ordinary capital is entitled to only 7 p.c. as a
standard; for pref. capital to be hereafter raised, the
standard is fixed at 5 p.c. If a co. reduces its price
below 3s. 6d., it may pay i p.c. more in dividend for
every Id. of reduction; if it charges more than 3s.
6d., the dividend must he reduced on the same scale
But, on official inquiry, if it is found that costs have
increased by reason of a Wages Board award, or any-
thing of that sort, the extra may be added on to the
3s. 6d., and the dividend need not be reduced. Share-
holders are also interested in the provisions respecting
new issues of shares. The first runs—“ All shares to
he issued shall be offered for sale by public auction
or tender.” Others put the brake on the accumula-
tion of reserves, the object, of course, being to compel
the cos to reduce the price of gas when they can
afford to do it. From all of which it is plain that
though gas cos. have been left the teeth they had!
they are not to be allowed to grow any more. The
distribution of fat bonus shares or cash is at an end
when the six months’ grace has expired.

Five years ago the co.’s property, apart from fixed
assets and machinery, totalled £5719, and the lia-
bilities were £lO,OOB. Now the property, other
than the fixed assets, is worth £8962 and the liabili-
ties are £320. Thus, from this point of view things
have improved by £12,931. Meantime, considerable
amounts have also been put into fixed property. But
this doesn’t mean necessarily that the co., after pay-
ing the copious dividend, is all that much to the good.
It depends on whether enough, or anything, has been
written off against depreciation ; and on that point
the accounts and report are silent. All that is plain
is that a few hundreds a year have been spent on re-
pairs ; but it doesn’t follow that a patched coat is
worth as much as a new coat. A balance-sheet item
six months ago left a nasty taste in the mouth—
“ Rebuilding Princes wharf, £4318.” That amount
has now been incorporated with the Property account
If the cost of building old Princes wharf had been
written off, then it would be a fair thing to drop in
the new item. But not otherwise. If a man bought
a coat for 525., to last a year, and if he put a shil-
ling into his trousers pocket every week to cover wear
and tear of the coat, then at the end of 52 weeks he
would have no coat worth valuing, but he would have
the price of another one. Then, when he bought an-
other coat, he would be entitled to put the cost of the
new coat among his assets. But he obviously wouldn’t
• en .titl e d to put the cost of the two coats there
Ihis is the doubt in this case. Does the co. write off
the value of its coat as it wears it out? Or does it
leave the old coat in the balance-sheet and put the
value of the new one on top of it? It doesn’t look
the sort of co. that would do this sort of thing but
the position ought to be made plain.

* * * *
The 1.0. A. (Insurance Office of Australia),
which was formed a' couple of years ago to butt into
the fire and marine business, has thoughts of taking
its head office from Sydney to Melbourne. The -joint
managers (Stockfeld and Doherty) are strongly in
favor of the move, arguing that more shareholders
are down south than up north, and that the co.’s
business prospects are brighter in Melbourne than
in Sydney, but the Board in Sydney is, perhaps
naturally, disinclined to sanction the shift. The
matter is to be fought out at the shareholders’ meet-
ing ; and on present appearances the change will be
made. Certainly, as the managers are the men who
promoted the co. and * are more or less personally
pledged to make money for the shareholders, their
considered opinion ought to, carry great weight.
Maoruand is still borrowing every penny
it can lay its hands on. This shows the Oct -Dec.
quarterly banking averages for the past four years:—
Deposits. Advances.
1909 .. . . £20,761,244 £18,779,569
1910 .. . . 22,563,318 19,653,388
1911 •• .. 23,431,130 23,191,003
1912 - . . . 22,906,779 23,346,543
Thus the advances are now in excess of deposits,
fixed and current. The figures suggest that the country
is living beyond its means; and there is only one
thing ahead of either country or individual that keeps
on this course long enough—smash!

for means of transport of the raw material, half-

terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of is. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

The Bulletin has said a few dozen or
hundred times that the prbfits of a trading co. depend
almost wholly upon the valuation of the merchandise
stock and other assets. If too big a price is set on
the stock, the profits are, of course, inflated. This
will probably be found to be what is most the matter
with the J. D. Williams Co., whose shares have come
such a smash in the past fortnight. On top of that,
there was water and more water, and the manage-
ment never got the confidence of the men who handle
the big investments. Add the fact that the bull
buyers were mostly weak, that a fire or two hap-
pened at an awkward time, and you've got all the
explanation necessary to account for the slump.
From an old insurance man : —ln my con-
tribution to The Bulletin of 19/12/’l2, I referred
to the substitution of the actuarial reserve value of
a life policy for the entirely arbitrary, and always
unjust, “surrender value” that an office may formu-
late for the victimisation of its policy-holders, and
for the increase of its surplus profits. In the Insur-
ance Bill, which has since been introduced, it is pro-
posed that “the reserve value of a policy shall be the
full amount reserved by the company "in respect of
the policy in accordance with its latest method of
valuation, and shall not be the amount which the
company is prepared to pay in cash on the surrender
of the policy.” This does not affect the position from
the point of view of the harpy; he may now pur-
chase a valuable policy for £3OO (the “surrender
value”), and, some months later, may obtain from
the office a fullv-paid policy for the reserve value of
from £5OO to £6OO. In the circumstances, the
Idunder I anticipated from the Federal Parliament
is apparent, and, therefore, the harpies have a free
hand for some months to come.
The proposed Bill is all in favor of the man who
is financial yet desires to cease premium paying
while ensuring his “cover,” and does not help the
man who is with his back to the wall and is obliged to
accept a “surrender value” for pressing needs. No
principle of equity can possibly justify such differen-
tiation as is proposed. If a co. can be forced by Act
to treat a reserve value as a single premium for
the purchase of a fully-paid policy, then such co.
should be obliged to make the amount of that reserve
value available for payment in consideration of the
absolute surrender of the policy. Section 64 pro-
vides as follows: “A policyholder who desires to dis-
continue further premium payments shall, on applica-
tion to the co., be entitled to receive a paid-up policy
of such amount as would be purchasable with
the reserve value of his policy at the due
date of the next premium available in re-
spect thereof.” It is clear that the Bill intends that
the reserve value shall be the single premium for the
purchase of the fully-paid policy. If an office
demonstrates by its actuarial valuation of two
policies that each has a reserve value of £6OO, it has
no right to be allowed to differentiate between the
two by allowing one policyholder a cash “surrender
value” of £3OO, because he is in urgent need of
money, and the other a purchasing power of £6OO,
because he will take out a fullyoaid policy. The
flagrant injustice of the differentiation between a
cash “surrender value” and a reserve value will be
illustrated in a striking manner bv the following
hypothetical case. Assume a policy for £1250, a re-
serve value of £6OO, and a cash “surrender value”
of £3OO. The life policyholder may borrow 90 p.c.
of the “surrender value,” i.e., £270. If he default
in payment of interest and future premiums, the co.
is presumably obliged ( vide section 65) to continue
the policy in force until the indebtedness amounts to
the reserve value. Thus, when such a policy would
lapse, the accumulations in respect of loans, interests
and premiums would amount to £7OO or more —the
then reserve value. If Parliament is prepared to con-
serve the interests of the policyholder up to the
standard of the reserve value, in respect of unpaid
premiums, why should the policyholder not be allowed
to draw the reserve value if he chooses to surrender
his policy, or 90 p.c. of that reserve value if he re-
quires a loan ?
If the provisions of the Bill under review are en-
acted by Parliament, then we will have the spectacle
of the Commonwealth permitting cos. to formulate
arbitrarily low and unjust “surrender values” to
operate against individuals in unfortunate circum-
stances ; while it gives those companies a license to
show preference to others who are less in need of
special consideration : and, furthermore, the Com-
monwealth will lend itself to the creation of a field
for the benefit of the harpy, at the expense of the
necessitous policyholder.
I venture to advance the views (1) that the reserve
value should be fixed as the basis of all relations be-
tween policyholder and co. ; (2) that loans should be
available up to the extent of 90 p.c. of the re-
serve value; and (3) that the “surrender value”
should be fixed at 80 p.c. of the reserve value. It
may be argued that this would lead to the loss of
considerable business ; but the opposite would be the
case. At present the loss of business, owing to the
exhaustion of the very limited “surrender values,” is
exceedingly large, and is due entirely to the scant
consideration =hown to policyholders. If the reserve
value of a policy were made the basis of a loan or
surrender (in the percentages mentioned above), verv
few absolute surrenders would ensue, for the reason
that a policyholder in necessitous circumstances could
arrange for the issue of a naid-up policy to himself,
and then borrow 90 p.c of the reserve value which
had been made the consideration of the issue of his
paid-up policy. Such policvholder would then have
an interest in a very considerable equity of redemp-
tion, which would be well worth protecting by pav-
ing the interest under his loan. Under the present
conditions everv inducement is offered to the policy-
holder in necessitous circumstances to surrender his
nolicv, instead of becoming a borrower, inasmuch as
he may draw the full “surrender value.” whereas if
he takes a loan he would receive only 90 p.c. of that
“surrender value.” Such policyholder naturally
argues that the 10 p.c. margin of the “surrender
value” left over the amount of the loan, mav be
swamped by any possible default, in respect of pre-
miums as well as of interest, on his part: so that. he
would be wise to surrender outright instead of taking
a loan.
Assuming a company had just completed an
actuarial valuation, and desired to wind up, would
the policvholders not be paid in cash the reserve
values shown to belong to them? If those reserve
values were not paid to the policvholders in full, to
whom would the balances belong ? Because such a co. is
desirous of continuing its operations, is that any
reason why it should deliberately set itself (with the
complicity of Parliament if the new Bill be passed)
to rob a section of the policyholders for the advantage
of the others?

Kyloe (N.S.W.) has done splendid work
in cooper concentration. A recent return shows
that the mill treated 239 tons of 4.5 p.c. ore in a
week, and recovered 80.3 p.c. of the copper in the
torm of high-grade concentrate containing from 20
h'L. P-c. metal. Kyloe can treat 5 p.c. ore
" ith two-thirds of a pound of crude eucalyptus oil,
and the cost of the oil is about sd. per ton of ore.
•Jig residues can be treated very effectively and
cheaply. 1 ailings contain less than 1 p.c. copper.
Considering the success achieved by its mill, Kyloe
deserves more luck with its mine; but No. 7 level
has not run into payable ore yet.

Mount Royal, Tottenham (N.S.W.), after
h fngmg up the smelter for three years, makes an-
There is a solid lode of sulphide ore at
the 315 ft. level worth 5 p.c., and this has been driven
on for 150 ft. to the westward. The width of the
lode varies a great deal. At the 213 ft. level it is
up to 10ft. wide. Other copper mines in this dis-
trict are in good heart, particularly the Carolina,
which .is four miles from Tottenham. This mine
sends its ore to the Bogan smelter, and regular ship-
ments of matte are made to Port Kembla.
The two biggest Broken Hill concerns are
hard at work on new industrial undertakings. The
H. Prop, has got its iron and steel proposition so
far advanced that it has just announced the issue of
240,000 shares at £2, or a premium of £1 12s.
The Sulphide Corporation is more modest. Its latest
activities are in the direction (1) of saving ammonia
and other coal by-products, and (2) of using the fur-
nace by-products in the manufacture of superphos-
phates. Plants for both purposes have been put in
hand at Newcastle.

* * * *
After seven years. Professor Nicholas has
again taken charge of Burbank’s Birthday Gift
(W.A.). In the old days when he had control, nine
levels were opened up and 132,700 tons were ex-
tracted and treated for a return of over half a million
sterling. About £lOO,OOO was paid in dividends;
then the co. experienced a lean time, which termin-
ated in reconstruction. 8., M. and Co. next came
upon the scene and dug out an additional £69,300.
They, however, did not succeed in making any money
for shareholders, and finally let tributors have a
look-in. The latter recovered another £69,000; but,
instead of making headway, the finances of the co.
went backward, and at the end of 1911 they were in
rather a bad way. Since then £28,000 more has
been extracted by tribute parties, and from all ac-
counts there isn’t much left for the Professor to get
hold of.
* * * *
Although warned that such was not the
case, Ida H (W.A.) clung to the idea 15 months ago
that a large tonnage of soz. ore awaited extraction
from the bottom levels and beyond. A little stoping
showed that there wasn’t much left, and after paying
the first of promised monthly dividends the Board had
to admit that it had made a mistake to pay anv at
all. Unhinged by disappointment, it tried to make
a recovery by gambling in Nigerian tin stocks, and
got deeper into the mire. At the meeting held in
London last month, shareholders were told that,
although the payment of dividends could not be
resumed at present, there was reasonable hope of
something later on. Shareholders will, however, be
foolish if they build on the-chance.
* * * *
With the aid of tributors and an occa-
sional turn-up, Paringa (W.A.) manages to earn a
little. Last year it dug up £12,352 on its own
account and collected an additional £713 in royal-
ties Recent development is stated to have exposed
a good shoot of ore at the No. 1 and 2 levels of
Geach’s workings, and as there are plenty of funds
in hand, the management has been instructed to bog
in and see how values shape in deeper ground.
* * * *
A conference of diggers and prospectors
sitting at Kalgoorlie has agreed upon . the follow-
in*?* (1) That no royalty should be paid by tribu-
tors unless each man earns at least £4 per week,
and then not more than 10 p.c. of the gold should be
handed over ; (2) that concentration of labor should
not be granted over more than 96 acres, and labor
conditions should not be considered fulfilled by tri-
butors unless one man is employed to every three
acres; (3) that concentration should not be granted
on idle ground unless a guarantee is given that it
will be let on tribute; (4) that rents should be
payable quarterly and power given a registrar to
defer payments; (5) that the size of areas pegged
by those who arrive first at a new rush should be
limited; (6) that no provision should be made for
a fine in lieu of forfeiture; (7) that Government
diamond drills should be more freely used for pros-
pecting purposes; and (8) that a mining bank should
be established to assist prospectors.
* * * *
The owners of the Revenue claim, Bamboo
Creek (W.A.), have had some remarkable specimens
on view in a window in Perth lately. They claim to
have crushed three tons of stone in 1911 for 8200 z.,
and one ton during 1912 for 2750 z. Judging by the
specimens, these startling results seem feasible. Bam-
boo Creek is in the Marble Bar district.
At the Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vic.),
it took just £16,500 during the past six months to
make £17,512, leaving £lOl2 profits available. The
bank cash account is £1156, plus stores, etc,
£1917. This isn’t much for a mine working sJdwt.
gold; but the Lord Nelson lot are cheerful
and still live in dreamland. Otherwise there would
be a lot of growling at the report. It is confessed
that 11,972 tons averaged only sJdwt., and the
extent of the stopes is disclosed ; but not a word is
said of the values at the 2225 ft. or the 2405 ft.
There isn’t, however, more than a hatful of stone left
at the 2225 ft. level, and work at the bottom level
shows that the formation has shrunk. Then the
fault that came in at about 1400 ft. has sliced the
shoot and the stone into two, the bigger slab being
to the north. All this indicates the wisdom of the
fusion of the Lord Nelson and the North; but the
price paid when the register was bounced up to
72,000 shares was out of all proportion.
Diamond Creek lies within an hour or
two of Collins-street, and holds the mine nearest
to Melbourne. Moreover, wonder of wonders, it
turns out divs. with happy regularity. With from
only three to nine inches to work on, ounce dirt
would frighten the color out of Alex. Gordon’s face.
As things go, the average pans out at 30dwt.
Therefore Diamond Creek furnishes the richest aver-
age of almost any mine of consistent running in Vic-
toria. Sinking is down to No. 9 level, where if the
co. has its old luck it won’t find the lode whittled
down to a thread, as was promised on one or two
occasions. No. 8 North shows a nine-inch shoot 200 ft.
long standing strong against the miner’s pick.
Central Red, White and Blue, Bendigo,
has alighted on the cap of a small saddle in its
shaft. That is not to interfere with the sink, for
it is on the cards that the Little 180 saddle will
come in lower down as a further sweetener. If the
co. happens to get on to a speck or two of gold in
either the present saddle or in the 180 formation,
there will be a little kick to help Bendigo at a time
when it ranks as an outsider. Connection is estab-
lished between the 400 and the intermediate levels
north at the Central Blue, and it is to this point that
the drill will now be applied. A nice block exists
here. It is needed, too, seeing how stoping has gone
on at 318 ft.; but the Beehive wants to know the
grade of this stone and its size. Still, the gamble in
the Central Blue is the way the Little 180 formation
may develop whenever it is got. The crowd at the
back of the show will know first, but there should
be a chance for shareholders to pop in if they worry
round sufficiently.
* # * *
To its immortal credit be it said that
Burnt Creek, Dunolly, paid a shilling or two in the
dim past. Since then there has been clanking of
pumps and hauling of dirt and wheedling of calls
out of a nest of optimistic shareholders. Called-up
capital appears at £66,000, represented for the
greater part this way: Mine account, £16,271;
machinery at cost, £10,883; working account, two
shafts, £39,000. The little co. has long had the
best support, men like James Randell, Prof. Nanson,
James McCrorey and the Finks having shouldered its
weight for a dreary decade. Now Burnt Creek is on
the dividend list again, largely because of some hope-
ful cyanide chap who has got on to the old slum
dump. Taking mine work, the return of £16,228
for the past six months represents_ an average of
14-klwt. per square fathom. This gives about
£2300 profit for the term. The Board isn’t taking
any risks about the future, being content to promise
sixpence monthly for some time ahead. What tempers
their anticipations is that in the rise in the west
end of the mine, water is heavy and prospects don’t
look very good. Crosscutting may change this, but
the further down the lead the higher costs must go.
* * * *
Cameron’s Ajax, Daylesford, lies to the
west of the Ajax group, and must not be con-
founded with the big Ajax mines that have caused
so many aches and pains to bulls and bears. The
chief possessions to date are hard slate and a well-
defined lode track. In the latter occur bunches of
quartz, respecting which the best testimony available
is that they give “good prospects at times.” This is
like the occasional sobering-up of a hard drunk.
There is also the usual mixture formation out of
which comes the usual flow of water. It is on this
shandygaff stuff that expectation is built. Seeing
the wad of forfeited scrip in hand, a strike of decent
quartz would mudhook up a fair-sized parcel of bears ;
but the situation of the claim doesn’t inspire confi-
dence that anything unexpected of the kind will
happen. * * *
A new tin find has been made at Redrock,
in the Stanthorpe district, about a couple of miles
on the Queensland side of the N.S.W. border. The
man in the street has long been asking for the
whereabouts of the lode from which all the tm
around Stanthorpe came. As a mater of fact, it did
not come from a. lode a.t all, but from the decomposi-
tion of the surface granite. In the new belt, how-
ever, extending from the Beehive pronerty across
Carpenter’s Gully to Sundown, there are quite a
number of lodes carrying good tm. Thus Sundown
has got specimen stone below 80ft. But outside Car-
penter’s Gully, where the stone is low grade, little
development work has been done so far. However it
may turn out at depth, as a surface show the Bee-
hive would be hard to beat. The lease (55 acres) is
nracticallv covered with wash dirt, most of it
payable and some of it highly payable, containing
loose -boulders of rich tin-bearing greisen. These
appear to have broken away from the junction of the
granite and slates, where there are still rich speci-
mens. Not enough work has been done to entitle
anyone to sav how much stone there is : but appear-
ances are promising. The Beehive people have had
V A Derrick and W. H. Rands running the rule
over their property, and both like the surface pros-
npcts Derrick, who has made two inspections, found
at the end of December that the work done since bis
previous visit was bearing out the very favorable
opinion he formed in September. #
* *
Chillagoe continues to feed its sharehold-
ers on hope. The return for four weeks reads: I<9
tons copper, 158 tons lead, 21,7620 z. silver and
5550 z. gold from 3986 tons of ore. An increased
output of metals has been maintained for some time
past so the financial returns for the year ending
March 31 may pan out better. The railway revenue
has also been well sustained, and is shaping for
£lOO 000 per annum. The branch line to Mount
Mullitran coal mine is the most important undertak-
ing since Chillagoe bought the Einasleigh copper-
mine and pushed its railhead to the Etheridge. The
new mill erected to treat the mineralised ore of the
district has done good work, but local opinion is
that charges are too high. The irregular nature. of
the ore shoots throughout the district keeps mining
costs high; and nobody is getting fat. Various
promising mines are being developed by the Mam-
moth Co. at Klondyke (renamed Cardross). The
feature is the high copper and gold assays. If smelt-
ing difficulties can be overcome, considerable quan-
tities of matte will be fed to Chillagoe.
Mount Lyell has been smelting for some
time past with only one furnace, and it has taken
over 100 tons of the low-grade stuff to produce a
ton of copper. The addition of Comstock ore should
brighten up the returns a bit. The North Lyell will
in course of time resume its output of 10,000 tons
per month, but no date can be given. Reserves figure
at 1,083,752 tons, sufficient to last nine years; in
fact, the ore taken out during the past nine years is
nearly the same in quantity as is estimated to be
showing in the mine. Still, in the face of the re-
turns for the last half-year, one has to be optimistic
to consider the North Lyell the old 6 p.c. proposition.

Concerning artist Lister Lister, it is
worth a line to say that he was born in
Sydney, and went to England with his
parents when he was too young to defend
himself, being at the knickerbockers age of
seven. He studied art in the famous Bed-
ford schools later on, afterwards exhibited
at most of the leading English and Scottish
shows,, and . returned here 24 years ago. As
an artist Lister Lister is mostly an out-door
man, and sometimes travels far to get the
glint on distant rivers. As a man. he is so
entirely free from malice that the fiercest
rival has never caught him in the act of
hitting back without the gloves.

The trfigic drowning of Mrs. Henry For-
syth (Newcastle) at Okere, in the Rotorua
district, recalls the sudden depths and illu-
sive shallows of some of Maoriland’s best-
known trout streams and lakes. Even in
midsummer the water is often icy cold, and
the currents add to the menace. Mrs. For-
syth was well known in sporting circles as
one ot the finest horsewomen ever raised in
Australia. A sister used also to be a clever
cross-country rider in the days before the
Sydney Hunt Club breathed its last gasp,
pome times mounted on The Ace, a famous
black hunter, at that time owned by “Banjo”
I aterson, the lady took the timber like a
bird. The late Mrs. Forsyth generally
accentuated her reputation as a sport by
wearing a smart short skirt and manly hat
and coat at local race meetings. Attractive
gaiters completed the outfit if the weather
was damp. Henry Forsyth is the manager
ot .Dudley .Colliery, near Newcastle. He
was with his wife at Okere, and made
strenuous efforts to save the ill-fated sports-
woman.

also acting as Minister for Bands, paused
to get his breath the other day, after some
strenuous weeks in his double-barrelled bil-

c^ an( f returned with a conviction that
the snowy nob is worth more attention than
it gets as a national health asset. After
a prolonged tussle with the telephone, which
buzzed like a swarm of infuriated bees,
Minister Trefle was understood to say, first,
that snowballing in summer was calculated
to bring a healthy blush to the cheeks of
the most hardened politician; secondly, that
the animal and vegetable matter in that
snow-kissed latitude might be added to with
benefit to the surroundings. And then he
effervesced with enthusiasm over the joy of
being alive, with the chance of catching
snowballs in mid-summer.

A golf enthusiast, just back from Leura,
where the N.S.W. Golf Union had six days
of unalloyed caddy and ball, poured a few
details into my listening ear. Monday, Jan.
20, the first day on the links, was heavily
rained on. It didn’t damp the golfer’s spirits
to any great extent, and a bogey competi-
tion gave Mrs. O’Callaghan, who tied with
another Golfing Petticoat, a chance to show
her mettle. On Tuesday, Jan. 21, Miss
Nellie Meers (Mudgee) snatched the Blue
Mountain championship from 18 or 20 com-
petitors. Weather good. Sunburnt noses
in the offing of the best players. Jan. 22,
Ladies’ Doubles. This was won by Mrs.
and Miss Purdie (Orange). The latter is
a golfer of some local renown, for she car-
ried off the country championship last July
at Kensington.
On Thursday the golf speciality known as
a choice score careered across the links.
The Choicest was Miss Edith Minter (Syd-
ney ), who was ahead of competition. Other
swift players, who tied in this match, were
the good-looking Miss Mabel Mackenzie
(Dubbo), Mrs. Christian (Hunter’s Hill),
Miss Egan and another. On Friday, any
inhabitant of Leura who was not on the golf
links was a person who was deaf to the yell
of the caddy, and habitually refrained from
the joys of “lost ball.” Two-thirds of the
population followed the players round the
rolling links that run up to the edge of a
vast, indigo-blue gully. Miss Edith Minter
won this set by an eyelash from Miss Egan.
On Saturday an orgy of golf ended in mixed
foursomes, won by Mrs. Christian and Mr.
Forsyth. Miss Kiss (Kensington) and Mr.
Wilkinson (Hunter’s Hill) put in some
swift work in the foursomes, doing the 18
holes in the gross score of 87. Miss Egan
entertained the golfers (about 40) to tea
in the Club House. The Leura links had
been loaned by the local club for the palpi-
tating events just mentioned. The Ladies’
Golf Union fell upon the necks of the care-
takers, Mr. and Mrs. Millington, at parting,,
and thanked them in three volumes for their
attention to the golfers’ hunger and thirst.
Artist Joe Wolinski and his fine, upstand-
ing sister Zara, went off to London in the
Maloja. Before leaving, he had something
of interest to say aibout Mark Rubin, the
London pearl merchant, one of whose safes
had, according to a cable, just resisted a 12-
hour burglar siege. The industrious and
scientific Dukes of the Crowbar got away
with nothing : indeed, they left in their hur-
ried flight £6O worth of up-to-date burgling
plant.
Says WoEnski: “Mr. Rubin, who is of
Polish descent, is a firm friend and patron
of mine. . I have done a good deal of work
for him, including a portrait of Mrs. Rubin
and her celebrated pearl necklet, which is
said to be worth £BO,OOO. These gems were
kept for safety in the equally famous safe,
a Chatsworth, which resisted the burglar’s
attack the other day. The contrivance is
supposed to be the last word in safety. It
is, roughly, the shape of a wide cross. In
the top part there is a place where four let-
ters, making a certain word, must be in-
serted. This releases a sheet of steel. Until
that is done, there is no hope of getting to
the lock. It was supposed to be an impos-
sible proposition for ‘the talent,’ and so it has
proved. It was Mr. Rubin who bought ‘The
Ghetto School’ and ‘Autumn Fancies,’ which
I exhibited here last year; he lent them to
me to show in my own city of Sydney.”
. Rubin, by the way, started in Melbourne
in the opal business as quite a young man.
Then he went off to London. Opals dropped
out of favor for a time, and Rubin went in
for pearls. To-day he is one of the foremost
dealers in these illusive gems, with extensive
fisheries in Westralian waters. His wife
was a Miss Rebecca Davis, of Melbourne.
The Chatriotic Club ladies lent their
good-looking Bligh-street premises for the
farewell sing-song to Zara Wolinski the
other night. . Solicitor Aubrey Halloran
handed the girl a plump silver bag, nicely
padded with sovereigns. This gift was from
various friends and acquaintances to speed
the singer on her way to Europe. Brother
Wolinski will put Zara’s voice under Bouhy
or some other top-notch teacher.
Pianist de Beaupuis, who has been holi-
daying in Rotorua (M.L.) and other geyser-
strewn hamlets, gets back to Sydney in a day
or two. At Whakarewarewk the small
brown babies danced a haka for the visiting
musician. He was so charmed with their
sense of rhythm that he acted as impromptu
conductor. Anyone who has seen a succes-
sion of fat and mostly unclad Maori young-
s^ rs ln a ku.ka, can picture the orchestral
effects of their toes under the baton of a
professional air-beater.
Arthur Gordon, a promising young fiddler
from the. Thames district of Maoriland,
passed this port the other day. He is on the
trek to Brussels to absorb technique from
the mighty Belgian, Ysaye.
Actor Will Desmond and his wife booked
their passages for a round trip to U.S. some
weeks ago. Their plans changed at the last
moment, though their names floated into the
list of departed passengers. The cause of
their hugging the land till Saturday last was
the continuous trouble in one of Mrs. Will’s
knees, the result of a slip down a brass-
bound staircase some time ago, the unlucky
joint getting about a baker’s dozen of bangs
in its descent. The Desmonds didn’t revoke
last, week, and left, for ’Frisco in the
Tahiti. The lady will place her bumped
cartilage under the care of a specialist in
that busy town, while the dark-browed Des-
mond rushes on to Noo Yark to fulfil a well-
doilared billet there. He didn’t rouse any
riotous enthusiasm in N.S.W., but he has
■a firm foothold on the American boards.

An unobtrusive musician named 11. Xevill
bmith has been spending a summer holiday
m Sydney. A Victorian by birth, and a cos-
mopolitan by habit, he spent 10 year, in
Europe before settling in M.L. for an inter-
lude. He took charge of a wandering sis-
ter s teaching connection in Timaru in 1910
or thereabouts, as locum-tenens till the lady
returns from a course of singing and other
instruction in Paris. Nevill Smith has a
small waggonload of reminiscences of Berlin
and the lighter frivolities of France. In
Berlin he met Prof. Delmar, an ex-Mel-
bourmte, and now professor of English at
the War Academy and the Uni. in the
Kaiser s chief city. Delmar for a time
edited the Continental Times in Germany.
After getting the appointments just men-
tioned, he passed the newspaper work on to
Jhis sister-in-law, Miss Hook. This daring
i^ s now editor of the foreign Times
which claims to have the largest circulation
'Oi any Hnglish paper on the Continent, ex-

Mrs. David’s cheerful chatter about her
trip to Funafuti in 1897, Prof. David being
in charge of the expedition, has recently
been reprinted in an Australian edition.
This slim booklet of 150 pages is to be intro-
duced to the State school child this year, as
a supplementary reader. Several of ’the
more experienced school marms fail to un-
derstand why. The style is breezy, but Mrs.
David never intended it, one imagines, to
be anything but a trivial, ephemeral record
of three months on a coral island, 8 deg.
south of the sunburnt Equator, its chief
interest is the lively account of the writer’s
housekeeping, while the learned members of
the expedition superintended the bores that
drilled holes in the coral atolls. I gather
from Mrs. David’s account of Funafuti that
it has many advantages as a residential isle.
No one knows his own or anyone else’s age.'
There appears to be no census nuisance, no
Man about the Village (the prototype of a
certain type of Man of about Town) to
add a few years on to the score of any
heathen beauty who has snubbed his ad-
vances, and no Funafuti club in which the
Society male discusses the birthdays of Mrs.
Tufaina or Miss Coral Tagasia. Happy
isle! Positively no brown lady knows even
the approximate age of her rival, for she
does not even know her own.
Grace Palotta looks about five-and-twenty
in “The Sunshine Girl.” She wears a foam
of white lace over a maize shimmer in the
first act, and wins the stage-frock competi-
tion by about two lengths. The apron with
voluminous pockets is a feature of the chorus
ladies’ clothes in Act I. Each minx has
pockets enough to complete a small hanging
wardrobe. In these days of no pockets, the
working woman can only writhe with envy
when she sees so many of these lost trea-
sures gathered together in one place. Frank
Greene is singing up to his best form lately.
As a soap aristocrat heavily disguised in
leather apron and leggings, he is up to the
best traditions of Make-Believe. Leslie
Holland seems to have a feeling for indigo
blue in a very-dark shade. I notice that he
now takes to this dandy tint in his evening
swallow-tails, and makes terrific headway
amongst the girls at Port Sunshine in con-
sequence.
Mrs. Louat sends along the news of her
daughter Ernestine’s engagement to Mr. E.
Parlongue, an engineer, of New Caledonia,
where you can get the most delightful coffee,
but mustn’t ask for a cup of tea, unless you
want to seriously inconvenience your host.
“Rosna” writes from London :
Another wedding imminent is that of Johanna Red-
mond, whose father leads the Irish party in the House
of Commons and whose mother was one of the Dal-
tons, of Orange. The damsel is a handsome and
brainy girl, with a leaning towards the inky way;
she has written several successful playlets. The
bridegroom is Max Green, chairman of the Prison
Board in Ireland, and late wrap-carrier on Lord Aber-
deen’s staff. This knotting is to be strictly private
and devoid of frills.
Lyn, another of the good-looking Maclur-
can girls, of the fashionable Wentworth
Hotel, is engaged. The man is C. PI. Lane.
Ma Maclurcan some years ago put her cook-
ery recipes into a volume which has become
almost an Australian text-book ; and if she
were writing an introduction to a new edi-
tion she would probably be inclined to repeat
her frequent proud boast that her daughters
know almost as much of the art as she does
herself.
Chappell and Co. forward a small library
of songs. In the first flight is the whimsi-
cal “On the Day I Get to Heaven” (music
by Liza Lehmann ; words by Sivori Levey)
which describes the Three Wonders of Para-
dise. First, the number of people the singer
didn’t expect to meet in Harping circles
above. Secondly, the people he did expect to-
see who were unavoidably detained else-
where. The Third Wonder was the fact:
that he slipped through the pearly gates;
himself. Liddle contributes a new setting:
—new to .me, anyhow—of Newman’s “Lead,.
Kindly Light.” Hermann Lohr, one of the-
most persistent writers of music to fit modern
ballads, comes along with “Rose of My
Heart” and “It Was a Song You Sang Me.”
The words of this song are not worth more
than half an encor ■.
Paling’s send a solid helping of music*
of fair to medium merit. Putting sus&fe pan-
tomime music as too liable to emfoorrage' ai
spirit of idleness in this sultry season, E
turned up “Two Sengs,” the music by a
N.S.W. composer, Kathleen Boydell. One
is “Uncertainty,” and the softly sentimental
words are written by a youthful Sydney
girl, M. Scott Shaw (a niece of Miss'Rose
Scott), who has a pretty trick of metre.
Robert Browning’s lines, “But Love.” don’t
fit the girl who sighs for a motor and a sack
of diamonds, but the new setting, by Elsie
M. Cowley, helps the old-fashioned notion
over a musical stile.

That the hand of our old friend Mr. Davis has not lost its cunning is proved beyond doubt bv
“The Book of Dan,” which has just been published.
Dan, the son of Dad, has grown to man’s estate, and developed many humorous peculiarities
which land him in particularly tight corners, out of which he generally manages to extricate
himself like a veritable Napoleon of the Bush. Dan is a character, and his sense of humor is
not always the same as the other fellow’s, but it will assuredly tickle the reader to tears.
Of this book there is no doubt; it is bound to become a favorite.
Over £lO,OOO worth of “On Our Selection” and “Our
New Selection” have been sold in Australia—a fact
that speaks volumes for their popularity.
Thousands of admirers of Steele Rudd, who could not hitherto afford the
prohibitive price of his books, can now laugh with the best.

On Saturday, at the Exhibition, the Per-
sonages progressed by spasms through the
great crowd that had come to the A.N.A.
Show of Australian Manufactures. Lady
i in , evi<:ie nce. but Mrs.
\\ att, with white lace bows topping a bridal
grey dress, walked with the spruce com-
mander of the Belgian Cadets. Mrs. Hen-
nessy sailed along under full feather rig in
company with her Gaud Mayor. The party
rallied at the impressive timber exhibit, and
made a sortie past boots, cigarettes, marble
pianos and other things, finally fighting their
way clear into the arena, where the refresh-
ments waited under canvas.
t • La £ y i* l j uller and tlle three elder of the
fair half-dozen daughters will sail for Eng-
land by the Moldavia on March 25. After
seeing them safely at school, her Ex. will

The one-step, Bunny hug, Turkey trot
etna all tlie other lawless prances which are
then- offshoots are doomed in the land of
J. Bull. They may still prevail for a few
years in Brondesbury, Fulham, etc.—impos-
sible suburbs where _he young men wear red
handkerchiefs in their evening waistcoats,
and the ladies are so genteel that thev feel
taint at the sound of the word d —n; but so
mu as £ ociet y is concerned they are off
Ihe really smart London hostess, following
the Court, will have none of them. Also,
Alls, htuyvesant Fish, who* despite her low-
comedy name, has attained to the position of
accredited leader of the New York Smart
Set, has put the acid on everything from
the Gaby _ Glide to the Duck’s Hop. This
wdl be grim news for the social climbers of
Australia. They have gone to considerable
trouble and expense to learn these dances
and so appear in the movement in the eyes
of Governors, A.D.C.’s and visiting nota-
bilities. Their reward is the reflection that
it they are observed airing their new gifts
they will be set down instantly as social
back numbers. The fact is, things are mov-
ing too. fast. If only someone could get to
the social clock with an axe !
, Society dame, fresh from three years
in the European capitals, throws a bomb on
the card table of the devoted bridge player.
According to the returned traveller, chess is
now the correct dissipation, and before it
even auction bridge is going to the wall.
Ixtled women sit, with knitted brows, con-
sidering an attack upon a castle, or plotting
the downfall of a bishop.
The Repertory Theatre intends to wake up
the public with a matinee revival of “Man
and Superman,” at the Princess’s Theatre,
on Saturday, February 15. The Theatre
Llub will rally for a general annual meet-
ing in the Austral Salon on Friday evening,
January 31, when present members will be
asked to fire in wise suggestions, and intend-
ing members urged to enrol.
Herbert Shaw, a nephew of the famous
Bernard, departed by the Orsova, after
loitering through the Commonwealth, and so-
journing a little while at a seaside place
near Melbourne. He was free from the
idiosyncrasies of manner that hallmark
Uncle, whom he allowed with an Oxford
accent was the family genius. Herbert made
the Australian trip for the benefit of his
heart, and, for the sake of the same organ,
he kept very quiet. His utterly unexciting
recreation consisted of putting his political
opinions on paper.
At the concert with which Richmond fare-
welled Elizabeth Newbold, the audience was
as crowded as the programme. The flow of
music threatened to go on till midnight, or
later, owing to the ceaseless encores. Lizzie
Newbold has a light soprano of fine quality,
and the artistic instinct to back it up. On
the platform she looked like a flower bathed
in sunshine. This attractiveness should help
her nicely in the lands where gilded laurels
grow. Laurels can be gathered in Australia
—Melba got some ere ever she went to
hurope—blit they were not gilded.
The Women’s Political Association has
opened a small shop in the corner of the tea-
rooms in Arlington Chambers where the
she-league has its headquarters. It is like
a bazaar on a modest scale, since it sells
gifts from enthusiastic suffragists and cash-
boxes the proceeds for the £lOOO fund.
Home-made jams, cakes and sweets, home-
made soap, books on the woman question, a
special brand of W.P.A. tea, and hair-nets
seem to be the principal merchandise. The
fund has just been strengthened by a dona-
tion of £lOO from Mrs. Barr Smith ; but they
will need to sell a heap of soap and hair-nets
to make up the balance.
Most of the Bananalanders now in Mel-
bourne (and there are lots of them) get
annoyed when the Victorian moans about
the heat. One loyal northern woman, how-
ever, fresh from a sparkling season in Too-
woomba, says defiantly, “Why, we had to
wear tweeds at the New Year meeting!”
loowoomba is a squattocratic stronghold,
and the Amateur Turf Club’s January gal-
loping draws crowds of people with a claim
to be considered Most Expensive. The Club
gave a big dance on New Year’s Eve, and
supplied every frivoller with iced champagne
and supper to match. Besides this, two of
the smartest squatting wives gave glittering
private dances, whereat the elect waltzed
the hours away. These energetic functions
are advanced by the Northerner as proof
that the maligned Northern climate is not as
hot as the Melbourne imagination.
Judge Real, of Bananaland, with his wife
and son, is pervading Melbourne in a car
tinted like a ripe pineapple. The Judge is a
big, handsome man, and must look impres-
sive, if.not absolutely terrifying, when backed
into his wig.. A daughter, who has the
family’s special brand of good looks, re-
cently married the owner of one of the
richest cattle runs in Bananaland.
London editors manage to extract a good
deal of gratuitous copy out of celebrities
by circularising them on some topical sub-
ject, with stamped. and addressed envelope
for reply. “What is. your most appreciated
Christmas present?” is a sample of this sort
of thing. Ada Crossley answered: “A little
piece of Christmas pudding made and sent
to me here in London by my dear mother in
my distant homeland, in Australia. It
contained a lucky threepenny piece.” From
the same source one draws the information
that mamma Grainger gave Percy a new
guitar to play with—but not for serenading
purposes, I’m sure.
Squatter Clark, of Bananaland, has come
out of the North, to matrimonially carry off
jolly little Elma Akins, of Geelong.
Evelyn Cudmore, who has seen her sisters
led to the altar, has promised to follow suit
with Stan Stogdale, of Melbourne. Miss
Cudmore, who hails from Avoca station, on
the Lower Darling (or is it the middle Mur-
ray?),. was one of the macintoshed crowd
that visited this dripping city last Cup-time.
One of the Murray-Puckle sons and Miss
Burges, of Westralia, are now bracketed on
the engaged list.
Ruby Austin, daughter of the late Edwin
Austin, M.L.C., is smiling sweetly in re-
sponse to congratulations on her engage-
ment to Linsley Smith, late of Buenos
Ayres.
One of the Wesley Hall heiresses, Dora,
a curly-haired girl who has been abroad for
some time, is matrimonially booked to
Major the Hon. George Morris. She is to
become the daughter-in-law of a nobleman
in April.
Edith Morris journeyed to Sydney to wed
her Rev. Irvine. The bride was head-mistress
of the Church of England Grammar School
for flappers, an institution founded by her
mother’s family (the Frenches), and turned
over to the Church by Morris, sen., who held
a cathedral job for 50-odd years. The pair
passed through Melbourne this week on their
way to London, where they will put up for
a year. Gweneth Morris, the bride’s voung-
est sister, on her way back from Westralia,
awaited them at Adelaide to offer congratu-
lations.
Rosna” writes me from London that
artist Penleigh Boyd takes his bride home
to Australia soon. The yarn goes that the
marriage was the result of a picturesque
accident. Boyd and some pals rented Miss
Anderson’s flat in Paris during her absence,
and before they were ready to quit the lady
announced her intention of returning to it.
Hang it all, we 11 have to kill this woman
or marry her,” said Boyd ; and apparently
found the latter course the. more convenient
at the moment, murder being unponular in
Paris just then. “You will notice,” says
‘Rosna,” “that this yarn gives the bride no
say in the matter whatever. But then it
was told me by a mere man.”
“Dudie” Griffiths, the jolly little daughter
of the squattocrat from Albury way. is
booked for what almost amounts to an “al-
liance ” with voung Niall. son of one of the
Goldsbrough-Mort potentates. Papa Niall,
who is a widower, has just returned from a
foreign trip, and Mrs. Rumor cheerfully
alleges that there will be a double wedding
in the family. By the way, the Niall bride-
groom-elect was best man at the recent
I airbairn-Jowett orange-blossoming. This
is the third engagement since January 1
which has been inspired by the wedding
march in the wake of some happy pair.
. A - H. Chambers, the newly-installed chief
inspector of the Union Bank, is sheltering
himself luxuriously in a Toorak house. Mrs.
Chambers is a particularly smart froeker
and, moreover, is possessed of the where-
withal. She was a Miss Ferguson, daughter
of an original Mount Morganaire.
Mrs. Dick Casey sends word to friends of
the gorgeous time she has been having as
guest of the Carmichaels. For a State ball
given in Calcutta on Christmas Eve, the
Australian visitor got a black tulle dress
and wore a showerbath of diamonds. Lady
Carmichael’s dress was blue and green chif-
fon over gold tissue. It sounds more like a
stage costume than the oulit of the quiet
Scotch lady.
Mrs. Jamieson and her sister, Hilda Marr,
two smart frockers in exclusive St. Kilda-
road circles, have cut all engagements, and
are making a sad journey northward to their
Queensland station. Mrs. Jamiesdn’s son
died there suddenly last week; and, as the
place is situated at the back of beyond, the
mourners will only arrive to hear everything
in the past tense.
The Morphetts (he is relieving manager
of the Queensland National Bank, and she
is daughter of one of the directors) like Mel-
bourne so much that they want to stay. The
little matter will probably be arranged for
them. The pair gave a jolly lunch the other
day to the visiting Coldhams (who hail from
Central Bananaland), the widow Clifton,
(Darling Downs), and rich bachelor Mor-
phett (Cloncurry).
Marshall Hall, it seems, is off to take his
Wagner Centenary undiluted in Europe. In
his absence, the duties of Conservatorium
director will fall to Edouard Scharf. Con-
ductor Fritz Hart, a wizard with the vio-
lin, will carry on the harmony lectures.
Colonel Ballenger, lately retired by Field-
Marshal Time, found that he could not settle
down quietly to enjoy his pension. So he
looked round to see how he could work off
his surplus energy, and decided that he
would become commander-in-chief of the
Austral Hotel, Melbourne. The two smart
Ballenger daughters, who are used to riding
over the country on thoroughbred horses, and
generally living the open-air life, were called
upon at short notice to try city existence.
They responded like good sports, and, in the
intervals of housekeeping, helped to put
Dad’s new place to rights.
Dick Ballenger the other day made a dash
through this city on his way from Westralia
to Queensland. He is hurrying north to
manage a Brisbane brewery in which the
Colonel is interested.
The dining-room of the Fairlie House flat-
ter was flowing with champagne and cream
puffs on Wednesday of last week, when Mrs.
h red Keats, of the John Cumming clan, hos-
tessed a feminine luncheon of farewell to the
Hussey-Chomley widow. Dame Madden
topped the guest list. The widow will have
as travelling companion the younger daugh-
ter of “Bill” Weatherly, of Billilla. The
Keats matron will shortly sail Londonward
herself, her two sons being due for a parental
visit.
Melbourne girls are losing bachelor Geoff
Kiddle for a time. He is off to India, ac-
companied by sister Rita, of the copper-
colored hair. They intend to put in an in-
structive travel before returning to Aus-
tralia.
The other day Bertie Power, son of Her-
bert Power, of Toorak, damaged his collar-
bone. Later on he stepped out of a French
window in a rural hotel, and fell 25ft. on to
a pavement. The fact that he is merely in
hospital after such a frightful smash shows
how a little experience in tumbling will save
a man.

Cliveden, the former home of the Clarkes
now turned into flats, is one of the few high-
toned places that don’t object to the young
of man. The Stuart Murrays have their
son there; the Rubber Edwards couple keep
a brace of boys on the handy premises ; and
several young matrons of the Very Best set
are establishing themselves there with their
human pets. John Madden, C.J., plays a
patriarchal part in the cosy coffee lounge
after dinner, patting every child within reach
and generally acting the benevolent great
man.
