Mrs. “Medlow” Burgess has established a
stylish guestery called “Le Cairo,” which is
going to knock hash off anything of its kind
in sight. The place is right up against the
basement doors of Parliament House
through which members short-cut to the
Council and Assembly.
Artist Hal Waugh has invented and
patented a contrivance for outdoor painting
It is a natty arrangement whereby the easei
is kept from wobbling, and the wet can-
vases (of which two or three can be carried
at a time) are stopped from making them-
selves into sandwiches. Hal’s ingenuity is
bred in the bone. Waugh, senior, now de-
ceased, was a clever engineer.
Some time in March, the Charles McEvoy
pair, with their smart girleens, will repack
their imported garments and move from the
Charlie Ryan abode, which they have ten-
anted since their return from England. Clive-
den Mansions will be their next shelter.
Olive May, the American beauty who has
just married into the nobility, is so mam-
years younger than famous sister Edna that
various cats declared, after her stage debut,
that she was really Edna’s daughter. A
Melbourne doctor who made mud-pies with
the Mav family remembers little Olive
when she was young enough to play
impish tricks with Edna’s many beaux.
Olive, by marrying a Paget, has
made the better match socially, but Edna got
the money. For her honeymoon trip, hus-
band Eewisohn gave her a gold-fitted auto-
mobile. rather like a palace on wheels. Even
King Solomon’s best girl in all her glory was
not conveyed in one of these.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Meningie, on the Lakes, awoke with a yell on
Thursday, and shaking off its normal condition of pro-
found depression, went quite gay. The occasion was
the Warrengie races. “Percy” and K. D. Bowman
carried off most of the prizes, including the Ladies'
Bracelet, which Mr. Arthur Jervois’s riding won for
Mrs. Keith Bowman. A large party arrived from
Campbell House. The Peter Rows came with the
Alan MacFarlanes, who motored over from Welling-
ton Lodge, and Mrs. John Pile and her daughter were
with the Alan Bowmans. The whole racecourse,
socially speaking, had tea with Mrs. Richardson, of
Warrengie station. Her husband is reputed to have
as good an eye for a horse as any coper in S.A.
Meanwhile, in the Angaston-Mount Pleasant dis-
trict, polo was raging furiously, as indeed it does
most days, these prosperous times. The players are
chiefly Angases and Murrays and Melroses and Daw-
sons. Scotchbyterians by origin but sportsmen by
inclination—an example, possibly, of the uses of en-
vironment.
. Miss Lizzie Waite has gone back to England.
Singer Serena Kleinschmidt (a protegfie of the
Waites) is understood to be scanning the horizon,
with a welcoming expression of the eye, in anticipa-
tion of her arrival.
Captain Clare, whose job is to teach the Westralians
naval defence, is here for his daughter’s wedding. It
occurs on Saturday. His future son-in-law is Mr. E.
Angove, offspring of the late vigernon-medico of Tea
Tree Gully. By the way, Mr. and Mrs. Carl Angove
are moving from North Adelaide to Renmark, where
an off-shoot of the business is to be established.
The Bosapquets had a tea and tennis party at
Marble Hill on Saturday, and a luncheon party on
Sunday.
The Adelaide Rowing Club Swimming Carnival
happened in the Glenelg baths on Saturday. Mr. E.
Luxmoore won most of the prizes. The successes of
this young man are getting monotonous. Mr C H
Moon won the test race of half a mile, and will con-
sequently represent S.A. in the Australian champion-
ship swim.
Mr. C. A. .Harnilton, who came second in the
Junior Championship, leaves for Melbourne shortly.
He was given a farewell dinner bv the Club at the
Pier Hotel on the evening of the carnival.
Four of our brightest burglars broke into a grocer’s
shop in Hyde Park on Friday night. After expend-
mg a good deal of energy, dynamite, etc., they were
mortified to find the safe empty. (Loud curses.)
J hev thereupon burst into the grocer’s private house,
the first thing of value which thev encountered here
was the grocer’s wife, who yelled. ’Hie burglars bolted
in wild disorder. Someone hurled a mallee-root at
the largest villain and hit the smallest one (who
was some 15 yards from the other) so hard that he
was observed to totter in his tracks. All the police
have to do now is to find a malefactor nursing an
injured skull. 6
Premier Denham called on us during the week and
told us how we had gone ahead during the last 25
years. He was thereupon fed (1) by the Ministry at
Parhament House, and (2) by the Commonwealth
( luh. There is this point about being a Premier one
need never go hungry. ’

POLITICAL
POINTS
_ Talking about the sinfulness of Protec-
tion, the Argus points out that Germany
last year took £6,000,000 more Australian
goods than Australia took of German goods.
The old dame then maunders:
What must be said of Germany’s conduct in import-
ing from this country such a very much larger amount
of merchandise than it imports from her? To think
that a nation intelligent enough to accept the sound
and comforting creed of High Protection should act
so foolishly!
The Argus doesn’t mention —perhaps it
doesn’t know —-that practically the whole of
the German imports from Australia consist
of raw products. Of wool alone the German
is now taking over £6,000,000 worth a year
from Australia. Enlightened Australia has
the tremendous job of clipping the stuff from
the sheep’s back: the benighted German
chuckles as he piles it aboard his own
steamers, spins it into tweed at his factories,
and then sells it to the Commonwealth for
about 20 times as much as he paid for it.
CLEARING THE TRACE.
Intervention by the Federal power in such circum-
stances was laying the axe at the root of the tree of
State sovereignty. —An indignant Fusionist on
Referendum possibilities.
MAN WITH THE COACH: “ Keep calm , little
one , keep calm. There's a limb or two that
have long been an aggravation and a hind-
rance to my progress , and I'm thinking of
trimming 'em down a bit and"
WEE VOICE (frantically): “ All very fine , all
very fine! But you COULD hack this
beautiful tree down with that horrid weapon
if you WANTED to, so please throw it away
at once!"
Concerning one of 1 ictoria's political has-
beens, a Melbourne daily lately printed the
following pair of headlines : “Alexander Pea-
cock. Remains in State House.” No doubt.
Still there are others who are even deader.
kat got something of a shock at a recent
by-election for Melbourne City Council. He
polled 1623 property owners’ votes, given to
a city iron merchant, while the Labor candi-
date, an almost unknown letter-cutter named
Cohen, scored 1306. It moves.
i- T s?^^^ a ? ian maternity bonus has called
loi ±113,000 in three months. In the same
three months two of the State Premiers and
a third Deputy-Premier have packed their
duds tor London, where they will bite Cohen’s
ear for further loans. What a farce it is to
give the little Australian a bonus with one
hand and a terrific burden of debt with the
other!
Following the lead of the Age, the Bendigo
Advertiser says that “the Opposition union
ot 1 rotectionists and Freetraders was an act
of sophistication, which debased the purity of
I rotectionist representation.” Quite true, of
course. Now, to come right home, what is
the Advertiser going to do about John
Quick, its debased Protectionist represen-
tative?
From a Northern Democrat:—
If Queensland experience goes for anything, it is
much, too early yet for Beeby to crow. The Labor
party s “whole hog’’ policy of helping the Tory who
bates the Labor programme with all his soul, instead
ot assisting the democrat who believes in 90 per cent,
of that programme, has been tried now for six years
in Queensland, with the result that the Democrats
who nearly always voted Labor have been wiped out.
Incidentally, the Tory majority has increased enorm-
ously, and the present Conservative crowd in power
Jo°ks^,]ik e Ly to remain there for many a long day.
But that fact doesn’t trouble the extreme Labor men
They have got the “whole hog’’ policy and their op-
ponents have got the bacon. What more could a
party want ?
Ihe Y\ omen s National League has snared
a courageous but foolish man named Pur-
brick, and persuaded him to contest the
Yarra in the Fusion interest. It would
probably do Frank Tudor good to be strenu-
ously, opposed, and might show him that
association with Freetraders corrupts good
politics; but Purblind isn’t the man for the
job. He means well, but he overlooks things,
lhus he rails at Labor for failing to provide
adequate protection for “existing local in-
dustries”—not a word about the much more
important industries that have no Austra-
lian existence as yet—but he conveniently
forgets that his party includes many Free-
traders, and that the other day a majority of
his party, in secret caucus, chose a Free-
trader as leader in preference to a Protec-
tionist.
From behind the scenes:—
In the election of a leader of the white-ant riddled
Fusion, Jokook polled 20 against Bigjohn’s 19. Out
of 46 all told, 39 turned up (Cameron, the Tas-
manian Senator, having refused to sign the Liberal
caucus pledge, has been struck off the roll.) J. T.
Walker and Simon Fraser, who are quitting politics,
did not attend. Josh Symon said nothing and didn’t
come either. Greene and Harper were ill. Banana-
land Edwards, also retiring, thought it didn’t con-
cern him. Deakin hadn’t the same scruples ; he was
there. Millen was Joe’s chief henchman, with Willie
Kelly as bottle-holder. Not until the chairman de-
clared the count was the result sure. N. S. Wales
polled seven for Joe and two for John, Victoria four
against eight, Queensland four to one. Westralia
threw all its votes for John, and the S. Aus. and Tas-
manian voting was equal.
During the sparring before the second
ballot for the Blayney (N.S.YV.) seat, event-
ually recaptured by Beeby, the oldest in-
habitant of a hamlet called Vittoria was
unearthed. The ancient told the story of an
election of 30 years ago, when the lonely
place, which only had 20 names on its roll,
was overrun with vehicles loaded with grog
and electors from distant parts. Vittoria
had missed the proper election day through
the non-attendance of the returning officer,
so the whole countryside rallied to the attack
regardless of rolls or anything else. Dead
men rose from their graves to vote at that
election, live ones were dragged from their
beds for the same purpose, and £2OO worth
of liquor was swallowed. When 500 or so
votes had been honorably recorded, the booth
was closed with a screwjack or something,
and the heated multitude tore down the
fences, and had a sort of pitched battle in
the dusk. His story done, the oldest inhabi-
tant sighed regretfully, and said that it was
a great day for the village, and the country
had now gone to the dogs.
In view of the remote possibility of a
M ade-Wood Ministry coming *to power after
next N.S.W. election, two Disraelis are mak-
ing strong running to be in it —Dan Levy
and J. J. Cohen. Both have the main essen-
tial for Cabinet position, viz., an apparently
safe seat, and both have a record for inor-
dinate talkativeness which is only excelled
by Wood and Fitzparrot on their own side.
Levy had a brilliant public school and a fair
University and law school record, but has
since faded away. Cohen is a strenuous man,
good tempered, not much of a debater, but
very persistent; and he has a weakness for
believing that he resembles Barton—facially,
of course. He came into prominence as a
whole-souled fighter for Federal union; but
he has tailed off since then into a confirmed
State Frighter—a melancholy result of get-
ting himself down to the intellectual level of
a suburban electorate where there are 14
pocket-handkerchief municipalities in about
two square miles of Austx-alian territory.
“The Wild Colonial Pressman”: I never
attend a political banquet without recalling
that inspiration of Goldsmith’s : “Those who
came to scoff remained to bray.”
“Politicus”: I was talking recently to one
of our newly-arrived immigrants about things
in the Cold Land. “People are leaving in
thousands,” he said. “The thing that riles
a Britisher is, not so much that want of
employment is the chief cause of this awful
loss of citizens, but that Continental im-
ports choke the market. The day our vessel
left. I saw at Tilbury Docks a cargo of 2000
coffins landed direct from Germany.” Ex-
porting men and importing coffins! Free-
trade in a nutshell!
Austin Chapman’s flank action in turning
up at the Liberal Caucus to help elect
Deakin’s successor was expected—by a few.
Last election Chapman was unopposed in
token of Labor’s appreciation of the Chap-
man grain sack. This coming election,
Labor has announced its intention to contest
the seat, so Chapman, still a sick man, rec-
koned things would be made easier if he
came down from the fence and collected the
support of one party or the other. He chose
that one whose device represents the Aus-
tralian being slowly smothered by the im-
ported goods of the‘sweated toiler of Europe,
Asia and America.
On an illuminated hoarding erected on the
site of the future Commonwealth Building
at the eastern end of the Strand, London,
George Reid displays “Australia’s Daily
Message.” This consists of a sentence in
movable wooden letters setting forth some
striking item of news calculated to hit the
passer-by in the eye and encourage him to
emigrate. Sometimes the High Billposter
seems to get gravelled for matter, and puts
up queer assertions. For instance, on
December 10, he proclaimed: “The small
percentage of applicants for the maternity
bonus is an indication of Australia’s re-
markable prosperitv.” It might be an in-
dication of a lot of other things as well —
of maternal bashfulness, of ignorance as to
the modus operandi, of false pride, or of
pure cussedness. But, as a simple matter of
fact, the “small percentage” isn’t true. It
is the large percentage that is surprising the
Government, in view of the preliminary
howl about the “degradation” of the
“bonus.”
THE SAME OLD ASS.
He warned them not to be led away by the new
dress; for, after all, it was the same old donkey.—
Premier Peake, on the new Referenda proposals.
(THE BULLETIN trusts it hasn't misunderstood
Mr. Peake.)
Customs Minister Tudor is trying to dis-
courage the use of flannelette by displaying
placards at the A.N.A. Melbourne Hygienic
Exhibition, and by authorising practical de-
monstrations of its inflammability. This
paper may live to yet see punishment for
murder abolished in favor of lessons on the
deadness of murdered corpses.
In the matter of Defence the Tory coat
seems to be completely inside out at last.
Says the S. M. Herald :
We are not always able to see eye to eye with
the present Federal Government, but in matters of
Defence it has shown a most enlightened attitude.
The direct reason for this generous outburst
is the decision to get men from the British
army to be instructors. The moment the
notion of importing something penetrated the
Tory mind, round went the coat, with all its
lining showing.
The fighting platform of the Jokook party
is as vague as the policy of a party of odds
and ends might be expected to be. It sets
forth: —
(X) That the policy of the present Commonwealth
tariff, as determined by the electors, be maintained.
(The match-makers, thrown out of the closed-down
factory, will be glad.)
(2) A permanent non-political body to be estab-
lished, having statutory authority to supervise and
report to Parliament respecting industrial production,
etc.
(3) Restoration of Responsible Government. (This
means, no doubt, abolition of the caucus. It was a
caucus that elected Joe Cook.)
(4) Sound finance. (J. Cook learned this brand of
finance no doubt from his former chief, G. H. Reid.)
(5) Electoral reform, to secure the better represen-
tations of majorities. (Hooray ! This means Unifica-
tion, if it means anything.)
(6) Industrial legislation, to obtain inter-State uni-
formity as far as is equitable. (And note how hard
the Jocook party barracks for the Referendum pro-
posals, which are designed to get it.)
(7) Old age pensions to be retained. (Marvellous
magnanimity.)
(8) National insurance. (And don’t mention the
Socialistic tiger.)
(9) Government and development of the Territories
on sound and progressive lines. (Which mav mean
anything, from colored labor to shindycatism ■ or
nothing at all.)
Why doesn’t the party frankly boil it all
down into a banner with the true device -
“On to the Tartshop!”

Colonel Owen, Kink O’Mal-
ley’s Director - General of
Works, is the latest champion
of the Cotter —the river where
the trout are suffocated with
mud and captured by hand.
Says the quite disinterested
Owen : “There is enough water
going down the Cotter to sup-
ply with storage a city of the
size of Melbourne.” But, then,
dear, dusty Melbourne is al-
ready supplied from other
sources, so there is no danger
of the Cotter being put to the
test. These large assertions by
the river’s champions would be
listened to with more respect if
those painfully frank fishing
correspondents who wrote of
strings of waterholes and chok-
ing fish had been contradicted,
or sacked, or boiled. A few
impressive drowning statistics
even might moisten the outlook.
But no such evidence of wet-
ness has been produced. Per-
haps G.-G. Denman might be
able to _ silence the critics by
ceremoniously drowning a dog
when he goes along to christeD
the place.
The Maoriland Telegraph
Department recently held a
sale of old stock in Christ-
church. Table telephones were knocked
down at 6d. each ; wall telephones averaged
a shilling. Now what price will the railway
stock, on which Cohen’s interest must still
be paid, realise when invention has kept
moving for another 20 years? Mechanical
property is no permanent asset these days.
The Right-thinking section of the com-
munity will be glad to hear that the Hobart
cemetery which recently adopted the sanitary
practice of digging up partly-decayed corpses
and burning them, was run by Private Enter-
prise. By comparison, how wasteful is the
average municipal depot! If a grave gets
full —why, the authorities nearly always in-
sist upon the sexton digging another one.
This paper has often said that a certain
variety of Liberal is, at heart, a violent
anarchist, and the older it gets the surer it
becomes of the fact. For example, last
week the Liberal party in the Common-
wealth Parliament met in Caucus to elect a
leader. Before, leaving for Melbourne to
take part in this business, Colonel Ryrie,
M.P. (whose speciality, according to himself,
is the capacity to administer stoush like any
hottle-o lad) was reported by the Sydney
dailies as follows :
If there is any move to do Mr. Cook out of the
leadership there will be trouble at that meeting.
And that trouble will come from me, and I can tell
you that I can break up any political meeting in the
world.
No. wonder that frail lightweight, W. 11.
Irvine, hastened to announce that he had no
intention of “doing Mr. Cook out of the
leadership” of the push, and that rather than
be suspected of anything of the sort he would
withdraw from the fray.
TO WORK!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“And now,” said he, “to work!” —Sydney D. T.
interview with Joe Cook, 24/1/T3.
“And now to work!” says Cook. Yes, yes! we like
this cheery bustle.
The time is ripe and over-ripe—’tis time to doff
our coats.
Come,- light the fire and start the wheels, for we have
got to hustle,
Or else the busy foreigner will cut our trading
throats !
Take down the shutters, speed the loom, and set
the chimneys smoking ;
Let engines hum and waggons roll, and whistles
call to toil;
When drags the Symphony of Work, ’tis time to stop
our joking
And start the clashing music with a touch of
honest oil.
“And now to work!” You bet we will! We’re sick
and tired of talking!
It’s time to get a move on now 1 Let’s mend that
Tariff wall!
But what is this ? Our rushing boss has stopped !
Our Joe is baulking!
This sudden, frenzied jibbing we can’t understand
at all!
He called for “Work” ! He also seemed impatient to
be doing—■
And, Heaven knows, there’s work enough to keep
him on the wing!
But in a corner see him now his Freetrade whiskers
chewing—-
O Joe, where is thy energy! O Cook, where is
thy spring!
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The inter-State 18ft. handicap on Port
Jackson (N.S.W.) on Saturday afternoon was as
exciting as any ever seen on that famous water.
Twenty-three faced the starter on a balmy, easy
breeze; but long before the race was over a wild
southerly burst over them, snapping masts like car-
rots, rolling boats right over, and in some cases
blowing masts and gear right out and overboard.
Of the 23 that set out only one crossed the line, and
that one was the Westana, the Perth boat which
had run second to Kismet when N.S.W. won back
the championship and the Foy Cup the previous Sat-
urday. When so experienced a master of the game
as Christian Webb gets blown right over in the Har-
bor he knows better than anyone else on earth, it
speaks volumes for the Westralian captain that
he lived through the terrific wind burst, and
crossed the line, sole survivor as well as victor.
Another boat which made a splendid effort was the
Queensland craft Colonial. Golding, with peak low-
ered, struggled up to Clark Island buoy, followed by
Australian and Colonial, in that order. The first
two approached it as the tailor did the sac coat,
cautiously, and played for safety by going in stays.
Colonial came with a rush, bravely, jibed and swung
the sail across amidst a thunder of cheers that
drowned the howling of the gale, and got round
safely. But Fate was waiting, and the northern boat
was plunging past Bradley's Head, when one sudden
gust lifted mast and sails right overboard; and the
plucky boat was a wreck. But wrecks were plentiful
that afternoon.
The gale that blew across Sydney Harbor
on the Saturday completelv wrecked the Sabbath
which followed it, for the fleet of 18-footers which
was smashed and scattered by 5 p.m. on Saturday,
came up smiling, spick and span, in spite of snapped
masts and all the rest of it, at the Anniversary Re-
gatta on Monday. Two inter-State handicaps were
run off, and Australian, which on Saturday was blown
right over, won both, and thereby healed the broken
heart of the veteran Christian Webb. In the second
race he had to go for all he was worth, and it is
doubtful if he would have got home even then had not
Kismet, through piling on another ounce, capsized
just as it leaped for the line. It was an exciting
finish.
* * * *
While the 18ft. squadron was being blown
to pieces on Sydney Harbor, excitement was begin-
ning to simmer in Perth over the 14ft. and 10ft.
inter-State events which had been set down for Anni-
versary Day. Monday found a stiff breeze blowing
over the Swan River when 10 starters lined up for
the 14ft. affair. The Sydney boat Rene, running
from scratch, slowly overhauled the fleet, passed
them one by one, and struggled out a winner with
15sec. to spare from Crusader, with Edna third ;
both of these were scratch boats. In the 10ft. tussle
the breeze was over-strong, and the local Britannia
and a N.S.W. boat called Wheatear were blown over.
Again it was a Sydney boat, the Waratah, which
won, and did it with the handsome margin of five
minutes from four others.
* * * *
A curious coincidence at the Sydney Anni-
versary Regatta was the ending of the race between
the cutters from the cadet sh’p Tingira. Three of
them raced from Bradley’s Head to the flagship, and
at the finish No. 1 cutter was first, No. 2 second
and No. 3 third.
**. * *
As expected, the detailed accounts of
Gunn’s record-breaking motor-bike run from Svdney
to Melbourne prove it to have been something in the
derring-do line of business. His actual running time
was about 23 hours, and the 24 hours record was
spoiled only by the hideous state of the road after
leaving Benalla, where sand drifts and broken timber
produced many falls, and the constant use of the ex-
haust lever caused too much oil to work past the
piston rings and foul the sparking plug. During the
last stages Gunn suffered severely in his wrists, which
gave signs of losing all power owing to the strain of
heavy falls and the vibration. When he left Sydney
at two o’clock on the Saturday morning, he was ac-
companied by .T. Booth, another Melbourne rider who
had previously been over the road, which Gunn had
not. Booth smashed up between Yass and Gundagai,
and Gunn went on alone. At Winton, where he
arrived somewhere before 8 p.m., 135 miles from Mel-
bourne, he found two cars, representing the Dunlop
and Vacuum Oil Companies, waiting for him. The
fierce light of their acetylene lamps alone made riding
over some parts of the road possible. It was 3.55
a.m. when he arrived at Melbourne Post Office, after
being exactlv 23 hours in the saddle. Booth, after
his smash., fixed up his machine and went in pursuit
of his friend, and arrived at Albury an hour after
the friend had left. Then he abandoned all attempt
on the record.

D. Campbell, who has been heard of before
with his 40-li.p. Vinot, proposes to make another
attack on G. G. White’s Sydney-Melbourne motor-car
record during February. And our beauteous artistic
motorist, Allen Doone, who has likewise been heard of
before, will also undertake the task in a 40-h.p.
National. White’s record, by the way, is 19hr.
47min. This record-breaking business is a splendid
idea—for the people who sell motor-cars.
The cycle car has made its appearance in
Australia, and a weird-looking little contraption it is.
But, presumably, like-D.T.’s. they run in all shapes.
The first that appeared in Sydney nearly caused one
worthy citizen to take a fit. He was of a party
returning from a scampering jamboree on the Blue
Mountains, and the car, a big, high-powered Fiat,
had just turned into George-street from the Newtown-
road. Suddenly the worthy citizen heard a sort of
coughing sound, and, looking over the side, he beheld
a tiny car running on small bicycle wheels, and con-
taining two people, who seemed to be quite comfort-
able. He stared at it; then .softly closed his eyes,
and stared again. It was. still there, coughing
merrily along. The citizen leaned over the front
and gasped into the chauffeur’s ear: “Shay, ole
fellow, don’t maksh pace too hot; this thing’s had
er pup, an’ poor pup ’as been boarded by two louts.
Stopsh and sling ’em out—or don’ go too fast.”
Then he fell back among the upholsterv, and held a
conversation with himself about the high pitch to
which motor-cars had been brought. Every few
seconds he would look over the side at the pup. He
nearly took the fit when he saw it slither down a side
street and lose its parent.
The Melbourne Six-Days’ Bicycle Race
will be held on the Exhibition track from February
24 to March 1, inclusive. The Melbourne Bicycle
Club is the promoter, and, between drinks, it will
run off the Austral AVheel Race and some other sprint
events. A third of the gross takings is to be ear-
marked for prize money ; the Austral is expected to
be about £l5O, and the first prize of the Six Days’
should work out at something between £4OO and
£SOO if the club’s expectations are fulfilled. Only
14 teams will be accepted, and entries close with the
L.V.W., 243 Collins-street, Melbourne, February 17.
* * * *
A boon and a blessing to motorists lias
just been put on the market in the shape of a vacuum
cleaning attachment, operated by the exhaust gases
passing through a suitably designed nozzle and
vacuum chamber. As the gases rush through they
create a suction in the hose as they leave the nozzle
and pass the vacuum chamber, and dirt and dust are
picked up by the cleaning brush and fired into the
atmosphere through the exhaust tube —just the bar-
ber’s hand spray over again in a rather elaborate
form. The apparatus is installed in the same man-
ner and placa as the ordinary muffler cut-out valve,
by cutting out a short section of the exhaust pipe
ahead of the muffler. All the time the engine is run-
ning the cleaner can be working, so that at the end
of a long run the machine should arrive clean and
free from dust in the parts that matter. After that
the chauffeur and his charges just line up, the ap-
pliance is run over them also, and they march into
the pub, clean, wholesome, spick and span. A great
idea!
When Gunn set up the new motor-cycle
record between Sydney and Melbourne, he beat by
seven minutes the time in the Dunlop Military
Despatch ride last Easter. Instead of scaring off
competitors, Gunn’s gruelling performance has stimu-
lated a small army to make an attempt on the gold
watch offered by the Dunlop Cos. to the first man to
do the trio in 24 hours. Also, a voice has flown over
from South Aus. asking the co. to have the prize and
conditions made applicable to a ride from Adelaide
to Melbourne. The distance is practically the same,
and, although they are very different, the difficulties
of both runs are about equal. Anyhow, they are
both the devil, wherefore, according to Euclid, they
must be the same. Q.e.d. The co. has decided that
if the watch cannot be won between Sydnev and Mel-
bourne by the end of this month, the offer will be
extended to the Adelaide-Melbourne run.
“Hermes”: The man who has been hard
hit by the depredations of the petrol-making pirates
may begin to take heart of grace. There is now dis-
tinct hope of a cheap substitute in the form of benzol,
which is obtained from coal. When one remembers
that in England alone 70,000.000 gallons of petrol
are used every year, one begins to get a glimmering
of the harvest the petrol pirates have made. By the
Del Monte process benzol can be obtained so cheaply
that it can be retailed at lOd. per gallon. One col-
liery in England is laying down a plant to produce
7,000.000 gallons per annum; and others are about
to follow suit. Benzol is not only cheaper than
petrol, but it is more powerful. No doubt, immedi-
ately danger threatens, the petrol pirates will lay
themselves out to crush the new industry by the
usual Trust methods; but they will find themselves
faced by a very strenuous opposition. If only
15,000,000 tons of the annual English coal output of
265,000,000 tons is devoted to benzol, no petrol need
be imported to Great Britain. The same thing
applies to Australia; N.S.W. alone could produce
enough benzol to make importations of petrol un-
necessary. A thumping duty on the spirit would
throw practically the whole of the money now sent
abroad into Australian pockets, and create a fairly
big industry.

Another Sydney-to-Melbourne Reliability
Test for motor-cars is looming on the horizon. The
N.S.W. Automobile Club approves of it, and so does
the Victorian body. The N.S.W. crowd is in favor
of a four days’ contest over the old route; the Vic-
torian club favors a five days’ tussle via the South
Coast, through Bega, Cooma and Tumut. The latter
has the advantage of being a far more picturesaue
run. Also, on account of the hilly nature of the
country, it would provide a much better test. There
is also a chance that a team competition, N.S.W. v.
Victoria, will be included in the outing.
A women’s rowing-club in Adelaide, call-
ing itself the Original, claims to be the first in Aus-
tralia to go in for inter-State rowing, feminine
variety. It has sent a four to Melbourne for the
Albert Park regatta of this week. The names may
go on record: Moyes (stroke), two Sullivans and
Beada. All are under 9st.
* * * *
“0.K.”: An Ararat (Vic.) hotelkeeper
has triumphantly asserted his right to bet his cus-
tomers “something to nothing” on a horse race. The
charge was that he, on his own licensed premises, did
issue coupons undertaking to pay £1 to any thirsty
prophet who succeeded in placing the first three horses
of the Melbourne Cup. Five customers won the “quid,”
one of them marking two coupons correctly, and get-
ting payment for each. For the prosecution, it was
contended that the customers practically paid the
price of a drink for each coupon, wherefore the land-
lord was betting them £1 to 6d., or £1 to a “tray”
that they couldn’t place ’em for the Cup. The de-
fence argued that the customers paid just the ordin-
ary price for their liquor. The local Bench decided
in favor of the defence, which decision seer-wth
good. The drink was bought for ordinary drinking
purposes; it represented the same usual value for
cash as any other drink supplied at the pub. On the
other hand, the landlord was conducting neither a
sweep nor a raffle, nor was he giving a guarantee that
he would pay away any money in prizes. He was
certainly not infringing the law that suppressed the
coupon system in shops, where every coupon issued
had a definite value. As for the argument that the
pub-keeper was a gambler in the same degree as a
bookmaker, the latter sells only a wager, which will
probably be worthless to the purchaser. The pub-
keeper sold a drink at its recognised price, and threw
his wager in. There must be two parties to a gam-
bling transaction, and in this case the customers
didn’t gamble at all. Their grog merchant bet them
“something to nothing.” Everyone is licensed to
do that. A bookmaker might do it on the steps
of a cathedral without breaking the law. P.S. —-
Against the decision of this Court there is no appeal.
James Wattie, one of Maoriland’s fine old-
time jockeys, weighed in for the Death Stakes at
Auckland the other day. Back in 1874 he steered Tem-
pleton to victory in the Auckland Cup, and for about
10 years after that rode winners of most of the classi-
cal races. Although his riding days were long over,
he died, figuratively speaking, in a' stable.
An effort to pick winners at the A.J.C.
Anniversary meeting by meteorological sie-ns fell just
as fiat as the old baked-dumpling device. On Satur-
day, with a record temperature of 100.9, punters
pinned their faith to Hot Air in the Challenge
Stakes, but the brute failed badly. On Monday,
with a southerly blowing refreshingly, Cool Air was
heavily supported in the Flying " Handicap, but
reached only third place. Punting on horses is evi-
dently a science which cannot be solved by signs,
wonders and dreams—a fact the large-voiced Solo-
mon McTavish is thoroughly aware of.
Remittancer”: London papers announce
the death of Crudon, winner of the 1901 Grand
National at Aintree. It was the most awful race, as
regards weather and falls, I ever saw. Snow and
sleet were sweeping across the course from the sea,
and it was likewise freezing. I was wearing the
only decent fur coat I ever owned and I couldn’t
keep warm. What must have been the sensations of
the horsemen faced with the prospect of negotiating
nearly five miles of the biggest country in the world,
practically in the buff! Seasoned performers like
Arthur Nightingall and “Snip” Ripley fortified
themselves with jumping powder, in the form
of alleged old brandy, without restraint. Coventry,
the starter, went out to sample the course, having
previously announced that he meant to give it a
clean bill of health whatever it looked like. The
prevailing hope on the part of the jockeys, both
amateur and professional, was that he would break
his neck. He jumped his horse, a safe old hunter,
over a fence near the stand. The gallant animal,
having slipped in the take-off, hit hard, pecked badly,
slid about 14 feet and then by some miracle recovered
itself. Coventry cantered back, announced that the
course was in splendid order, and the horses went out.
Bletsoe, Grudon’s trainer, had spent the previous
ten minutes feverishly filling his charge’s feet with
butter. The idea was to prevent the snow caking in
them. It was a good move. Half the others fell
because their feet were unmanageable after the first
mile. Grudon, jumping faultlessly, won with con-
siderable ease, leaving a mass of fallen and dead
horseflesh in his wake. During a considerable part
of his career he worked in a milk-cart; in fact, his
owner, a farmer, first saw him between the shafts.
He bought him for £3O, and won with him a dis-
tinction which kings have striven for in vain.
“Ex-Englishman” : Re the two pars
about starting-price betting (B. 16/l/’l3), England
does most of its betting on horses it never sees;
and Australia doesn’t get the long prices about
outsiders that the papers name as on offer. The
London club-frequenter doesn’t want to be troubled
to find out what is a fair price about his fancy; and
millions —literally millions—-of factory hands, etc.,
know they have no hope of findin"' out. So the club-
frenuenter puts his fiver, and the others put their
halfcrown, or coppers, on So-and-so at starting-price.
“S.P.” have thus become about the most familiar
initials in all Britain. The sums wagered on a
course are the merest trifles compared with those
booked by big business-like offices in town,. or on
the Continent. When a 20 to 1 chance has won, the
Australian Isaac likes to have it given out that the
price was 50 to 1 ; and reporters are not too particu-
lar about verifying the figure—for what does it
matter? But in England all Isaacdom would be
eager to have the vast stay-at-home public told it
was 12 to 1. The pressman whose duty it is to
assess the odds for the one or two sporting papers
whose “S.P.” is accepted as final, is, therefore, a
tremendously important person.
Meanwhile, tricks are rampant. The one desire of
the modern owner, with a good thing not widely
known, is to rush in an immense backing commis-
sion, spread over a dozen “S.P.” offices, so close before
the race that they won’t have time to wire to the
course, lav off there, and so reduce the “S.P.” It is
no swindle, but lust Business. If he can block the
only available line by wiring the whole Book of
Genesis himself, he is only doing what war-corres-
pondents have been praised for doing. One of the
biggest recent jobs of the kind was directly aided
bv Providence, which sent such an electrical dis-
turbance all up and down the Thames valley that no
telegraph or telephone would act for half an hour,
and so the horse went out almost unbacked (on the
course). Then there are the plain swindles such as
t P p R o f. Tli e big offices receive, by wire
or letter, hundreds of bets after the race, and accept
them all, if they might reasonably have been des-
patched before it. Hence much clever fraud, and
purchase of the souls of pound-a-week post and tele-
graph officials. Nothing but the Totalisator —which
the man to be present on the course, with
the money, before the gong sounds—will wipe out all
th ; s swindle-encouraging system.

A record from “sleepy Castlemaine”
(Vic.), where H. Iredale knocked up 263 runs
(not out), in a district cricket match, in three hours
25 minutes. The district’s next best (254 not out)
was made by J. D. Edwards, at Bendigo, in 1888.
But one afternoon was not enough for him. Iredale
must know something about the game. Although
quite young, he has 23 century scores to his credit.
* * * *
It is doubtful if Trumper was ever in
better form than at present. After failing in the
southern tour with a miserable 46 in four innings
versus S.A. and Vic., he returned to Sydney and
slogged 201 against S.A., 102 in a club fixture, and
138 against Vic. But he isn’t the wizard of old.
The fancy shots are among the relics; it is in the
solid work that he is shining now.
One of the humors of the day is the effort
of a clique to drag “Mary Ann” Noble back nnto
the front rank of first-class cricket, per medium of
the Trumper benefit match. “Noble will probably
play if he is asked,” said one ingenuous scribe.
Under the circs, it was a fair thing for Noble to
offer; and if he will not offer, the selection com-
mittee should be quite able to lay its hands on
enough good players to make the match the success
all wish it to be, without crawling to anyone.
But for Armstrong’s 118 in the first inn-
ings, Victoria’s chances in the Sheffield Shield match
would have been hopeless from the first. With even
that solid foundation to work on, the southern team
smacked only 274, and of the 106 made by the rest of
the team Sundries contributed 12. Ma’s reply was
terrific. Trumper slashed up 138 and Eric Barbour
146, and the rest of the team smacked 193, making
477 for the innings. With a deficiency Of 203 on the
first innings, V ictoria started on an almost impossible
second innings. But the team was full of sand, and it
played the game. Up till Monday night not a cen-
tury had come to its rescue, yet when the stumps
were drawn 335 had been accumulated for nine wic-
kets ; so with one to fall the team had 132 in hand.
The position looked hopeless. And hopeless it was.
The last Victorian wicket saw 30 added, and the
score was as the days of the year, 365. This left
N.S.W. with only 163 to get to win. They were got
at the cost of two wickets, Macartney carrying his
bat for 76, and Bardsley doing likewise for 46. Eric
Barbour made 30 and Ivelleway and Sundries seven
and four respectively. So N.S.W. won by eight
wickets.
An interesting feature of the cycling at
Sydney Cricket Ground, on Monday night, was the
international teams race, wherein McNamara, Spears
and Don Kirkham represented Australia, while Apple-
hans, Rosseau and Jenkins rode for anew Triple
Alliance of England, France and Germany. The dis-
tance was a mile and pace was laid on. When the
pacer dropped out Rosseau took Applehans to the
front and began to carve speed. But Kirkham re-
sponded, and the Australians fastened to his wheel
and made a brave showing. As they rounded into the
straight McNamara went to the front, followed bv
Spears and then Applehans. McNamara won by a
length from Spears, who beat Applehans by inches
So Australia was on top.
Last week, or the week before, Johnson
allowed his motor-cars to be seized and sold to satisfy
a judgment, and declared himself stone motherless
broke and financially down and out. Presumably
he wanted to see how much sympathy, moral and
practical, he was likely to get. He seems to have
got none. The next news was that he had descended
upon a most exclusive resort somewhere in Wyoming
bought a charming house there, and presented it to
his latest and most beloved wife, who used to be
Lucille Cameron, as a place of refuge for her at the
birth of her half-caste child. Poor little kid!
Dick Williamson, Adelaide’s boxing boss
has thrown in the towel, and does not intend to pro-
mote contests in the future. He was disgusted over
the Doran v. Lister fight on New Year’s Night.
These two merely prodded one another for if)
minutes, and the crowd was naturally unanimous
that the men did not try. Some of the mud that
was thrown at them stuck on the promoter, and he
didn’t like it.
“Down an’ Out”: I always understood
that the habit men have of kissing one another was
restricted to Frenchmen, drunks and so forth. But
I find it isn’t. At the Melb. Athletic Pavilion, last
week, pugs. Alf Goodwin and Frank O’Grady met
and smote each other hard for 17 rounds; and bv
that time O’Grad.v was so sorely damaged that the
referee (E. Fullalove—nice name for a boxing re-
feree) ordered O’Gradv into dock, and declared Good-
win the winner. That’s all right. But when Good-
win discovered that he had won, he forgot about his
gore-stained countenance and bleeding nose, careered
up to his trainer and threw his arms round his neck
and kissed him. Then he treated his seconds to an-
other blood-stained peck apiece, and anyone who ap-
proached him to congratulate him was similarly
smeared. After all, is the “gentle art” really a
training for a Dorcas meeting?
* * * *
The ring-passing of Hock Keys, probably
the finest boxer in the world—said by those who have
seen both to be quite equal to the late Joe Gans—
took place on Saturday night at Sydnev Stadium
He was up against Frank Picato, and was giving awav
about half a stone in weight. At the first he got
all over the Italian from America, and gave one of
the best exhibitions of sparring ever seen in the
Rushcutter’s Bay ring. But early in the fight he
found out that his blow's made no impression upon
the gritty youth before him. Poor Kevs"never had
much of a punch, and he had lost whatever he once
had. Once or twice when Picato rushed in he met
straight lefts which had some sting in them, but the
sting came from Picato’s own rush. In the fifth
Picato caught Keys on the point of the chin, and
Keys all but fell; but when Picato stood in to finish
him, his marvellous skill enabled him to block a
shower of blows till the gong w'ent. The feature of
the fight was Keys’s defence. Over and over again
Picato got him on the ropes and slogged away, only
to have every blow taken on gloves and arm. Yet
it was rather a pitiful exhibition. Kevs was quite
incapable of offensive action, and could only defend
himself by interposing gloves and arms to' Picato’s
blows. Picato proved himself to be the most generous
opponent, and was obviously determined not to
knock out a man who was palpably done, though
he could have smashed him over and over again.
He vvas quite content to win on points, and merely
sent in blows which would count without domg Keys
anv great harm. Keys was given every chance to
pull himself together, but at the end of the 12th
round he saw his case was hopeless and the towel
fluttered into the ring as the gong sounded for the
resumption of hostilities.
* * * *
Monday was a perfect day for surf-bathing
m Sydney, but before long the beaches at Manly
and Bondi were crowded with people whom wild
horses wouldn’t drag into the water. For the coast
was being raided by that variety of Portuguese man-o’-
war known as the blue-bottle. The waves were
loaded with them, and the surfers gave up bathing
and smashed the nuisances with sticks and boots, and
buried them in the sand. Some of the children were
bad] v stung. It begins to look as though the surfer
will .have to dress from neck to heel to avoid the
raverting monsters ; and the presence of broken bottles
on our .sand beaches makes the wearing of shoes
almost imperative. The man who breaks class or
china on any beach should first be dragged over it at
the tail of a donkey, then heavily fined, and finally
jugged for three months.
Dan Green, of Sydney, achieved a wonder-
tul sporting feat the other day, and nobody was more
surprised than the striker. Dan has been going in for
un?* a t Albert Park (Melb.), and a phenomenal
was ambition. He realised his ambition
with one fell practice swipe. The ball’s flight was in-
terrupted by a swooping swallow and the bird fell
dead. The old story of the cricket ball that killed a
sparrow can consider itself snuffed out.

Not looking for trouble, but pos-
sessing that quiet confidence to look
after yourself when trouble does
come your way.
You admire the pluck of a man who
takes a beating from the experienced
boxer. But do you admire his awk-
wardness, or the way he leaves him-
self open, or how he punches the air,
or the blackened eyes that advertise
his downfall for days afterwards? Or
do you admire watching the man who
plants his blows where they sting,
who evades wild rushes, and who
comes out of the fiay unscathed?
Which part do you play? And
which part are you going to play in
future?

Lieut. Petre has just arrived in Melbourne
as instructor of the Australian Flying Corps
at £400 a year. The chief is Major White,
who will be Director of Intelligence. The
second instructor, Lieut. Harrison, a native
of Victoria, will shortly arrive from England
with four aeroplanes, purchased for £3200.
Harrison formerly worked in Melbourne as
a motor mechanic; he went to England and
learned flying on the advice of Busteed, an-
other Australian, now one of the best-known
airmen in England. The Australian flying
corps will consist of four officers, seven
warrant-officers and sergeants, and 32
mechanics, or a total of 43. The aerodrome
is to built at the Duntroon training centre,
within Federal capital territory.
A new edition of “Ironbark’s” verses is
to come along in a few weeks. “Ironbark,”
when he isn’t writing his cheerful lines, is
G. H. Gibson, an inspector in the N.S.W.
Lands Department for 17 years past. But
he has been a lot of other things. Until
his distaste for the law got the better of
him he was a solicitor in England; then,
starting to rove, he wandered through Maori-
land and over the backblocks of Queensland
and N. S. Wales, where he put in six years
learning bushcraft. From there he drifted
into press work in Sydney; he started to
contribute to The Bulletin with its first
issue in 1880. He now has three volumes
of verse to his credit, and the lot that will
appear in “Ironbark Splinters” are the best
he has done.

What especially distinguishes
Odol from all other mouth-cleansing prepara-
tions is its unique antiseptic property.
After the mouth has been rinsed with Odol
an all prevading influence spreads itself im-
perceptibly over the whole of the interior of
the mouth, exerting its power for hours.
It is this lasting effect (which no other pre-
paration possesses) that gives to daily users
of Odol the absolute assurance that their
mouths are permanently protected against the
processes of fermentation and decomposition
which, if not guarded against, inevitably
destroy the teeth.
Arnold Bennett, who wrote “Milestones” in colla-
boration with Edward Knoblauch, was originally a
shorthand clerk in a solicitor’s office. He contributed
to Tit Bits in his leisure hours, and, winning several
prizes, was enticed to go in for journalism. While he
was assistant-editor of a feminine weekly, Woman, he
turned out several novels of prodigious length. But
he will not do it again. He has discovered that plays
which run to only 30 printed pages pay a million
per cent, better.
“Haresfoot”: The “Prize Drama” business makes
me think of an anecdote about Eugene Scribe and De
Musset. Scribe (to De M.) : “I cannot make out
why the public relishes your comedies more than
mine. I am celebrated most for a strict adherence
to the rules of the dramatist, whereas you defy
them.” De Musset: “The public like me because I
write to please myself, whereas you write to please
them.” The aspiring playwright has the public too
much in his eye. “Look into thy heart and write,”
says Emerson. The worst drawback to Pinero is his
knowledge of the stage. He would have gone higher
without it. He is only a Sardou—not so good, in
my opinion—when he might have been a Sheridan
Knowles. I coolly say Shakspeare did not write for
the stage. His plays were nearly all twice too long
for the Globe Theatre, where the performances lasted
only two hours, as he says in a prologue.
Ihe old-established “first part” of vaudeville dies
hard. Upon Melb. Opera House presenting
a few good artists in distinct turns, the
Gaiety followed along the same lines; but
“Billo” and his “tom” have found the change
little to their liking. So the old order again
reigneth. Lee and Rial were the first to break
away in Melbourne, some 10 years ago, at the Bijou,
and Harry Rickards followed suit. But as soon as
the rival show moved on, ’Arry went back to his be-
loved “first part.” He had a good nose for his pub-
lic s requirements.
“10-2-4”: She was a small, flat-chested woman,
with a reddish nose and the sort of false teeth which
sway with every wind that blows. She sat near me,
watching Apollo Barnstorm in “The Beauty Actor.”
“Is he not divine?” she murmured, turning a watery
brace of eyes upon her companion, a sympathiser of
her own sex and species. “Do you know that, though we
have never even met, and though my family has
always been perfectly respectable, yet. a couple of
years ago, had it not been for Emmanuel’s unvarying
kindness to me and the children” (dramatic pause)
“I should have given myself to that man,”
Shakspeare’s alleged “Troilus and Cressida” has
been given a few performances in London by the
Elizabethan Stage Society. It is poor stuff, though,
and seems to have been revived only once before in
its 300 years of existence.
From London: G. R. Sims has again provided the
book of the Drury Lane pantomime—“ The Sleeping
Beauty. Sims has now had 50 years’ experience
of play-going and play-writing, and “has seen a whole
mountain-range of theatrical ups and downs. He
remembers the manager of a AVest End house who,
m rejecting a play, mentioned that he had read the
snycopsis.’ The last time Sims saw that manager
he was wheeling a barrow into the Bethnal Green
market. Another manager whom Sims knew very
well began life as a restaurant waiter. After being
for many years prosperous, with a town and a coun-
try residence, etc., he sank back to his original
position, and when Sims last met him he was a
waiter on board a penny steamer on the Thames.
“Lud”: G. W. Smalley, friend of both, took Sarah
Bernhardt to see Irving in “The Bells,” at the
Lyceum, in London. After the show the three supped
together in the Beefsteak Club Room. Sarah: “What
is that painting on the wall?” Irving: “Kean, as
Sir Giles Overreach, in his great situation, touching
the electric chain with which we’re darkly bound, as
Byron says.” Sarah: “You touched it in my soul to-
night. Your Mathias is infinitely more powerful than
Coquelin’s, though not so true to Nature.” Irving:
“I know it. A man could never have lived at such
a tension for a course of years. No, not for a month.
Mathias is a thing forced on me by the public. I
want them to judge me by Hamlet.” Sarah: “Thus
I feel with regard ta Fedora, Theodora and La Tosca.
Judge me by Phedre. Do you know I’m studying
Hamlet, now you have cast it off?” Irving: “No,
no; the young Lord Hamlet cast me off.” Sarah:
“I will never consider myself too old for Hamlet.
Look at Moliere’s Mdlle. de Brie. ‘No,’ said the
public, ‘you shall not even give up the ingenue at
60.’ She wanted to play old women, but the people
said, ‘What are you doing—Child?’ ”
Negro minstrelsy was to be revived on a colossal
scale at the London Palladium- on Boxing Day, with
a troop of no less than 100 performers—singers,
dancers, comedians, coons, banjoists, etc., recruited
from the United States and the old-time Mohawks
and Moore and Burgess burnt-cork combinations. The
music-hall killed negro minstrelsy. After the
variety and vivacity of the halls, the semi-circle of
solemn-looking black-faced gents singing a lot of
sentimental and a few comic songs, varied by mild
conundrums from the cornermen, was voted dull, and
not worth the bob. But a new public has grown up
since the old show went under, so it may have a
successful resurrection for a time.
Bernard Shaw’s latest idea is that Birmingham
ought to be disfranchised, not because it is Joe
Chamberlain’s pocket borough, but because reallv
good high-class plays—his own, for instance —
fail there. Managers lose in Birmingham the money
they make in Manchester, Liverpool, Glasgow and
Dublin. Even Forbes Robertson, whom Shaw calls
the “greatest living British actor and our only
classical one,” was unable to rouse the screw-place
from its deplorable apathy. “And Birmingham,”
exclaims Shaw, “is not ashamed of itself; it cannot
blush ; it does not even apologise ; it is brazen in its
Philistinism.” Apropos, there is now going on in
that metropolis of the English midlands an official
inquiry into the conditions under which hooks, but-
tons, needles, etc., are made. The sec. of the Bir-
mingham Trades Council declared in his evidence
that “the way in which these industries are being'
carried on is a disgrace to the city and all con-
cerned. Thousands of women and girls are paid
only a penny an hour.” How many plays could
Shaw see on that wage?
“J.F.H.”: Daring chaps, these French dramatists,
gaily tackling subjects which the English playwright
would not touch with a fork. There has just been
produced in Paris the most blushful play of the year.
It is entitled “The Good Old Custom.” What cus-
tom is that? Why, as a Parisian dramatic critic
put it, the “right of the lord of the manor in the
Middle Ages to provide his tenant’s bride with late
supper and early breakfast on the wedding day and
the day fitter.” “The Good Old Custom” will never
be staged in England or Australia. The bride was
played by one of the handsomest of French actresses,
Arlette Dorgere. And her disrobing scene !
From Paris: The Th&fitre des Varieties is putting
on “The Green Coat.” It offended the Minister for
the Argentine, with the result that Australia has to
suffer. One of the characters—a fiddler —says: “I
have been offered 100,000 francs to go to the Argen-
tine for two months and give concerts ; but I’m not
going to - risk my life among the niggers.” The
Argentine Minister protested to the French Foreign
Office against this insult to his country. The theatre
people got an ultimatum, and the play was sub-
edited —by altering “Argentine” to “Australia.” So
far George Reid has not threatened war or anything.
Brander Matthews, who has put in a good many
years as critic and story-writer, with an occasional
try at plays, and is also a Prof, of Literature, tips
that “the American drama is imnroving, and the
drama in general reviving, because the author is now
certain of his pay.” He thinks that for this reason
and others the great day of the novel is passed. It
is now in closer competition with the play, and the
latter to-day pays as well, if not better, than the
novel. Drama is the only one of the arts which de-
pends on the crowd. It cannot depend on a clique.
This is right enough, but while a clique cannot log-
roll a drama to success as easily as a novel, it can,
if opposed to a play, sometimes prevent its reaching
the stage and the crowd.
The aspiring youth wanted employment in a
theatre, and wrote to the manager: ‘‘You will see
from the enclosed newspaper extract that I have con-
siderable aptitude for the stage.” This was the
extract: “The prisoner conducted himself in a
highly theatrical manner.” The manager regretted,
as usual, that there was no vacancy.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.

Bellerive: A bush tragedy this week: —
STRUCK BY LIGHTNING.
Frowning clouds gathered,
A storm raged around,
Huge trees were uprooted
And hurled to the ground,
When a poor woodcarter
With his horse and Dray
Was struck by Lightning
On his lonely wav.
A fond wife and children
Did anxiously await
For their loved ones who
Met an appalling fate,
An electric flash of lightning
The poor carter did kill
A stone’s throw from his home
At the foot of the hill.

“Emdee” on the sacred “introduction” :
Every now and then some well-meaning cleric or
pedagogue sails in with a full cargo of scandalised
remarks about the youthful Australian’s infamous
habit of making street acquaintances. Far be it
from me to defend the dashing Knut or Pirate who
forces himself on unwilling females as a matter of
sport—this, I take it, being his economical substitute
for big-game shooting, hunting and such-like risky
diversions, dear to those who can afford them. But
I say most strenuously that there is nothing inher-
ently wrong or immoral or even undesirable in the
unconventionally formed friendship. What magic is
there in the “introduction” which is insisted on so
fiercely in middle-class circles ? None! The rela-
tions between people of opposite sexes are settled, in
every case, by the inclinations, plus the moral, social
and religious principles of the parties concerned. The
acquaintanceshin that is begun casually in a tram,
ferryboat or what not is as likely as any other to
lead to marriage—the only honest profession that is
open to fully 75 per cent, of women outside what
we call the laboring classes. A well-known city
clergyman once told me that, in a goodly proportion
of happy marriages, in his own experience, the couples
had originally met in ways that would have given the
genuine blue-nosed Wowser the cold spasms to see.
There is something senseless, not to say cruel, about
the absurd convention which restricts marriageable
women to the society of their own little set. The
effect of it is to lessen their chances of securing a
husband by about two-thirds. Last month, according
to the current London papers, a girl hanged herself.
Before doing so she opened her Bible and marked
the words of Psalm cii., 11: “My days are like a
shadow that declineth ; and lam withered like grass.”
How many are there -who do not number amongst
their friends depressing maidens who answer eloquently
to this description ? Thev have no hone, situated as
they are, of fulfilling their - natural destiny, and they
have come to realise it. Given a larger sphere, they
might find eligible youths in a similar plight to
themselves. In England, the home of social conven-
tionality, desperate attempts have been made to
solve this problem by means of marriage agencies,
agony-column advts., and other devices. They have
not proved successful, partly because such things
lend themselves naturally to the machinations of the
swindler and the adventurer, and partly because most
folks are unwilling to approach Romance through
such severely business-like channels. The best solu-
tion of an absurdly artificial system, that has had a
pernicious effect on the community in scores of ways,
is that which the ultra-moralists so furiously de-
nounce—l mean the practice of young men and
women who like the look of one another striking up
an acquaintance off-hand. It need go no further than
the girl wishes, and under favorable circumstances it
may lead her to the church door and a life of honor-
able usefulness.

GAVE HERSELF UP AS DONE.
I have been a resident of Rockhampton since
L 866, and of late years have been a sufferer from
indigestion,” says Mrs. Mary Ann Hawke, East
Street, Rockhampton (Q.). “To make a long story
short, I gave myself up &s done with this world
when I happened to take up a Daily Record, in
which I read of the cure for my complaint—Chamber-
lain s Tablets. I at once got a bottle and started to
take them. Before I had hall finished it I felt as if
I could eat and enjoy my food, which I could not
do before.”*

George Howstout Reid struck the right
setting for the usual jokes about his fat-
ness when he waddled recently through the
Chicago canneries. What would happen to
the Sly Commissioner if he suddenly became
thin and his balloon waistcoat hung about
him in folds? Would he retire from public
life until he reconstructed his jokes, or
would he go on cheekily making capital out
of his departed figure, as years ago he made
capital out of the departed fatness of Free-
trade? A flat, wheezy Reid trying to per-
suade the world that he still went four laps
to the mile, and that the old gags fitted him
as well as ever, would be one of the confi-
dence tricks of history.
“Chasuble” on the sweated toilers in the
Vineyard :
The trouble with Archbishop Clarke, who goes to
England again—the second time within five years or
so-—is that three of his family are settled in “the
dear Homeland.” and there are three grandchildren
he has not seen, which, of course, is an intolerable
hardship. Archdeacon Hindley takes the reins as
administrator. Hindley is a sort of buzzing bumble
bee, a human hurdy-gurdy, with unadorned horse-
sense in the pulpit; but he is the real motor of the
diocese. It was he who invented the three dioceses
of Bendigo, Wangaratta and Gippsland, chopped off
from Melbourne in Goe’s time. First he nervously
proposed Bendigo, and seemed on the very point of
being thrown out by the Synod ; whereupon he took
the holy men’s breath away with, “Not only that,
but two more.” The sheer audacity of it won. On
the best of authority I hear that this humble Arch-
deacon has refused six bishoprics and a trifle of two
deaneries. As an effective man, he is on a par with
the best bish. Melbourne ever had—that’s to say,
Perry, the first one. Meanwhile, there are current
doubts as to whether Clarke will stick to this diocese,
and he may be gone prospecting. He is an eminently
presentable man, fine, upstanding, like Sydney’s Bar-
ker. Asquith will be tempted to pop him in for orie
of the numerous four-thousand-a-year billets,, even as
Salisbury popped Moorhouse into Manchester. The
Melbourne screw is £2OOO ; but they don’t expect to
get the next one under £3OOO, the price of groceries
having gone up so.
. Bigjohn Forrest, who is alleged to have
just lost the leadership of the Liberal party
in the Commonwealth by one vote, had a
brother named Dave. Dave had weight, but
no oratory. Still, for the sake of the family
name, he thought he ought to run for the
mayoralty of his suburb. For some time
prior to nominating, Dave had engaged the
council in a duel concerning some Moreton
Bay fig trees which grew on his property
and shed their rubbish on the footpath. For
his campaign he roped in a pressman friend
to write an opening address. On the ap-
pointed night he appeared on the platform
with the document, and proceeded to read it,
tracing the lines with his finger as he read.
Just as he was warming up, some cantanker-
ous person yelled out, “What about the More-
ton Bay fig trees, Dave?” For a few
moments Dave struggled for a crushing plat-
form retort. Not getting it, he let himself
go in his own way. Shaking a large fist, he
shouted : “None of that sort of shenan-
ackin here, young feller.” When the uproar
had subsided, Dave realised that he had irre-
vocably lost his place, and sat down.
Lola Montez, the vivacious dancer who
toured Australia in the ’so’s, turns up again
in a book published in London under the
title of “Love Affairs of the Courts of
Europe.” Lola bewitched King Louis of
Bavaria, who made her first favorite in his
harem and created her a Countess. She prac-
tically ruled Bavaria for a year or two.
Then came a revolution, and she had to flee
to Switzerland, dressed or disguised as a
young man. After that she resumed her old
career as actress and dancer, and shook up
the goldfields of Victoria. This new book
says there has always been a mystery about
the lady’s parentage, and mentions a theory
that she was the daughter of Lord Byron
and a charwoman. Maybe. She certainly
might have been a chip off the old block.
Concerning Rothschild, the flea-collector,
the hero of recent Bulletin verse :
Rothschild, the flea-man, is brother of the Roths-
child who is the bird-and-bug man. To do them
justice, they both intend to give their collections to
the British nation. The bird-and-bug (-and-butter-
fly) collection is, of course, by far the more im-
portant of the two. It is contained in a private
museum, and includes scores of new species. The
only thing that troubles these men is that the British
Museum won’t accept such collections as collections,
the items must be dispersed into their proper classes,
A few years ago a yarn went round the papers that
the Flea Rothschild had paid a fabulous sum, —
£l°oo or so—for a Polar-bear flea. I asked about
that flea, and mentioned the story. He laughed. “I
can sell quite a lot for half-a-crown apiece,” he said.
In fact, you can have a few for a shilling.” The
flea-hunting is really a side-line with the bird and
beast hunters. Practically every animal and bird,
if it has a flea at all. has its own flea. The Roths-
childs chief collector was asked how they
pursued the flea. A policeman happened to
be passing at the moment. “Well,” said he “sup-
posing I was wanting a policeman-flea. The right
w » v tO , ?? about it would be to kill the policeman,
and put the body into a white calico bag. When the
policeman was dead and cold the fleas would quit the
body and take refuge in.the seams of the white bag.
You could then easilv muster them into a phial”
produced. The flea-king talces six of a kind and
pays 6d. a piece. It doesn’t sound much; neither do
the prices paid for rare birds—such as new birds of
paradise. Of course, these millionaire collectors occa-
sionally commission a man to find them some special
thing—-the' male, perhaps, of a species of which thev
have the female—-and that may run to a thousand
pounds. But mostly even thm’r greatest treasure hasn’t
cost more than a few shillings. The trouble is that
they have to take dozens and dozens of duplicates that
they don t want.
Dead in England, at 78. Tom Pickernell,
a famous amateur steeplechaser. A school-
fellow and friend of Adam Lindsav Gordon
he too came over here in the ’so’s, and spent
several years in Tasmania. He was back
m England by 1859, when he had his first
mount in the Grand National at Liverpool.
He rode in 17 altogether, and won three;
finished third in his last one, when aged 41
His racing name was “Mr. Thomas.”

BRISBANE.
IN
BANANA LAND
When 70 p.c. of a recent Westralian loan
had to be taken up by the underwriters, Bris-
bane Tory papers clamored exultantly about
the financial failure of a Labor Government.
The other day, 80 p.c. of Digby Denham’s
latest flotation had also to be taken up by
the underwriters —and the same old journals
bellowed themselves hoarse about the “mag-
nificent success” of the Liberal Treasurer.
A sense of humor is a fine thing.
UNNECESSARY APOLOGY.
CHOLLY: “ Oh, pway forgive me, pway weally
do, for walking on your feet.”
OLD G ENTLEMAN: “ Don’t mention it, don’t
mention it. I often walk on them my self!”
Rockhampton’s new Bishop, Dr. Sheil, will
arrive from Dublin towards the end of
March. Instead of wearing the episcopal
millinery, he nearly achieved a halo; for
some months a trying typhoid illness baffled
Dublin’s best medical skill. But he is better
now. The new prelate is Gft. 2in. in height,
list, in weight, 38 years of age, an all-
round athlete, and owns, according to all
accounts, a striking personality.

Queensland country hospitals refuse to
admit cases of venereal disease, with the re-
sult that sufferers betake themselves to the
Brisbane “General,” which interposes no such
barrier. In more than one Queensland town-
ship the fact that a man is off color and
taking a run to Brisbane is coming to have
quite a significance.
The greatest compliment ever paid the
prickly pear came from J)r. Jean White, who
is experimenting on the spiny curse at
Dulacca. This lady expert alleges that if
you apply poisonous compounds to the out 1
side of the pear, the wily weed promptly re-
sponds by thickening his skin to rhinoceros-
hide proportions. If, on the other hand, you
pierce the pear and inject a vitus, he (or
she) immediately ‘‘sets up a combination of
protoplasm with the poison, forming a com-
pound innocuous to the plant.” F’or such
resourcefulness and its fine faculty for” meet-
ing emergencies, the blackguard almost com-
pels this paper’s admiration.
P. F. Rowland, M.A., headmaster of the
Townsville Grammar School, strdck out a
new line in his annual “report.” He didn’t
worry much about the great things Thorn-
kins minor had done in the mental arith-
metic class; he rather laid himself out to
help slay the nasty notion that the North
is no place for the white man. Incident-
ally he remarked that the white man has
been steadily moving towards sunlight and
warmth, until now Norway and Sweden are
left half populated, and the Scot is thriving
best, not in his own bleak land, but in places
where the sweat-drops stand out among his
freckles.
“Speargrass,” on the vegetable curse: —
“Don Q.” (B. 9/1/’ 13), talking of the prickly-pear
pest in Gayndah and district, says -there are avenues
of pear 20ft. and more high, tor 20 or 30
miles. This is a terminological inexactitude. There
is, I believe, one bunch of pear ,25ft. high in the
Woodmillar scrub, near Gayndah/ which is looked
on as a curiosity. Of course, I don’t wish to convey
the impression that there is no pear in Gavn-
dah and district. There ig quite a fair bit
of it, but there are no avenues, and the
ordinary growth would be'more like 4ft. Local
bodies have not got Mount; Morgan to draw on ; and
considering the means at their disposal and the
enormous area that comes under their jurisdiction,
they don’t do so badly. Also, the owner of a prickly-
pear selection, besides clearing pear, has his living to
get; and he has to toil good and hard to get it.
“Don Q,” talks of drastic laws and fines. It re-
minds. one of Alice ip Wonderland—“off with his
head.” A good bonus would be more conducive to
pear eradication than any amount of fines. You don’t
want to drive men off these lands. It’s hard enough
to get anyone on them as it is.
On a sly-grog selling change the Brisbane
magistrates let Ah Bung, of China, off the
other day with a fine of 7s. Gd. and costs. His
accomplice in sin was Show Pan. The pro-
secution produced two bottles of liquor with
labels attached, and on these latter being
translated by the court interpreter, it was
found that the grog had been placarded by
the tricky heathen as a cure for rheumatism,
painful bones, swollen muscles, diseased feet,
codghs and headache ; and taken inwardly it
was alleged to be at all times beneficial. The
Court couldn’t quite make up its mind
whether Bung and Pan really did offer the
as a. medicine, or whether they were
merely pulling the Caucasian leg; so it com-
promised on the fine mentioned above.
“Flinderino”: Port Alma, the new port
for Rockhampton, consists of four houses and
one street. The street is constructed of nar-
row planks, set on wooden blocks, with a
handrail on one side and unfathomable mud
on the other. The dwellings are on high
piles, and even then the Pacific Gcean licks
the doorsteps at high tide. At low water
there, is sometimes a few square feet of un-
certain ground to each residence. When
there are no boats in the port the inhabitants
amuse themselves by fishing out of the bed-
room windows, and spearing crabs from the
verandahs. The only domestic quadrupeds
are a couple of shell-fish eating cats and half
a dozen semi-marine goats. The local mos-
quito, so the inhabitants claim, is the largest
and hungriest outside Hades. The residents
catch them in spring traps.
Some people learned a useful lesson in a
Brisbane court the other day. The plain-
tiff claimed that lie had been struck or biffed
so that he suffered acute anguish of mind
and body, and was damnably prejudiced,
morally, physically and mentally, and so
forth. The alleged smiter produced, in his
defence, a collection of witnesses who swore.
privatim et seriatim, that an unknown had
done the deed, and then “vanished like a
shadow.” The sole flaw in their joint and
several yarn was its uncanny flawlessness.
Each of them used the same phraseology,
the same similes, the same everything. After
listening to the exhibition of unanimity, the
P.M. remarked: “It was not necessary
to waste the time of the Court any longer.
Five pounds or a month.” It was a good de-
cision. Notoriously, no two human beings
see any given happening in exactly the same
light—a thing that private detectives, law-
yers and other earnest suborners of perjury
need to bear constantly in mind if they
aspire to be a success at their professions.
Nearer Every Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
China and Japan are two days nearer to Brisbane
under the new schedule of the Nippon Yusen Kaisha.
Yokohama to Brisbane formerly took 30 days; now
it will only take 28. Nagasaki to Thursday Island will
only be a matter of 17 days. —News item.
E was just 5000 years be’ind the age.
And we laughed to think ’e’d ever catch
it up ;
E was shut in olden fashions like a cage,
And we made him drink of shame the
bitter cup ;
’E was ’appy with his bonzes and ’is Buddh
(Wot a pity that we couldn’t leave ’im
so!),
But we battered in his gate, and we broke
’is yellow pate,
And we set ’im on the way that ’e should
go.
But listen to the tramp of the legions,
And ’ark to the thunders in the bay;
Tou may take it well or ill, it’s a mortal
bitter pill,
But the Asian’s cornin’ nearer every day.
We ’ad charted out a country for the White,
And the Yellow Man we showed the bolted
door;
Now lie bares his lips in readiness to bite,
Because ’e felt it just a little sore;
And ’e keeps a cornin’ closer every hour,
And callin’ us ’is “Allies” in ’is fun,
But underneath ’is smile and ’is message full
of guile
You feel ’e’s gettin’ ready with the gun.
But it’s. ’ark to ’is small drill sergeants,
And list to the thunders in the bay;
They break upon your sleep as they roll
across the Deep !
By God, they’re cornin’ nearer ev’ry day!
'E was severed from our cities by the ocean,
And ocean was the field where We were
Boss;
That ’e’d ever run a navy was a notion
We’d never for a moment come across;
But when ’e’d broken up a big Armada,
And fattened up the fishes on the Buss,
We began to ruminate, if the ’Eathen at the
Gate
Mightn’t ’ave a grim significance for us!
But —it’s list to the guns of the Mongol,
And ’ark to his cannon in the bay ;
Then sit you down and think what is wait-
ing on the brink
When Asia’s cornin’ closer ev’ry day.
QUEENSLAND.

QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, January 25, 1913.
My Dear Tjiargomindah,—
The local rep. of the Industrial Union of
Vice-Regal Stone-Masons, William Macgre-
ger. shouldered his tool-kit this morning
and left for Dalby, where he will lay a
foundation block for a new hospital. The
job finished, he and his apprentice, Foxton,
will go to Gowrie, where they will spell-oh
at the home of the hospitable Kings.
Lady Macgregor is due here in March,
with the long-lost Vision and a youthful
grand-daughter. So father’s days of “bach-
ing” are nearly over. Someone else will soon
wash the dishes, boil the spuds and scale the
fishes.
MURPHY, ALL. A., Queensland's
Independent Party.
Yesterday afternoon, at Government
House, the Excellency presented gold medal'
to four of the boys who did such plucky work
at the time of the recent Southport bathing
fatality. The medallions were the gift of
Mr. and Mrs. Arthur Hobbs. Acting-Premier
Barlow, Mayor and Mayoress Raymond, and
the relatives of the boys were the only guests.
The four who stood in the limelight (Will-
cocks, Lahey, Robertson and Collins) looked
as if they would have cheerfully bartered
their medals for a desert island. Mr. and
Mrs. McNish also gave to Robertson a gold
sovereign-purse, since he it was who saved
their little daughter. Mr. T. A. Bond, who
was responsible for two rescues, and whose
reputation for daring deeds dates back to the
great flood of ’93, was among the onlookers.
Last Thursday, local Presbyterians, headed
by Governor Macgregor and twa kilties wi’
the pipes blawing, gathered to welcome Mini-
ster Cosh and his wife. The Rev. Cosh has
succeeded to the place left vacant by the
death of the Rev. Frackleton, round whom
waged such a kirk riot as will be forgotten
only when Glencoe is forgiven. There were
400 in the multitude, and they were fed on
speeches, prayers, music and other palatable
and unpalatable viands.
“Bob” Philp was Exhibit A at a river trip
on Wednesday night. The people who had
farewelled. him with a silver tea and coffee
service prior to his departure on his recent
trip, reassembled to give him the welcome-
home hand. It was a chilly evening, and a
strong breeze was blowing, so that the flap-
ping of all the female coats on board was
like unto the sound of an angel host battling
for places on the Roost of Peace. Long
stretches of speeches were unrolled in the
gale and blown to windward, and if the dole-
ful band on the hurricane deck had gone in
the same direction all would have been well.
In the circumstances, it was only #-well.
Miss Isabel Rutherford, who has been
governess to the Chelmsford children for five
years, has bought Miss Burdorff’s school
here, and will get her muscle into trim at
once.
James Edmond, editor of The Bulletin
with his wife and daughter. arrived in Bris-
bane yesterday by . the St. Albans from
Manila. They will motor to Toowoomba
next week, and return to Sydney by mail
train.

land. He devoted himself "almost entirely

included a half-share in Mount Brisbane sta U
