There is only one child, a boy of 12. Mr

Mrs. John Brown and her pretty and
popular daughters left Brisbane for "keeps
on Sunday last, by the Kyarra. At Sydney
they tranship for London, where the head of
the family has been settled for some months
as manager of Thomas Brown and Sons’
London house. It will be a long time be-
fore the social chasm caused by their depar-
ture can be filled in.
Another outward-bound is Mrs. W. S.
Byrne, who is known to all the younger set
as “Auntie.” She will be greatly missed,
too. Her intention is to visit Ireland
(where she has a group of aboriginal
cousins) and England. The Willcoeks family
departed with her. After a year’s travel
they will leave Hazel, the strikingly hand-
some youngest daughter, at school in Swit-
zerland.
Bacteriologist Found this week returned
from the United States, where he has been
gleaning knowledge of the family life of the
tick, its habits, vices, political opinions, and
weakest point of assault. Local tick stocks,
however, are still firm.
Schoolmaster Wheatley, late of the Rock-
hampton Grammar School, is doing research
work at Oxford.

Cairns, in order to gain a first-hand know-
ledge of the warm coloring of the scenery.
It glows there in profusion.

AUSTRALIA JANE.
Queensland State school teachers are a
strenuous lot. Quite a number of them
sacrificed their holidays, or a good part of
them, to go into camp at Southport and
learn military drill. Then came a confer-
ence, in which many of the military men
participated. After that, a number of lesser
mostly-educational conventions, well-attended.
All of which proves, over again, how a sub-
tropical climate saps the vigor, physical and
mental, of Northerners.
From the North :
Lacaze, a Herbert River cane-grower, has invented
his own rain supply. On his three farms (total 600
acres) he has put down six stations, with six 2in.
spears on each farm. Each spear is down 22ft., and
each contains from 16ft. to 17ft. of water. A 4in.
centrifugal pump, capable of raising 20,000 gallons
an hour, driven by a 12-h.p. oil engine, is mounted
on a waggon, and is drawn from station to station.
Spraying, to imitate rain, is obtained by a pipe
system. It is claimed that this spraying is far better
than flooding. At a demonstration, young cane which
had not hitherto been watered, opened its leaves im-
mediately the spray got to work ; and on a field
which had a fortnight-old wetting there was a growth
of a foot. The Herbert begins to think it knows
now how to make a permanent success of sugar-
growing.
The Bulletin doesn’t know whether any-
body has guaranteed that there will always
be water in the shallow bore-holes.
“Kuscobin,” on a piece of statecraft: —
When Kidston was Premier of Queensland, and, in-
c'dentallv member for Rockhampton, he left his
electorate a legacy that ought to earn him its grati-
tude for all time, in the shape of the Port Alma to
Rockhampton railway. By agreement with the Brit-
ish India Co., the Government charges ss. per ton
for loading on this track, a distance of 37 miles.
The rate applies all round, whether the goods carried
be gelignite or chaff. This works out at under lfd.
per ton per mile. The average rate from (say) Brisbane
to Ipswich works out at just a fraction under 6d. per
mile per ton. It costs the collieries at Ipswich 106 s.
6d. per ton to bring lip anything under four-ton lots
of gelignite from Brisbane, or just under Is. per ton
per mile, whilst Rockhampton mines have to pay only
ljd. The Port Alma line, of course, is making a dead
loss that has to be levelled up by overcharges on
other lines; yet the Department can’t mend matters
because of the agreement with the 8.1.5. N. Co. It
is a fine tribute to Wullie’s statesmanship—or what-
ever you like to call it.
A Cairns police court was the scene re-
cently of a sensational drama. A Chow,
accused of wilful murder, attracted attention
by his peculiar appearance. Medical exami-
nation showed him to be a leper—one of the
dozens that are supposed to be roaming N.Q.,
or, too sick to roam, are waiting for death in
a hovel on a banana plantation. Without
wishing its friends any ill, The Bulletin
would dearly love to have the choosing of the
Court to try the alleged murderer. It would
assign Bruce Smith for his defence, and ask
that the leprous accused, being a stranger in a
strange land, might be permitted to take a
seat at the Bar table. A score of the fattest
gentleman who say that the country can’t be
developed with white labor should be enrolled
as court criers, jurors and sweepers. Then
it would rope in another 20 of the advocates
of a piebald Australia, and make them toss
for the privilege of being Ah Pong’s hang-
man or perpetual gaoler, as the case may be.

On Thursday and Friday.
24th and 25th APRIL, 1913.
At Freeman R. Jackson and Co.’s Yards,
Wanganui.
BUSIIY PARK STUD IIEREFORDS,
KAI IWI, WANGANUI,

THIS FINE HERD was founded upon
Mr. James Stuckey’s renowned TE
RANGITUMAU HERD. It has even been
still further improved from time to time by
importations from the best strains of Great
Britain. Its present owner, MR. FRANK
MOORE, can confidentely claim that it is
unrivalled in the Australasian Colonies for
EVENNESS OF TYPE, CONSTITUTION.
EARLY MATURITY and GRAND RICH
DARK COLOR.
Owing to the expiry of certain Leases, the
whole of these FAMOUS CATTLE are to be
sold by PUBLIC AUCTION on 24th and
25th APRIL, 1913.
ABSOLUTELY WITHOUT RESERVE.
The Sale is a Genuine Clearing One.
The BUSHY PARK HERD includes, ap-
proximately—
-101 COWS now in calf, and which will be
again in calf. From these are expected
about 45 Heifer Calves this season.
29 HEIFERS, two-years-old. They will be
in calf at time of sale.
23 HEIFERS, yearlings.
5 COWS, old, in calf.
These latter are the only OLD cows in the
lot. The foregoing constitute an aggregate of
203 FEMALE STUD HEREFORD
CATTLE.
To this total must be added a
LARGE NUMBER OF STUD BULLS
of different ages. The majority of these will
be rising two years at time of sale.
Then there will also be sold 45 Bull
Calves.
These MAGNIFICENT CATTLE are
practically all eligible for entry in the ENG-
LISH HERD BOOK, and they will be in
splendid condition when sold.
The Sale of the BUSHY PARK STUD
HEREFORDS is a unique opportunity for
New Zealand breeders to improve their
Herds, or for Australian and South Ameri-
can buyers to introduce a new strain of blood.
In the conduct of the Sale there will act
in conjunction—
MURRAY, ROBERTS AND CO., LTD.,
FREEMAN R. JACKSON AND CO.,
WILLIAMS AND KETTLE, LTD.,
Auctioneers.

Missing Friends.
*jT HOMAS LITTLE, late of Dumfries, Scotland,
Plumber, deceased. Relatives are requested to
communicate with FOXTON, HOBBS & MACNISH,
Solicitors, Brisbane.
D URSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division
* of the High Court of Justice, made in the Mat-
ter of the ESTATE of KOBERT TOWNSEND HIP-
PISLEY, Deceased, Hippisley v. Hippisley, 1912, H.
1515, and dated the 24th October, 1912, the follow-
ing inquiry is directed, viz.: An inquiry whether
Charles Ernest Hippisley, named in the will of Robert
Townsend Hippisley, is living or dead, and, if dead,
when he died, and whether he left any and what will,
and whether he was ever married, and if so when and
to whom, and whether there were any children of the
said marriage, and who are his legal personal repre-
sentatives.
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN, that the said
Charles Ernest Hippisley and all persons claiming
through or by descent from him are, personally or by
their solicitors, on or before the 24th day of June,
1913, to come in and prove their claims at the
chambers of Mr. Justice Joyce and Mr. Justice Eve,
Room 692, Royal Courts of Justice, Strand, London,
England, or in default thereof they will be peremp-
torily excluded from the benefit of the said order.
Wednesday, the 9th day of July, 1913, at half-past
two in the afternoon, at the said chambers, is ap-
pointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated the 17th day of December, 1912.
A. KEEN, Master.
ROBBINS and CO., 218 Strand, W.C., London,
for Abbot, Pope and Abbot, Bristol, Solicitors
for the Plaintiffs.
Note. —The above-named Charles Ernest Hippisley,
who was a son of the testator, Robert Townsend Hip-
pisley, a solicitor, of Bristol, England, is now, if
alive, about 51 vears of age. He went to Australia
in February, 1889, and was there employed by Messrs.
Robert Reid and Co., and afterwards, till May, 1897,
by Messrs. Sands and McDougall (Limited). He
resided at Rochester Lodge, Collins-place, Melbourne.
He left Melbourne in May, 1897, by the Orient steam-
ship Orizaba, for London, where he stayed one or
two months, and is believed to have resided at 123
Fulham Road, London. In October, 1897, he left
London, and until April, 1898, was employed by
Messrs. J. Gurlick and Co., of Alderley Street, Cape
Town, South Africa, and resided at 43 St. John
Street, Cape Town. He returned to Melbourne in
1898, and expressed his intention of going to Queens-
land or to Sydney. He is alleged to have been seen
in London by an old schoolfellow in 1908.
Tenders.
NOTICE.
Sale of Mineral Leases and Plant—
Northern Territory.
SEALED Tenders will be received at the
Office of the Administrator oi£ the
Northern Territory of Australia, at Dar-
win, up to the 31st day of March, 1913, for
the purchase of four (4) Mineral Leases,
each of an area of 80 acres, and comprising
the whole of that mining property known as
the Daly River Copper Mine, and for the
purchase of a Reverberatory Furnace, with
slag heap, at such mine.
This property is situated about 60 miles
westerly from the Brock’s Creek Railway
Station, on the Darwin-Pine Creek Railway,
and about four (4) miles from the Daly
River Landing; the River being navigable
to such Landing to vessels drawing not
more than five and a half (s£) feet.
The present Mineral Lease of the prop-
erty expires on the 31st day of March, 1913,
when the Leaseholders have to give up
possession.
The Smelter on the Mine was built in
1908, and is in good condition; its capacity
is approximately from 150 to 180 tons of
ore per month.
The term of the Mineral Lease will be
for 99 years from the 30th day of June,
1913, the rental Is. per acre per annum,
and 21 per centum of the net profits; the
conditions, reservations, and stipulations
pertaining, are, with two alterations, those
laid down in the Northern Territory Mining
Act and Regulations in respect to Mineral
Leases.
Each tender must be accompanied by a
cheque for £2OO, marked “good” at the
Bank.
Further particulars can be obtained at the
External Affairs Department, Northern
Territory Branch, Melbourne, and the Gov-
ernment Secretary’s Department, Darwin.
J. A. GILRUTH,
Administrator.
Office of the Administrator,
Darwin, 27th August, 1912.

COUNTERFEIT COINAGE PLANT.
£lOO Reward.
"Virr HEREAS certain imitations of silver
V t coins have recently been passed into
circulation at Sydney, in the State of New
South Wales: Notice is hereby Oven that a
reward of One hundred pounds will be paid
by the Government of the Commonwealth
for information leading to the apprehension
of the coiners and the discovery of the plant
used in the manufacture of the coins referred
to. The Commonwealth Government will
request the State Government to extend a
full pardon to any accomplice who will give
the desired information. This offer ytrill
remain open for thre monthb from this
date.

POSITIONS in the office of the Engineer-in-
chief for Commonwealth Railways, Melbourne: —

salary £3OO p.a.
Qualifications. —Applicants must be survey drafts-
men capable of preparing working survey drawings,
sections, etc., and have a knowledge of grading, earth-
works and drainage arrangements. Preference given
to those who have had field experience.
DRAFTSMAN, salary £250 p.a.
Qualifications. —-Applicants should be capable of
designing waggon and car stock, and have a know-
ledge of locomotive work.
Applicants for the above positions should furnish
full details of experience and qualifications.
Anplications, which close on February 8, 1913,
should be addressed to the Secretary, Department of
Lome Affairs, Melbourne.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hoar of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. W hat a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when .the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers 'restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot h” r t the ear;
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the W T ilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve co m-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been

whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we

Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.

—“ I had a young baby left in my care
about two years ago, and the child caught
a very severe form of skin disease. The
skin disease first appeared on her body j ust
like little water blisters under the skin, and
it used to irritate the child that much that
she could not sleep night or day, and she
used to scratch herself to pieces. Even in
her sleep, she would scratch.
“Then I saw one of the Cuticura adver-
tisements and I at once sent for a sample
and applied it. I found that it seemed to
cool the affected parts at once so I procured
a pot of Cuticura Ointment and a cake of
Cuticura Soap. She had always been in
such a burning heat, and the nasty sores
that used to come after it, and it used to
swell so, and through scratching herself she
got it under her finger nails, in fact, her
fingers and thumb were so bad that I
thought she would lose the first joint. The
whole of her body and even her head was
affected. It is six months since I left off
using the Cuticura Remedies, and she hasn’t
had a sign of the trouble since. Nothing
seemed to ease or cure it till I tried the
Cuticura Soap and Ointment.” (Signed)
Mrs. Ethel Christian, May 16, 1911.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know lull well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair C^nver.
1 am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. Aly hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of niv true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others—but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

Food
Business, Robbery, etc.
Stony : After the New Year goose was dead and
digested, one of Melbourne’s largest poultry-providing
firms disclosed some fowl facts to a daily 'paper. Of
the allegations, the most startling was that an em-
ployee had earned 275. in one day during the festive
season, dressing Christmas dinners. In one work-
ing day (number of hours not specified) this expert
did 80 birds, which works out at about 4d. per fowl.
Since it seemed to be a simple matter, and we had
a fowl or two fit for killing, I decided to take 10
minutes off yesterday and prepare one for the table.
Beginning at 9 a.m., I removed the rooster’s head
and let the bird hang for a while. I then proceeded
to take 20 minutes more off and rough-pluck the
corpse. At 9.30, I took another half-hour off and
began systematically to remove the smaller feathers.
By 9.45, there being then only a fine sub-stratum
of feathers left, I plucked the thing all over once
again. At 10.15, having singed off the bird’s hair
(fowls have hair, though not enough to keep them
from looking somewhat bald when their feathers are
removed), I set out to truss it, following printed
directions. Removing the neck at the shoulders, I
sat the biped up on its haunches (see instructions)
and endeavored to remove the crop, “which is easily
done.’’ Only a part of the crop parted willingly
from its parent fowl. The rest got lost, and took
until 10.30 to discover. By 10.40 I had drawn the
bird, all except the gizzard, which resolutely refused
to be drawn for some time. At 11.15, working at
high pressure, I had the rooster bested, and handed
it in at the kitchen door, feeling that 4d. was
microscopic money for the job. In reporting the
earning of 275. by one man, the newspaper which
printed the glad news remarked that poultry-dress-
ers were scarce. I expect it’s the apprenticeship
that chokes ’em off.
* * * *
“Green Lamp”: The indignant chemist hailed from
Maoriland, where good fish is cheap enough to be
regarded as everyday food. He told me he had that
evening paid a Greek patriot to supply him with a
meal of schnapper, but had been given instead a
dish of octopus, or shark, or some other carrion
thinly disguised in sauce. While he was fuming
over the swindle, he propped his paper against the
dirty cruet and read a triumphant report of the
prosecution of a Sydney chemist for selling olive oil
or something which was slightly below standard. The
pill merchant had not substituted train oil or lard
for the superior product, but had passed on a pro-
duct that was a decimal point or so below the
official odds. The swindled visitor desired earnestly
to know why the Health people passed bv the out-
rageous fish-pirate and leaped on his profession. 1
was unable to tell him. Seems to me that the fish
and oyster freebooter should, so to speak, dwell per-
manently under the cold eye of the Health Depart-
ment. His tragically dilapidated dustbox and the
ruinous smell that hangs like a curse over his fishy
den call for eternal suspicion and supervision. No-
thing but a shark could live in such an atmosphere.
* * * *
“R. 8.”: Pure Foods Acts have insisted on - the
publication of the presence of salicylates in explo-
sive tomato sauce ; the new and incomplete Life As-
surance Bill does something towards forcing the con-
fidence-trick companies to state something approach-
ing the truth of their financial position; and i”s
time the advertiser got the facts of newspaper cir-
culation. Statutory declarations of circulation for a
month are able to state quite truthfully the reverse
of the facts, by reason of the newsagents being in-
structed to keep back “returns” for that month. I
know of one paper whose circulation is increased by
nearly 50 p.c. during the time of audit by keeping
the “returns” out of the office for that period ; and
it’s a concern quite big enough to be above the
fraud. Furthermore, the quantity of financial notices
and puffs of school speech-day reports which are
paid for would amaze the public which accepts these
statements as gospel. Let the habit go to its reason-
able sequence, and by-and-bye all the genuine, un-
paid-for news in some Australian dailies will be the
stodgy leader that nobody reads, the cable about the
unspeakable Turk who wouldn’t pay for it if he
had the chance, and the last dying speech and con-
fession of the condemned who is executed before he
has been asked to pay for the notice of his per-
formance.
* * * *
Public Actuary Gouge, of S.A., presses the claim
of his State to be the thriftiest in the Common-
wealth. Issuing a belated report on Friendly Soci-
eties for the five-year period ending with 1909, he
quotes the capital per member in these societies at
nearly £l5. Victoria was then second, and has since
gone top. As for the Savings Bank, depositors num-
bered more than half the State’s population, with an
average credit of £34. Westralia was second in
that comparison.
Victoria’s insolvents numbered 392 last year as
against 289 in 1911, but their total deficiencies were
only £40,062 in ’l2, as against £51,035 the year
before. Of the 392, only 84 were in Melbourne; so
the country trader and manufacturer “goes bung”
about four times as numerously as his city competi-
tor. Thus Ballarat had 40 insolvencies, Bendigo 21
Horsham 18, Warracknabeal 19, Geelong and Echuca
14 each, and Kerang 25. Melbourne’s insolvents
were some £7OOO short altogether, while Kerang’s
little lot owned up to a surplus of assets over lia-
bilities amounting to £4OO. The trouble evidently
was that the country trader couldn’t turn his assets
into cash —which mostly means that “the back-
bone” couldn’t pay its bill. Almost anybody thinks
he can run a store in the country. When he finds
that “the backbone” wants a year’s credit, and then
occasionally requires the bill renewed till after next
harvest, the storekeeper without capital begins to feel
un v. ell.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CITY PIG SALES.
City pig sales: All grades firm.”
—Nourishing news.
When I’m sick of Fancy’s flights,
Tired of gossamer delights,
Weary of fine dreams and visions—
T hings remote as star collisions—-
When my thoughts, no longer buoyed,
Fall like star-dust through the void,
Fail and fall towards red damnation,
Seeking for some firm foundation,
Helpless then I sit and squirm,
So I fight on,
Till I light on:
“City Pig Sales —All Grades Firm.”
“Porkers baconers, backfatters.”
Poets are as mad as hatters !
Who could pen a line like that,
Full of nourishment and fat ?
I prefer to wordy jigs
Droves of daily-paper pigs,
Grunting down the figured column
In a manner stout and solemn.
Dreams are hungry things. The worm
Turns from talkers
Unto porkers.
(“City Pig Sales—All Grades Firm.”)
When from eating bacon I
Rise on Fancy’s wings to fly,
Do I scorn the waddling thing
On whose fat I daily sing?
Heavens, no ! My appetite
Soon will force me to alight,
Bring me diving from mid-air
Like a plummet back to where
Good “Backfatter” serves his term.
Bards are sighing !
Bacon’s frying !
(“City Pig Sales—All Grades Firm.”)

A SYDNEY CARTER’S PRAISE.
“Some few weeks ago I had a painful experience,” writes Mr. J. R.
Williams, South Sydney Street, Mackay, Queensland. “I was employed by the
well-known Sydney grocery firm of I. Israel, Sussex Street, and when removing a
2cwt. cask of soda to the cellar it got away and smashed my left hand against
the stone steps, causing three very severe lacerations and bruising my hand all
over. The pain I suffered was intense, and for some days my hand was useless.
I could not bear to touch the injured hand, and although I did not have to relin-
quish my work, yet it was with the greatest difficulty that I performed my
duties. I fortunately was recommended to try Rexona, an ointment that I
saw advertised in the city papers, and after one application the pain commenced
to disappear and the lacerations to heal, and the bruised part to regain its nor-
mal condition. I persevered, and Rexona soon completed its rapid cure, much
to my satisfaction. I can heartily recommend Rexona to anyone who meets
with an accident of similar nature.”

The Rout of the Forest.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the city of Sydney, 272 buildings erected,
j j n the suburbs 7717, at an expenditure of
*£s 060 000, approximately —all in the year.
—Triumphant daily paper article.
The flames were practically unfightable. Strenuous
fforts were put forward by local residents, but all to
o avail- At times it appeared as if the many pretty
'little cottages in the way would be destroyed, but
the rain saved the situation.
—Report of suburban bush fire.
r|IEN thousand years grew the forest
' ( facing the harbor so blue ;
Then came the land agent, rancid,
babbling of "ozone” and “view” ;
Transient creatures, wrapped loosely in flow-
-1 ing and dark-colored bark —
Growths that he spoke of as “buyers”—•
cackled like biids in a park;
Cackled, and prodded, and listened ; rustled
like leaves in a breeze ;
Clotted the murder and downfall of Time’s
irreplaceable trees ;
Loudly they plotted and chaffered, drove
little pegs in the ground,
While the dumb prey of the headsmen
rustled in horror around.
Jones* was the name of the fungus that
bought up this forest-land choice ;
Jones sent his steel-laden minions to murder
the things without voice ;
Bravely they sawed at the branches, bravely
they hacked at the truuks,
Till, with a cracking of sine-"'=. they tottered
like so many drunks —-
Tottered and fell ’midst Lie bracken, and lay
with their roots in the air.
Then came the drays and the woodmen ; each
of them gathered his share,
Loaded, and slowly deported, a mass of in-
animate sticks,
All to make room for the fungus and its
indispensable bricks.
Jones built a house, and a good one, where
had been “nothing but trees” ;
On the veiandah, at even, Jones loved to
lounge at his ease —
Lounge and look over the busliland where he
had “picked lots” to sell.
Jones, when he thought of his foresight, used
with importance to swell.
When he read building statistics showung the
rapid increase,
Jones felt annoyed with the forest full of
cool shadow's and peace ;
Trees did not help to pay taxes —no cash
came from busliland retreats.
Jones in his dreams saw' the busliland cut
into sun-blasted streets.
Oh! but the forest survivors, standing like
veterans at bay,
Kept all their eyes on the fungus sprawling
on brick o’er the way ;
Every zephyr that stirred them took, in the
strange forest code,
Messages re the invader, camped by his
well-guttered road ;
Every gale saw mass meetings—meetings
wdiere speeches were made,
Stringybark. Red'Gum and Blue Gum pour-
ing forth curse and tirade;
Jones, quite at ease by his fireside, w'atched
the mad rage of the trees,
Yawmed like a fool and said dully, “Yers,
that’s a pritty stiff breeze.”
Then with the march of the seasons flame-
haired young Summer came round ;
Tree locusts shrieked in the branches, hot
crickets whirred in the ground ;
Jones, very hot, took the hose out to give
the geraniums a drink,
When, in the shade of the forest, he saw a
red eye give a wink ;
Ere he could cast the hose from him there
was a rustle and roar,
And the pent rage of the giants started to
smoke and to soar.
Jones seized a branch like a duster (Jones,
though a fungus, w'as game).
Then he dashed into the battle, thrashing
the pillars of flame.
Fiercely the Summer blaze crackled, vali-
antly forward it strove;
Rut to the aid of Jonesfungus came a sub-
urbanite drove ;
Just for the sake of the cottage and building
statistics they came,
Broke off the quaint litle branches, joined in
the fire-thrashing game.
Then the skies wept for the outrage—Jones
versus Forest-on-Fire ;
Wept for the Forest Primeval, and so
quenched its dearest desire.
Which was to w'ipe out its foeman, and grow
o’er his bricks and his bones!. • • •
Sigh for the vanishing Forest! Pray tor
the conquering Jones!
N..S.W.
KODAK.
Elder Man’s Lane.
III. —THE LILY OF ST. LEONARDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
J Horse Ferry—or, as the crews call
I it, the Punt —is, as I have shown in
another sketch, the morning refuge
of certain known and notorious citizens of
the suburbs of North Sydney, whose daily
work, or daily loaf, lies in the city, and who
have made consummate asses of themselves
going across home on the passenger teiry
the night before —generally at the end o
the month. As witness the case of my de-
plorable artistic acquaintance, Johnson.
Not that these citizens care much tor the
spirit of North Sydney—nor it for them,
for that matter, seeing that North Sjdney
has even less spirit than Sydney itselt (an
that’s saying a good deal) ; but they re genei-
allv sick and shaky and sorry and ashame ,
and haven’t the needful for the two drinks
that will remedy those failings, and
they’re anxious to escape the. observation o
any chance members of their audience o
the previous evening. ll]ar
There is horse nature on the vehieulai
ferrv, as well as human— only one s
always sober. When the punt bumps the
side of tin* landing they all waltz involun-
tarily, and wake up simultaneously with ex-
pressions of surprise, from mild to start ed.
1 remember one big old draught horse that
always went to sleep comfortably as ° ‘ p
he came aboard : and, when the bump came,
he always reminded me, in Ins win s,
Oranny, when she’d nodded too far o ■
knitting, and brought up with a jerk, an
thought she'd dropped her specs.
But tins is the story of the Lily of St.
Leonards; and I don’t know what the horses
have to do with it, except that, without
doubt, there was or had been a Granny in
the ease, and perhaps Lily had often perched
on her knee or sat beside her while she nodded
over her knitting or her Bible ; or even said
her prayers at her knee. Or nodded over
her gin—But, no! I prefer the knitting
and Bible and prayers. It sounds better
and it costs nothing. Besides, I used to say
my prayers when I was a child.
She w r as a slip of a girl, half Jewish, and
Jewish by name, with, dark brown eyes and
blue-black hair. She had the face as well as
the figure that the artist looks for; and she
also had brains. A rare combination in any
man’s experience. Why she was called the
Lily of St. Leonards I’ve either forgotten
or never knew. Perhaps because she was
born and lived there, and her heart and soul
and character were considered spotless in
those days. She was an artist’s model and
a Venus of Milo. At least I think that is
the name. She was known in all the studios
and a favorite there —was warmly and
uproariously welcomed at artists’ diggings
and artists’ suppers, for her wit and humor,
philosophy and laughter. She had common
sense, too. _ The most enjoyable holiday
trips, picnics; theatre-evenings and oyster
suppers I ever spent were with the Lily of
St. Leonards, and a few chosen spirits
(dead or lost or respectable now) in the
days ere Bohemia died in Sydney and was
buried with Victor Daley.
The last time I heard her laugh was at a
send-off supper given to a writer and an
artist. And, a few days later, I, John Law-
rence, went to the world away to seek the
living my own land begrudged me; and Lily
went her way rejoicing and to bring joy of
life and laughter into the hearts of others.
But there was a writer, and he was a scoun-
drel. He is doing well in two nations now,
and I hope he’ll come across this poor sketch
and read it to the end when he isn’t feeling
too good.
* * # *
A few months after my return from
foreign lands I had occasion to go across to
North Sydney. I had left all my chances in
foreign lands —no matter why. I wanted to
hunt up a member of that vanished send-off
supper party of the near, dear denarted days,
and maybe inouire after the Lily and some
of the other boys; but he had departed also.
So I came back on the vehicular ferry, be-
cause an uncle or cousin or something of
mine was then skipper of the old punt, and
I wanted to save the penny. I had five other
coins, and they represented two (2) pints of
beer.
It was getting late one day in the summer
of our year and the winter of mine, and near-
ing the time for the last horse ferry across.
There was the usual little collection of
honest and weary carters and draymen and
draught horses going home. There was also
an early taxi-cab (of its kind), with a veiled
and slouch-hatted and silent mystery inside
the shade. Perhaps it, too, wanted to avoid
the glare and stare of the passenger ferry.
Likewise there was a belated bottle-o cart;
and I recognised the driver as none other
than my old friend Benno. We greeted each
other in silence. He never asked where I
came from —Benno never wondered at any-
thing. I attached myself to Benno’s cart,
and so was at once independent of ticket col-
lectors —and of relatives, too, in a financial
way of speaking.
There had been a blue-black pall overhead,
but it had risen in a great arch over a broad
sunset, and in that light I saw a woman
standing by the bulwark, for’ard the wheel,
on the other side of the deck. She had her
back towards us, and was looking up the
Parramatta River. Over her was a long,
brownish jacket, of the style affected by the
English servant girl of 10 or 12 years ago,
or by ’Arriet of East End and London
North at an earlier date ; and her hat was of
the modified style of the Liz of our own
Billo. But neither seemed to suit her, some-
how, even though seen from the rear in the
gathering gloom. The jacket was worn and
frayed, and hung loosely on her figure. As
she sort of side-stepped quickly and de-
cisively, apd moved further up the deck and
awav. something struck me in a flash as
familiar. But it might have been a trick of
sunset lights and shadows.
“Who’s she?” I asked Benno; then added
quickly, to save a mistake, “Is she the
missus?”
“Noh!” snorted Benno (but in a whis-
pering snort), not that he held her or any
woman in particular contempt, or women in
general, but at the bare idea of his ever
marrying one of them. “My old 'tin’s good
enough for me. > But I know her,” he said,
still in a hoarse whisper. “She’s got a old
mother or grandmother or something, up
back here on the Shore, off Blue’s Point-
road. I git bottles there, sometimes. She’s
been goin’ it strong this last year or two,
that one. Just done a month on the Island
for her last drunk and disorderly, an’ I
s’pose she’s ashamed to be seen on the pas-
senget (ferry). She’s well known on the
Shore. I seen her cornin’ over on the punt
this mornin’, an’ I met her nivht before
last.” And Benno went to the little shelter
behind the wheel to light his “lamp”—a
bottle with the bottom knocked out and a
piece of pared candle dropped into the neck
of it.
I looked at the girl—or woman —again.
The breeze pressed the old .cloak, and it
clung to her figure, showing girlishly—out-
lined by a sunset that was reddening
now, and crowing glorious. And again I
was conscious of the something familiar.
That irritating, on-the-tip-of-the-tongue sort
of consciousness of a forgotten memory that
makes a man, when in company, hold up his
finger for silence, and put that finger to his
lips or wrinkle his forehead and consult
the ground, while consulting the back of his
head with the palm of his left hand.
Benno came back. Benno misses nothing
without seeming to see anything.
“You seem interested.” he said. I d call
it a mash: but I wouldn’t if I was you.
Yeh don’t know her.” .... .
Benno is very slow and deliberate with his
information, and gives it: with long pauses
between the pieces. The longer you cant
seem to wait, the more Benno can. He
fixed his bottle in its socket. „
“She used to be a hartist’s model once,
said Benno. “They call her the Lily of
Si Leonards. The boys shout it after her
when she’s shickered. Gorblime, it s a
Weedin’ shame.”
HENRY LAWSON.

“ For some considerable time I have been out of sorts,
due to irregularity of the bowels and constipation. I
had frequently seen Chamberlain’s Tablets advertised
in the newspapers and at length decided to try them.
I did so doubtingly, because I had tried so many other
things without obtaining the slightest benefit from
them. I obtained immediate relief from the use of
Chamberlain’s Tablets and now use them regularly.
In fact I carry a bottle in my pocket wherever I go,
and would not be without them on any account.”
Thos. Rodda, Ouse, Tasmania.
Dead Man’s Creek.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Til HIS Dead Man’s Creek lies on the West
| Coast of Maoriland, within a fertile
rift of mountain terraces that widen
out into broad, soggy moorlands, reaching to
the sea. Every mining region has a Dead
Man’s Creek, and if this one deserves the
name better than others, it is because all the
men who won gold from its dark tunnels, and
made its tiny valley fragrant with the in-
cense of silver-pinewood, are dead as they
ever will be. They lie buried over against
the township, five miles away; and the weka
comes down the creek at night, and lilts its
wild notes among their deserted tenements ;
plum trees drop their ripe load on prostrate
paling fences; and the small, shameless-
looking pub stands with its two front win-
dows boarded up like a pair of black eyes.
Of the four derelicts of Dead Man’s Creek
only two were any longer capable of exer-
tion. One was a solemn-eyed man on the
far side of 70, who dyed his hair and beard
an unconvincing green-brown, and then hid
himself far from the admiring eye of mor-
tals, within the depths of a tunnel. With his
mate he secured in gold about the amount of
an old age pension, which might have been
had clean and dry for the asking; but they
prized their independence like the vagrant
goats that capered on steep patches of ver-
dure above the precipice of the creek. At
length they also went down into the un-
pi omising shaft which the sexton digged for
them.
Percival Creech, the third male survivor
of a glory that had been, lived in crazy isola-
tion far up the creek. He was a reputed col-
lector of empty matchboxes, with which he
had partly filled a room, and he claimed pro-
perty in the unattainable goats, though they
treated with levity any attempt on his part
to approach them. Nevertheless, he roared
asseveration of his rights, and discharged a
gun wildly when a chance visitor entered the
valley. At length he was left in peace; no
one came near but the weekly baker boy, who
left his bread at a respectful distance in a
box nailed to a post.
Female society had but one representative
—a bustling, white-haired old woman, who
plied the visitor with monstrous compliments,
and pattered round after the grumpy baker
boy for the treasure of his conversation. She
grew geraniums and tended a few fowls, until
an excess of loneliness and salt meat drove
her forth, and she started out across the
moors in the small hours of a summer morn-
ing. She might have had speech of the man
with the sooty-brown beard and his mate,
except that during the day-time they were
buried in the tunnel, and even at Dead Man’s
the conventions forbade a lonely woman to
enter the abode of a bachelor gentleman after
nightfall. There was Creech, to be sure; but
Creech was not sociable. And, besides, he
had been an enemy of her husband, the late
Sandy Mcßean. Sandy’s pig had acquired
a comfortable habit of sleeping under
Creech’s dwelling, until the owner, with the
fiendish ingenuity of the half-crazed, bored
auger-holes in the floor, and poured scalding
water on the recumbent pork. It snorted
hurriedly, and, after a swift journey home,
began to develop bald red patches, the mys-
tery whereof was not hidden from Sandy
Mcßean. So he sought an interview, which
only, ended in unprofitable bellowing and
shaking of fists —threats of the law on the
part of Sandy and threats of a gun on the
part of Creech.
Thus it came about that some railway
gangers saw a frail old woman with yellow-
white hair and a frightened look, moving
over the moor in wide, eccentric circles.
Then the mental hospital claimed her, and she
had company, if not of the best.
Last of all came the turn of Creech. The
baker boy, seeing that his bread supply ac-
cumulated in the box, ventured up for an
explanation; and when he saw Creech glar-
ing at him from a bunk, with the red light
one sees in the eyes of a dog, he stopped to
ask no questions. A policeman and an ex-
press driver came to remove Creech, and
found him speechless. Constable O’Grady
looked round the room, and seeing nothing to
take charge of, stepped into a rear apart-
ment. which he marvelled to find stored with
matchboxes, occupying corner-wise a third of
the room. He opehed several, and, finding
them empty, kicked the collection playfully
with his heavy foot. Creech listened and
squirmed as the operations of the sacrilegi-
ous feet were borne upon his consciousness,
and then he was carried out, writhing like a
captured eel. The perturbation of his spirit
and the roughness of transport were too
much for Creech, and he died soon after his
arrival at the hospital, with his face twisted
towards Dead Man’s Creek.
* * * *
In the township lived Billy Spritt, an ath-
lete and hero of football fields, with a jaw
like a money-box. That unlovely jaw be-
tokened, among other kinds of determina-
tion, the resolve not to work. For Billy was
like the bucks of certain primitive tribes that
consider hard work dishonoring to a man of
mettle, and devote the pauses between deeds
of valor to meat and drink and sleep. This
was well enough while a match or a race
could be bartered, and a parrot-nosed in-
triguer found to make it worth while. But
at last selection committees averted their
nostrils from Billy; and being desperately in
want of money he accepted a bribe to lose a
race, got his pals to “put a bit” on himself,
and then sped for the tape like a wild Indian.
After that temporary triumph he was left
idle in the market place, because no man had
hired him. So he punched his wife for being
a nuisance and an expense, and when a
Friendly Society that had lent him money
at 10 per cent, began to squeeze, he was
sold out.
It was at this point William bethought
himself of Dead Man’s Creek and its aban-
doned dwellings, and thither he migrated on
a waggonette with his wife and two bant-
lings. They took possession of Creech’s hut,
and Billy spent the afternoon panning the
bed of the creek. But the results were
meagre, the occupation cramping to a man of
action, and the sandflies that devoured his
hands and neck absorbed enough dishonest
blood to infect them for life. Then he re-
membered the days of his youth and unen-
cumbered blessedness, and disappeared.
Mrs. Spritt set about clearing out the
empty matchboxes, hurling them through a
small window in handfuls, till her head grew
dizzy and she was recalled to herself by
striking a score of loaded boxes, which on
examination were found to contain money,
biie o-ot a sugarbag and began to stuff it
feverishly with rust-stained notes and gold
and small change. At last she took the bag
and went to the door to screech for Billy, but
on second thoughts hung it up within a skirt
av • • • ' away from his Profane eye.
Waiting with a sensation that was partly
exultation and partly terror, until the night
Jiad long closed in, she burned manuka scrub
to drive out the mosquitoes, and barricaded
the door, bhe lay awake through the long
dark, listening to the plaintive trill of the
creek , then, rising at daybreak to count her
money, she found that by the grace of God
and the perseverance of Creech, there was a
total of over £l9OO in the sugarbag.
. -that day, also, Billy did not return, and
in the afternoon she wrapped the bag in
brown paper, and, taking the two children,
moved heavily back to town. A sympathetic
purveyor of bad news announced to her that
Willy had departed by steamer the night be-
fore, and wondered tlmt she took no steps to
stop his flight.
" illiam Spritt was in Sydney when, many
months after, news was wafted to him of
his wires prosperity. On inquiry she had
round that Creech was relationless, and she
chose to explain her wealth as a legacy from
an a_unt. Billy s own affairs had not pros-
pered in the meantime, and he resolved to
return and take command of his old ship,
bo he snatched a handbag to pay his passage
and was delayed four months. Then he
shipped as a fireman, got nearly roasted on
the trip across, and arrived in a state of
yelping rebellion against the crowded fur-
naces. finally, on arrival home, he found
i s Wl^e had divorced him for desertion.
• a t,-Y,. on l ail e^Ps to drag a bloke down,” he
said bitterly, translating his case into general
aad t ,le “ wh f. n she gits any think
she turns er back on Im.
But Billy got something, after all. He
was seized on an old count of obtaining
money under false pretences—the very money
that had taken him to Sydney—and he got
another six months.
J. S. S.
